Objection of Ambiguity to Prophetic Actions
Author defines objection of ambiguity with examples and rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection of ambiguity (ijmāl) is being used against a prophetic action and formulate the correct rebuttal by providing preponderating evidence.

Core Idea
An ambiguous action without preponderance cannot prove a ruling.

This lesson introduces a key dialectical objection in the study of prophetic actions: the claim of ambiguity (daʿwā al-ijmāl). The objector argues that the Prophet's action admits multiple interpretations, making it unfit as evidence. We will analyze the structure of this objection through two classical fiqh examples and see how the proponent can rebut it. By mastering this tool, you will better understand how jurists debate the evidentiary value of Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Find where the muʿtarid asserts that the action is ambiguous (mujmal) due to multiple possibilities without preponderance.
Look for phrases like 'يحتمل وجهين' or 'دون ترجيح'.
2
Determine the two possible interpretations
Identify what the alternative interpretations are. For example, in the grave prayer case: either the woman had the funeral prayer performed before burial or not.
The ambiguity hinges on an unknown contextual factor.
3
Check for any context or clue
Determine if the hadith itself contains a contextual clue (qarīna) that removes ambiguity. The passage states there is none initially.
If a clue exists, the objection fails.
4
Examine the proponent's rebuttal
Observe how the mustadill argues that the ambiguity is resolved. In the semen example, the rebuttal is that ʿĀʾisha used the report as evidence for purity, implying the quantity was large and not excusable.
The rebuttal provides a preponderant factor (tarjīḥ).
5
Assess the strength of the rebuttal
Evaluate whether the rebuttal successfully removes the ambiguity. If it provides a convincing preponderant factor, the objection is neutralised.
The repetition of the action strengthens the rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي بدعوى الإجمال وذلك أن يكون الفعل الصادر عن النبي محتملا لوجهين، دون أن يكون هناك دليل يرجح أحدهما على الآخر، وليس في سياق الرواية قرينة تنبئ عن رجحان أحد الوجهين فيدعي المعترض إجماله ليمنع المستدل من الاحتجاج به . ذلك : أن يستدل الشافعي على الصلاة على القبر بعد أن صلى على الميت، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه صلى على قبر امرأة سوداء كانت تقم المسجد فيعترضه المالكي قائلا : لا يصح الاحتجاج بهذا الخبر لأننا لا نمنع الصلاة على كل قبر، وإنما نمنع الصلاة على قبر من صُلّي عليه قبل دفنه ونجيز الصلاة على قبر من لم يُصَلَّ عليه ولا نعلم أن هذه المرأة صُلِّي عليها قبل الدفن، فيحتمل أن يكون قد صلي عليها ويحتمل ألا يكون صلي عليها، وإذا احتمل الأمرين وجب التوقف حتى يقع البيان . ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل الشافعي في طهارة المني بأن عائشة قالت : «كنت أفرك المني من ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو يصلي عليه» (1)، فلو كان المني نجسًا لقطع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الصلاة. فيقول الحنفي معترضا : هذا الحديث مجمل، لأنه متعلق بعين، فيحتمل أنه كان قليلا ويحتمل أنه كان كثيرًا، فوجب التوقف فيه . والجواب أن يبين المستدل بالدليل أنه كان كثيرًا، لأن عائشة احتجت بهذا الخبر على طهارته، فلا يجوز أن يُحتج بما يُعفى عنه، ولأنها أخبرت عن دوام الفعل وتكراره ويبعد مع التكرار أن يكون ذلك قليلا مع الكثرة .
Third Topic: Objection to reasoning with the Prophet's action by claiming ambiguity (ijmāl). That is, when the action emanating from the Prophet is susceptible to two interpretations, without there being any evidence to preponderate one over the other, and no contextual clue in the transmission indicates preponderance, so the objector claims its ambiguity to prevent the proponent from using it as proof. For example: al-Shāfiʿī argued for prayer at the grave after praying over the deceased, based on the report that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prayed at the grave of a black woman who used to sweep the mosque. The Mālikī objected, saying: 'It is not valid to use this report as proof because we do not forbid prayer at every grave; rather we forbid prayer at the grave of one over whom the funeral prayer was performed before burial, and we permit prayer at the grave of one over whom it was not performed. We do not know whether this woman had the funeral prayer performed over her before burial. It is possible that it was performed and possible that it was not. When both possibilities exist, one must suspend judgment until clarification occurs.' Another example: al-Shāfiʿī argued for the purity of semen by citing ʿĀʾisha's statement: 'I used to rub semen from the garment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) while he was praying in it.' If semen were impure, the Prophet would have stopped the prayer. The Ḥanafī objects, saying: 'This hadith is ambiguous (mujmal) because it pertains to a specific instance. It could be that it was a small amount or a large amount. Therefore, one must suspend judgment.' The answer is that the proponent should demonstrate that it was a large amount, because ʿĀʾisha used this report as evidence for its purity; it is not permissible to use as evidence something that is excused. Also, she reported the continuity and repetition of the action, and it is unlikely that with repetition it would be a small amount.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This is a dialectical passage that defines and illustrates a specific type of objection (naqḍ) known as 'claim of ambiguity' (daʿwā al-ijmāl) against arguments based on the Prophet's actions. The author first gives a general definition: the objector alleges that the prophetic action admits two or more possible interpretations without a preponderant factor (tarjīḥ) to favor one, thus blocking its use as evidence. The author then provides two examples from Islamic jurisprudence. The first example involves prayer at graves: al-Shāfiʿī uses the Prophet's prayer at a specific grave to argue for the permissibility of praying at any grave, while the Mālikī objector argues that the action is ambiguous because it could be that the woman had already been prayed over before burial (which is permissible) or not (which is impermissible). Without knowing which, the action cannot prove the general ruling. The second example concerns the purity of semen: al-Shāfiʿī uses ʿĀʾisha's report of rubbing semen from the Prophet's garment while he prayed to argue that semen is pure, since the Prophet would not have prayed with an impurity. The Ḥanafī objects that the report is ambiguous about the quantity—small amounts are excused (maʿfūw), so it does not prove purity in general. The author then gives the rebuttal (jawāb): the proponent should argue that the quantity was large, because ʿĀʾisha used it as evidence for purity itself, which would be unnecessary if it were a small excused amount. Additionally, the action was repeated, making it unlikely that it was always a small amount. This passage demonstrates how dialectical reasoning resolves ambiguities in evidence.

Key Terms
الإجمال
المستدل
المعترض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example, the Maliki objector claims the prophetic action is ambiguous because:
A) The hadith is unreliable.
B) The action could be interpreted as either prayer at any grave or prayer only for those not prayed over before burial.
C) The woman was not a Muslim.
D) The Prophet only prayed because she was a servant.

Key Takeaway
The claim of ambiguity (ijmāl) is a valid objection only when the action admits multiple interpretations without a preponderant factor; the proponent can defeat it by supplying contextual evidence that tips the scale.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, the Hanbali argues that the Prophet's practice of eating camel meat and then praying without performing wudu proves that camel meat does not break wudu. The Shafi'i objects that the action is ambiguous because it could be that the Prophet made wudu separately or that the ruling applies only to him. Formulate a rebuttal using the principle of preponderance (tarjīḥ) by referring to contextual clues from other hadiths or general principles.
?
Identify the objector's claim of ambiguity in the following scenario: A Mālikī argues that the Prophet's action of shortening prayer while traveling proves that qasr is mandatory. A Ḥanafī objects that the action is ambiguous because it could be optional or contingent on other conditions. Is this a valid instance of ijmal? Explain your reasoning.

Defusing the Objection of Shared Proof (Mushāraka) in Dialectical Debates
Defining the objection of shared proof and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mushāraka objection and construct the standard rebuttal by specifying the conditional nature of the ruling.

Core Idea
A shared proof is neutralized only if it is unconditional; a conditional ruling resists the objection.

In the dialectical tradition (adab al-bahth), one common objection is that the same evidence used by the proponent also supports the opponent's case. This is called ‘mushāraka’ – shared participation. Without a proper response, the argument stalls. The key to rebutting is to show that the proponent's position is conditional while the opponent's is absolute, so the evidence does not equally support both. Let's see how this works with a classical fiqh example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's argument
Find the claim and the prophetic action cited. In the example: Mālikī/Hanafī says division is not obligatory because the Prophet left some Khaybar land undivided.
2
Spot the objector's move
The objector claims the same action is shared: the Prophet both left and divided, so both sides can cite it. This is the mushāraka objection.
3
Construct the rebuttal by specification
The proponent responds that his ruling is conditional (permissible if the imam sees benefit) while the opponent's is unconditional (obligation). The evidence (leaving) only contradicts unconditional obligation, not conditional permissibility. So the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي بدعوى المشاركة في الاستدلال به وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي أو الحنفي في جواز ترك قسمة الأراضي المغنومة بأن النبي ترك قسمة بعض خيبر، ولو كان ذلك واجبًا لما تركه عليه الصلاة والسلام. فيعترض عليه الشافعي أو الحنبلي، بأن يقول : هذا دليل مشترك بيننا، وكما أنه حجة لكم فهو حجة لنا أيضًا، إذ أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام إذا كان ترك قسمة البعض فقد قسم البعض الآخر، وإن جاز لكم أن تتعلقوا بما ترك، جاز لنا أن نتعلق بما قسم . والطريق في الجواب عنه أن يقول المستدل مالكيًا كان أو حنفيا : إن القسمة تجوز عندنا إذا رأى الإمام ذلك مصلحة وأداه اجتهاده إلى ذلك، وأنتم توجبون القسمة على كل حال ولو كان ذلك واجبا لما جاز له الترك .
Fourth Topic: Objection to reasoning with the Prophet's action by claiming shared participation in its use as evidence. For example, a Mālikī or Ḥanafī argues for the permissibility of not dividing conquered land, because the Prophet left some of Khaybar undivided. If dividing were obligatory, he would not have left it. The Shāfiʿī or Ḥanbalī objects, saying: 'This evidence is shared between us. Just as it is a proof for you, it is also a proof for us. Since he left some undivided, he divided others. If you are allowed to cling to what he left, we are allowed to cling to what he divided.' The way to answer it is that the proponent, whether Mālikī or Ḥanafī, should say: 'Division is permissible according to us if the imam sees a benefit (maslaha) and his independent reasoning (ijtihād) leads to it, whereas you obligate division unconditionally. If it were obligatory, it would not have been permissible for him to leave it.'
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This is a dialectical passage that defines and illustrates a specific type of objection known as 'claim of shared participation' (daʿwā al-mushāraka). The objector argues that the same prophetic action used as evidence by the proponent also supports the objector's position, thus neutralizing the evidence. The author gives an example: a Mālikī or Ḥanafī argues that the Prophet's leaving some of the land of Khaybar undivided shows that division is not obligatory (since if it were, the Prophet would not have left it). The Shāfiʿī or Ḥanbalī objector counters that the same action also shows that the Prophet divided the rest, so both parties can claim support from the same action—the proponent uses the leaving, the objector uses the dividing. The author then provides the standard rebuttal (jawāb): the proponent must clarify that his own position holds division as permissible if the imam sees a benefit (maslaha) and his ijtihād leads to it, while the objector's position makes division obligatory unconditionally. Thus, the fact that the Prophet left some undivided does not disprove the proponent's conditional permissibility, but it does contradict the objector's unconditional obligation. This shows how the proponent can defuse the objection by specifying the conditional nature of his ruling.

Key Terms
المشاركة
القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given, the Shafiʿi/Hanbali objector says the action is a shared proof because:
A) The Prophet's action is ambiguous.
B) The Prophet both left some land undivided and divided other parts.
C) The Ḥanafī/Mālikī position is weak.
D) The imam's ijtihād is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The rebuttal to a mushāraka objection lies in specifying the conditional nature of your ruling, showing that the shared evidence does not contradict your position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar debate: suppose a Ḥanafī argues that qunūt in Fajr prayer is not obligatory because the Prophet sometimes omitted it. A Shāfiʿī objects that the Prophet sometimes performed it, so it is shared proof. Formulate the Ḥanafī's rebuttal by specifying the conditional nature of his ruling (recommended, not obligatory).
?
Identify the mushāraka objection in the following: 'The Prophet married multiple wives, so it is permissible; but the Prophet also married only one wife for years, so it is obligatory.' Explain why this is an invalid mushāraka and how to rebut it.

Responding to Conflicting Narrations in Dialectical Usul al-Fiqh
Author defines the fifth type of objection: conflicting reports of Prophet's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formulate a response to a conflicting narration objection using harmonization (jama') or preferment (tarjih).

Core Idea
Conflicting narrations are not fatal; harmonize or prefer.

In dialectical debates over the Prophet's actions, opponents may cite a different narration that seems to contradict yours. This lesson teaches you two principled responses from usul al-fiqh: harmonization and preferment. The example of the Prophet's marriage to Maymunah illustrates the method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Recognize that the opponent is presenting a conflicting narration about the same prophetic action, not a direct refutation of the evidence.
2
Examine the two possible responses
If possible, harmonize the narrations (jama') by interpreting them as compatible. If not, preponderate (tarjih) your narration over the opponent's based on criteria such as chain strength or context.
The passage lists these two methods explicitly.
3
Apply to the example
In the Maymunah marriage debate, a Hanafi could try to harmonize the 'muhrim' and 'halal' reports, or argue that his narration is more reliable (e.g., more narrators, stronger memory).
The passage does not provide the detailed harmonization; the student is expected to know the general principles of jama' and tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الخامس : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي باختلاف الرواية وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنفي في جواز نكاح المحرم، بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تزوج ميمونة، وهو محرم. فيعترضه الشافعي أو الحنبلي قائلا : قد روي أنه تزوجها وهما حلالان. والجواب عنه من أحد وجهين : الأول : أن المستدل يجمع بين الروايتين إن أمكنه . والثاني : أن يرجح روايته على رواية المخالف.
The fifth demand: The objection to using the Prophet's action by conflicting narration. That is like when a Hanafi argues for the permissibility of a muhrim's marriage by saying that the Prophet married Maymunah while he was in ihram. The Shafi'i or Hanbali objects by saying: it has been narrated that he married her while both were halal. The answer to this is from two aspects: first, that the proponent harmonizes between the two narrations if possible; second, that he preponderates his narration over the opponent's narration.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type of objection against using the Prophet's action: the opponent presents a conflicting narration about the same action. The example is the marriage of the Prophet to Maymunah: the Hanafi uses the report that he was muhrim to justify marrying in ihram; the Shafi'i/Hanbali counters with a report that both were halal. The answer offers two methods: (1) harmonization (jama') between the narrations, and (2) preference (tarjih) of one's own narration over the opponent's. These are standard usul al-fiqh tools for dealing with contradictory evidence. The passage does not provide a detailed harmonization, but instructs the student that these are the valid responses.

Key Terms
اختلاف الرواية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two ways to answer an objection based on conflicting narrations according to this passage?
A) Reject both narrations and seek new evidence.
B) Harmonize the narrations if possible, or prefer one's own narration.
C) Accept the opponent's narration and concede.
D) Deny the authenticity of the opponent's narration.

Key Takeaway
When faced with conflicting narrations, an usuli has two options: combine them or show one to be stronger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the example: A Maliki argues that the Prophet performed 'Umrah after Hajj (qiran) based on a report; a Hanbali objects with a report that he performed 'Umrah separately. How would you respond using the two methods?
?
Identify a situation from hadith where two authentic reports seem to conflict on a prophetic action. Sketch a response that either harmonizes or prefers one narration.

Responding to Conflicting Narrations in Dialectic
Author defines and exemplifies objection based on conflicting narrations with Hanafi-Shafi'i disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection to a prophetic action inference is based on conflicting narrations and construct an appropriate response using either reconciliation (jam') or preponderance (tarjih).

Core Idea
Conflicting narrations do not destroy a proof; they demand a dialectical response.

In usul al-fiqh debates, one common objection is that the prophetic action cited is contradicted by another narration. This lesson examines how the original deducer can respond, either by harmonizing the two reports or by showing his narration is stronger. The passage gives two classic examples from Hanafi-Shafi'i and Maliki-Shafi'i disputations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original inference
Pinpoint the specific action of the Prophet that the deducer (e.g., Hanafi) uses to support a ruling (e.g., permissibility of marriage in ihram). Note the narrated action exactly.
2
Recognize the objection
Identify the conflicting narration presented by the opponent (e.g., Shafi'i). Understand how it appears to contradict the first report.
3
Assess reconciliation (jam')
Ask: Can the two narrations be interpreted to both apply without contradiction? For example, perhaps the marriage contract occurred when not in ihram but the consummation occurred in ihram. This is the preferred response if possible.
Reconciliation is only valid if it does not distort the clear meaning of either narration.
4
Apply preponderance (tarjih)
If reconciliation is impossible, the deducer must argue that his narration is stronger. Criteria include reliability of narrators, number of memorizers, conformity to other established principles (like wudu being based on lightness).
5
Formulate the response
The deducer now presents a structured answer: either showing how the narrations harmonize, or giving explicit reasons why his narration is preponderant. This is the jawab in the jadal exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الخامس : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي باختلاف الرواية وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنفي في جواز نكاح المحرم، بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تزوج ميمونة، وهو محرم. فيعترضه الشافعي أو الحنبلي قائلا : قد روي أنه تزوجها وهما حلالان. والجواب عنه من أحد وجهين : الأول : أن المستدل يجمع بين الروايتين إن أمكنه . والثاني : أن يرجح روايته على رواية المخالف . ومن أمثلته كذلك : أن يستدل المالكي في أن التكرار في مسح الرأس غير مسنون، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه توضأ ثلاثا ومسح رأسه مرة واحدة ، وهذا دليل على عدم سنية تكرار المسح. فيعارضه الشافعي بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم توضأ ثلاثا ومسح رأسه ثلاثا . وطريق المالكي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يبين أن التعلق بالرواية التي استدل بها أولى فيقول : هذه رواية الحفاظ من أصحاب الحديث، وأما رواية الثلاث فلم تثبت عن النبي ولا هي في الكتب الصحاح، وأيضًا فإن الرواية التي استدللنا بها يعضدها النظر، وذلك أن المسح مبني على التخفيف، والتكرار ضد التخفيف.
Fifth Topic: Objection to inference by the Prophet's action due to conflicting narration. That is like the Hanafi inferring the permissibility of marriage while in ihram by saying 'The Prophet married Maymuna while he was in ihram.' The Shafi'i or Hanbali objects saying: 'It has been narrated that he married her while both were not in ihram.' The answer to him is from one of two ways: First: that the deducer reconciles the two narrations if possible. Second: that he gives preponderance to his narration over the opponent's narration. Among its examples also: the Maliki infers that repetition in wiping the head is not sunna by what is narrated from the Prophet that he performed wudu three times and wiped his head once, and this is evidence for the non-sunnah of repeating wiping. The Shafi'i counters him by what is narrated that the Prophet performed wudu three times and wiped his head three times. The Maliki's way in answering this objection is to show that clinging to the narration he inferred with is more appropriate, saying: 'This is a narration of the memorizers (huffaz) among hadith masters. As for the narration of three, it is not confirmed from the Prophet nor is it in the authentic books. Also, the narration we inferred with is supported by reasoning, because wiping is based on alleviation, and repetition is contrary to alleviation.'
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies a specific type of objection in jadal (dialectic) when one party infers a ruling from the Prophet's action, and the opponent objects that the narration is contradicted by another narration. The author gives two examples. First, the Hanafi uses the Prophet's marriage to Maymuna while in ihram to argue its permissibility; the Shafi'i/Hanbali objects that there is a conflicting narration that the marriage occurred when both were not in ihram. The respondent has two options: reconcile the narrations if possible, or preponderate his own narration over the opponent's. Second, the Maliki uses a hadith that the Prophet wiped his head once in wudu to argue that repeating wiping is not sunna; the Shafi'i counters with a hadith that he wiped three times. The Maliki's response is to argue that his narration is from reliable memorizers and is supported by considerations such as wiping being a lightened act, whereas repetition goes against that principle. This passage is typical of usul al-fiqh dialectic, showing how conflicting textual evidence is handled.

Key Terms
اختلاف الرواية
الجمع بين الروايتين
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of objection due to conflicting narration, what is the Hanafi's evidence?
A) The Prophet married Maymuna while both were not in ihram.
B) The Prophet married Maymuna while he was in ihram.
C) The Prophet performed wudu three times and wiped his head once.
D) The Prophet performed wudu three times and wiped his head three times.

Key Takeaway
When a prophetic action is cited, an opponent may object with a conflicting narration; the deducer must either reconcile the two or argue that his narration is stronger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a scenario: A Shafi'i argues that wiping the head in wudu is to be repeated three times because the Prophet wiped three times (as per a narration). A Maliki objects using the narration that the Prophet wiped once. Using the methods from this lesson, construct the Maliki's response, either reconciling or giving preponderance.
?
In the Hanafi example, suppose reconciliation is not possible. List two criteria the Hanafi might use to give preponderance to his narration (e.g., number of narrators, memorization strength, conformity to other principles).

Responding to Abrogation Claims Against Prophetic Actions
Author defines objection by abrogation with example of sujud al-sahw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection based on abrogation in a jadal exchange and formulate a valid response by either challenging the abrogation or reconciling the conflicting texts.

Core Idea
Abrogation can invalidate evidence from the Prophet's action, but the responder can counter by refuting the abrogation or harmonizing the texts.

In this lesson, we examine a specific dialectical objection: claiming that the Prophet's action used as evidence has been abrogated by a later action. Through the example of sujud al-sahw, you will learn how a responder can defend against such an objection by either disputing the abrogation or reconciling the conflicting narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original evidence
Determine which prophetic action is being used as evidence (e.g., the Hanafi's use of al-Mughira's hadith for sujud al-sahw after salam).
2
Find the opponent's abrogation claim
Identify the later action that the opponent claims abrogates the first (e.g., Ibn Buhayna's hadith of prostration before salam, with al-Zuhri's statement that the last action was before salam).
3
Formulate a response
Choose one of two strategies: (a) challenge the abrogation by arguing the second action does not actually abrogate (e.g., it may itself be abrogated or not universal), or (b) reconcile the two actions by explaining that both are valid in different circumstances.
4
Apply to the example
The Hanafi can respond by claiming that the Ibn Buhayna hadith is itself abrogated or that it applies only when the salam is not yet said, while al-Mughira's hadith applies when the salam is said after prostration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب السادس : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي بدعوى النسخ وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنفي على أن سجود السهو في النقصان إنما يكون بعد السلام، بما روي عن المغيرة بن شعبة أنه سها فقام في الركعتين الأوليين، فسبحوا له، فمضى فلما فرغ من صلاته سجد سجدتين بعد السلام، ثم قال : هكذا صنع رسول الله . فيعترض المالكي أو الشافعي عليه بأن هذا منسوخ بما روى ابن بحينة أن النبي قام من اثنتين، فلما كان في آخر صلاته وانتظر الناس تسليمه، سجد سجدتين وهو جالس قبل السلام، ثم سلم ، وقال الزهري : كان آخر الأمرين من رسول الله السجود قبل السلام . والآخر من الفعلين ينسخ الأول منهما. وطريق المستدل في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يتكلم على الناسخ بما يسقط الاعتراض به، أو يجمع بين الحديثين إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا.
Sixth Topic: Objection to inference by the Prophet's action by claiming abrogation. That is like the Hanafi inferring that the prostration of forgetfulness for deficiency occurs after the salam, based on what was narrated from al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba that he forgot and stood up after two rak'ahs, so they glorified him, but he continued. When he finished his prayer, he prostrated two times after the salam, then said: 'Thus the Messenger of Allah did.' The Maliki or Shafi'i objects to him that this is abrogated by what Ibn Buhayna narrated: that the Prophet stood up after two, and when he was at the end of his prayer and the people waited for his taslim, he prostrated two times while sitting before the salam, then said the salam. Al-Zuhri said: 'The last of the two actions of the Messenger of Allah was the prostration before the salam.' And the latter of the two actions abrogates the earlier one. The way of the deducer in answering this objection is to discuss the abrogation in a manner that nullifies the objection, or to reconcile the two hadiths if he finds a way to do so.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents another type of objection: claiming that the Prophet's action being used as evidence has been abrogated. The example involves the ruling on prostration of forgetfulness (sujud al-sahw) for deficiency: the Hanafi argues that the prostration is after the salam based on a hadith of al-Mughira. The opponent claims that this action was abrogated by a later action of the Prophet, as reported by Ibn Buhayna, where he prostrated before the salam. The responder (the one who brought the first hadith) can either discuss the abrogation (perhaps arguing that the second hadith does not constitute abrogation or that it is itself abrogated) or reconcile the two hadiths (e.g., harmonizing them by stating that both are valid in different cases). This shows the importance of considering the chronological order of the Prophet's actions and the principle that later actions may abrogate earlier ones.

Objection via Interpretation: The Wording Aspect
Author introduces objection via interpretation of the wording describing the action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two aspects of objection via interpretation to a Prophetic action and explain how the wording can be contested in jadal.

Core Idea
The wording of a Prophetic action can be challenged as ambiguous, opening the door to alternative interpretations.

In jadal (dialectical debate) over usul al-fiqh, opponents often object to using the Prophet's actions as evidence. One powerful line of objection is through interpretation (ta'wil). This lesson introduces the first aspect: the ambiguity of the wording narrating the action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of objection
The author states there are two aspects ('min jihhatayn'). The first is the wording (al-lafdh) by which the action is narrated. The second is not provided in this passage but typically relates to the interpretation of the action itself.
In usul, the second aspect often involves context, intention, or the action's modality.
2
Focus on the first aspect: wording ambiguity
The objector argues that the wording used to describe the Prophet's action (e.g., 'kana yaf'alu') may have multiple meanings, so it cannot definitively support the claimant's conclusion. The claimant must argue that the wording is clear (zahir) or that the intended meaning is specific.
For example, 'kana yaf'alu' could mean habitual or occasional action.
3
Recognize the jadal structure
The objector (mu'tarid) challenges the evidentiary force of the wording. The claimant (mudda'i) must respond by clarifying the wording's import, often with linguistic evidence or context. This is a standard move in jadal.
This objection is not a rejection of the action itself but of its interpretation from the narration.
4
Anticipate the second aspect
Although not given, the second aspect likely concerns the action's own interpretation (e.g., whether it was specific to the Prophet or general). This sets up a deeper discussion in the broader debate.
Together, the two aspects cover both the textual and contextual challenges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب السابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي من جهة التأويل وهذا الاعتراض يتجه إلى الاستدلال بالفعل من جهتين : إحداهما : جهة اللفظ الذي حكي به الفعل.
Seventh Topic: Objection to inference by the Prophet's action from the aspect of interpretation. This objection is directed to the inference by the action from two aspects: one of them: the aspect of the wording by which the action is narrated.

This short passage begins the seventh type of objection: challenging the use of a Prophetic action by questioning its interpretation. The author states that this objection can be made from two perspectives. The first perspective is the wording by which the action is narrated (i.e., the text of the hadith may be ambiguous or open to different interpretations). The second perspective is not given in this excerpt but would presumably be the interpretation of the action itself. This passage sets up a discussion on how the actual text of the narration can be contested in jadal.

Objection by Claiming Abrogation (Naskh) in Jadal
Author defines objection to prophetic action by claiming abrogation with example from sujud al-sahw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze an objection based on naskh in a jadal exchange, and formulate a response strategy.

Core Idea
Naskh is a powerful dialectical objection that forces the responder to prove continuity.

In dialectical reasoning, one common way to challenge an opponent's use of a prophetic action is to claim that the action was later abrogated. This passage introduces the 'objection by claiming abrogation' and provides a concrete example from the debate over sujud al-sahw. You will learn to recognize this move and the strategies available to defend against it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first party's evidence (mudda'i)
The Hanafi uses the hadith of al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba to argue that sujud al-sahw for deficiency is performed after the salam. Locate this evidence in the passage.
2
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
The Maliki or Shafi'i objects by claiming that the Hanafi's hadith is abrogated by the hadith of Ibn Buhayna and al-Zuhri's statement that the last action of the Prophet was prostration before salam. The later action abrogates the earlier.
The principle: 'The later of two actions abrogates the earlier'.
3
Analyze the responder's options (jawab)
The Hanafi can either argue against the claim of abrogation (e.g., show the later action does not meet the conditions of naskh) or reconcile the two hadiths (e.g., interpret them as applying to different cases).
Reconciliation is preferred when possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب السادس : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي بدعوى النسخ وذلك مثل أن يستدل الحنفي على أن سجود السهو في النقصان إنما يكون بعد السلام، بما روي عن المغيرة بن شعبة أنه سها فقام في الركعتين الأوليين، فسبحوا له، فمضى فلما فرغ من صلاته سجد سجدتين بعد السلام، ثم قال : هكذا صنع رسول الله ﷺ فيعترض المالكي أو الشافعي عليه بأن هذا منسوخ بما روى ابن بحينة أن النبي قام من اثنتين، فلما كان في آخر صلاته وانتظر الناس تسليمه، سجد سجدتين وهو جالس قبل السلام، ثم سلم ، وقال الزهري : كان آخر الأمرين من رسول الله ﷺ السجود قبل السلام). والآخر من الفعلين ينسخ الأول منهما. وطريق المستدل في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يتكلم على الناسخ بما يسقط الاعتراض به، أو يجمع بين الحديثين إن وجد إلى ذلك سبيلا
The sixth demand: Objection to inference by the action of the Prophet by claiming abrogation. That is like when the Hanafi infers that the prostration of forgetfulness for a deficiency is only after the salam, based on what was narrated from al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba that he forgot and stood up in the first two rak'ahs, and they said 'Subhan Allah' to him, but he continued, and when he finished his prayer he prostrated two prostrations after the salam, then said: 'Thus did the Messenger of Allah ﷺ.' Then the Maliki or Shafi'i objects against him that this is abrogated by what Ibn Buhayna narrated: that the Prophet ﷺ stood up from two (rak'ahs), and when he was at the end of his prayer, and the people waited for his taslim, he prostrated two prostrations while sitting before the salam, then said the salam. And al-Zuhri said: 'The last of the two actions from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ was the prostration before the salam.' And the later of the two actions abrogates the earlier. And the way for the one inferring to answer this objection is to discuss the abrogator in a way that removes the objection, or to reconcile between the two hadiths if a way to that is found.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage defines the objection to a prophetic action by claiming abrogation (naskh). The author first states the type of objection, then gives a concrete example: the Hanafi uses the hadith of al-Mughira to argue that sujud al-sahw for a deficiency is after the salam. The Maliki or Shafi'i objects by bringing the hadith of Ibn Buhayna, which shows the Prophet prostrated before the salam, and cites al-Zuhri's statement that the last action was before the salam, thus abrogating the earlier practice. The passage then explains the responder's strategy: either argue against the claim of abrogation or attempt to reconcile the two hadiths. This is a standard dialectical move in usul al-fiqh. The example is firmly rooted in fiqh debates about the timing of sujud al-sahw.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revealed ruling or prophetic action; here specifically, the later prophetic action supersedes an earlier one. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, naskh is a standard concept; in jadal, it is used as an objection to undermine an opponent's evidence.]
سجود السهو (sujud al-sahw): Prostration performed to compensate for forgetfulness in prayer; the debate is whether it is before or after the salam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Objection by Ta'wil in the Wording of Prophetic Reports
Author explains objection to prophetic action by interpretive ambiguity in the wording of the report.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and respond to an objection based on interpretive ambiguity (ta'wil) in the wording of a prophetic report using jadal strategies.

Core Idea
In jadal, ambiguous wording opens the door to objection by ta'wil, which the responder must either block or neutralise.

One of the most common objections to inference by prophetic action is that the wording of the report is ambiguous. The opponent claims the key term could bear another meaning, thereby weakening the evidence. This lesson breaks down how such an objection works and what the responder can do to defend the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
Locate the key word in the report that the opponent claims has more than one meaning. In the example, the word 'muhrim' is the focus.
2
Determine the opponent's alternate interpretation
Understand what alternative meaning the opponent proposes and why it weakens the original inference. Here, 'muhrim' is said to mean 'inside the Haram' instead of 'in ihram'.
3
Assess whether the wording genuinely admits both meanings
Check if the alternate interpretation is lexically or contextually plausible. If not, the objection fails. If yes, ambiguity exists.
4
Choose the responder's strategy
The responder has two options: (1) argue that the wording does not support the alternate interpretation, or (2) engage with the evidence for the alternate interpretation to defend the apparent meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب السابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بفعل النبي من جهة التأويل وهذا الاعتراض يتجه إلى الاستدلال بالفعل من جهتين : إحداهما : جهة اللفظ الذي حكي به الفعل. والثانية : جهة الحال التي وقع فيها الفعل. -- 1 فأما من جهة اللفظ الذي حكي به الفعل : فمثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تزوج ميمونة وهو محرم فيعترض عليه المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي قائلا يحتمل أن يكون المراد أنه تزوج وهو في الحرم فإنه يقال لمن كان داخل الحرم؛ محرم . . وإذا احتمل اللفظ كلا المعنيين لم يصح حمله على أحدهما إلا بدليل. والطريق للحنفي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أحد أمرين : أن يسقط التأويل ويبين أن اللفظ لا يحتمله ، أو أن يتكلم على دليل التأويل ليسلم له الظاهر ويستقيم له ترجيح المعنى الذي استدل له
The seventh demand: Objection to inference by the action of the Prophet from the angle of interpretation. This objection is directed to inference by action from two aspects: one of them is the aspect of the wording by which the action was narrated; the second is the aspect of the situation in which the action occurred. -- 1. As for the aspect of the wording by which the action was narrated: its example is that the Hanafi infers by what was narrated that the Prophet ﷺ married Maymuna while he was muhrim. Then the Maliki or Shafi'i or Hanbali objects to him saying: It is possible that what is meant is that he married while in the Haram (sanctuary), because one who is inside the Haram is called muhrim. And if the wording admits both meanings, it is not valid to apply it to one of them without evidence. And the way for the Hanafi to answer this objection is one of two things: either to invalidate the interpretation and show that the wording does not admit it, or to discuss the evidence of the interpretation so that the apparent meaning is conceded to him and he succeeds in preferring the meaning for which he inferred.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage explains the objection to a prophetic action based on ta'wil (interpretive ambiguity) in the wording of the report. The seventh demand is introduced, and it has two aspects: wording and situation. The author first addresses the wording aspect. The example involves the Hanafi relying on the report that the Prophet married Maymuna while muhrim. The opponent objects that 'muhrim' could mean 'in the state of ihram' or 'inside the Haram' (since both are called muhrim). Therefore the wording is ambiguous and cannot be used as evidence without clarification. The responder has two options: either argue that the wording does not support the opponent's interpretation, or engage with the evidence for the opponent's interpretation to defend the apparent meaning. This is a classic jadal move about interpretive ambiguity.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil): Interpretation that takes a word away from its apparent meaning to a non-apparent one; here used as an objection to weaken the evidence. [Note: In tafsir, ta'wil often means a deeper interpretation; in jadal, it is a tool of objection.]
مُحْرِم (muhrim): Can mean either a person in the state of ihram (pilgrimage consecration) or a person who is within the Haram (sanctuary) of Mecca.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The Prophet only married her after leaving ihram.
D) The marriage was abrogated by later verses.

Key Takeaway
Objection by ta'wil in wording exploits lexical ambiguity to challenge evidence; the responder must either refute the alternate interpretation's validity or engage with its evidence to defend the apparent meaning.

Objections from Circumstance of the Action in Jadal
Two subtypes of objection from circumstance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two subtypes of objection based on the circumstance of an action and formulate a response to each in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
In jadal, attack the wording or situation of an action, not just the action itself.

The second type of objection (i'tirad) targets the circumstance (jihat al-hal) in which an action occurred. The author distinguishes two ways an opponent can challenge a proof: by questioning the wording of the report or by citing a special physical situation. We'll break down both using real juristic debates between Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i scholars.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two subtypes
Read the passage and identify the two categories: objection from the wording (al-lafz) and objection from the actual circumstance (al-hal). Note how each targets a different aspect of the narrated action.
The wording-based objection exploits lexical ambiguity; the circumstance-based objection invokes a special condition (e.g., illness).
2
Analyze the wording-based objection
In the first example, the Hanafi uses the hadith 'the Prophet married Maymunah while muhrim.' The Maliki objects that 'muhrim' could mean 'being in the Haram.' Identify the two possible responses the Hanafi can give: (a) show the wording cannot bear that meaning, or (b) discuss the evidence for the interpretation.
The objection succeeds if the wording is genuinely ambiguous; the respondent must break the ambiguity.
3
Analyze the circumstance-based objection
In the second example, the Shafi'i uses the hadith of wiping the forelock and turban. The Maliki objects that this was due to sickness, not a general rule. Note how the Maliki then provides his own evidence for the opposite ruling (wiping the whole head).
The objector must not only raise the special circumstance but also defend the default ruling with evidence.
4
Formulate a response strategy
Choose one subtype and write a short jadal exchange. For wording: propose a hadith with an ambiguous term and craft the objection and response. For circumstance: propose an action with a possible special situation and show how the respondent can defend its generality.
Use only Islamic examples from fiqh or usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانية : جهة الحال التي وقع فيها الفعل.
(1)
فأما من جهة اللفظ الذي حكي به الفعل :
فمثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تزوج ميمونة وهو محرم فيعترض عليه المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي قائلا يحتمل أن يكون المراد أنه تزوج وهو في الحرم فإنه يقال لمن كان داخل الحرم؛ محرم . . وإذا احتمل اللفظ كلا المعنيين لم يصح حمله على أحدهما إلا بدليل. والطريق للحنفي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أحد أمرين : أن يسقط التأويل ويبين أن اللفظ لا يحتمله ، أو أن يتكلم على دليل التأويل ليسلم له الظاهر ويستقيم له ترجيح المعنى الذي استدل له (2)
2 - وأما من جهة الحال التي وقع فيها الفعل :
فمثل أن يستدل الشافعي في جواز مسح بعض الرأس بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه
(3)
توضأ فمسح ناصيته وعمامته فيعارضه المالكي قائلا يجوز أن يكون فعل ذلك لمرض ألم به، ثم يثبت : : بالدليل وجوب مسح جميع الرأس
(4)
And the second [type]: the circumstance of the situation in which the action occurred.
(1)
As for [objection] from the aspect of the wording by which the action is narrated:
Its example: that the Hanafi deduces by what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) married Maymunah while he was in a state of ihram. Then the Maliki, or the Shafi'i, or the Hanbali objects, saying: It is possible that the meaning is that he married while in the Haram, for one who is inside the Haram is called muhrim. When the wording possibly carries both meanings, it is not valid to restrict it to one of them without evidence. The way for the Hanafi to answer this objection is one of two things: either to invalidate the interpretation and show that the wording does not carry it, or to discuss the evidence of the interpretation so that the apparent meaning is conceded to him and the preference of the meaning for which he argued becomes sound (2)
2 - And as for [objection] from the aspect of the circumstance in which the action occurred:
Its example: that the Shafi'i deduces the permissibility of wiping part of the head by what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed ablution and wiped his forelock and turban. Then the Maliki objects, saying: It is possible that he did that due to a sickness that befell him. Then he establishes - by evidence - the obligation of wiping the entire head (4)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of objection (i'tirad) in jadal based on the circumstance of the action. The author divides it into two sub-types: objection based on the wording of the narration, and objection based on the actual state in which the action occurred. For the first sub-type, he gives the example of the Hanafi using the hadith of the Prophet marrying Maymunah while in ihram. The opponent objects that 'muhrim' could mean being in the Haram, not necessarily in the state of ihram. The Hanafi's response is either to show the wording cannot bear that interpretation or to argue over the evidence for the interpretation. For the second sub-type, he gives the example of the Shafi'i using the hadith of the Prophet wiping his forelock and turban during ablution. The Maliki objects that this may have been due to illness. The Maliki then proceeds to establish the obligation of wiping the entire head with evidence. The passage demonstrates how dialectical objections focus on the ambiguity of the narrated action's wording or circumstances.

الاعتراض
جهة الحال
محرم

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the circumstance of an action can be challenged through ambiguous wording or a special situation; the respondent must either refute the alternative interpretation or provide counter-evidence.

The Two Methods of Answering Objections: Jam' and Tarjih
Author explains two ways to respond to opposing hadith: combining or preferring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two jadal methods—jam' (combining) and tarjih (preference)—to resolve apparent conflicts between hadith narrations.

Core Idea
In jadal, you don't just choose one side—you either reconcile (jam') or prefer (tarjih) conflicting evidence.

In this lesson, you will see a concrete dialectical example: two hadith reports about the Prophet's hand position in the takbir—one says shoulders, the other ears. The Hanafi and Maliki scholars present opposing arguments. The author explains the two classic responses: jam' (combining) and tarjih (preference). By the end, you'll know how to use these tools when faced with conflicting texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting evidence
Find the two opposing hadith in the passage: one reports the Prophet raising hands to shoulders (Abu Humayd), the other to ears (Wa'il ibn Hujr). This is the mu'araḍa (opposition).
The conflict is about the exact hand position during takbir.
2
Understand the first method (jam')
Jam' reconciles the two by interpreting each as referring to a different part: the first hadith is about the palms reaching the shoulders, the second about the fingertips reaching the ears. Both can be applied without contradiction.
Jam' requires a plausible interpretation that preserves both texts.
3
Understand the second method (tarjih)
Tarjih prefers one hadith over the other based on strength: the Maliki's hadith is narrated by ten Companions, making it more reliable. The Hanafi's hadith from Wa'il is weaker in terms of number of narrators.
Tarjih uses criteria like chain strength, text clarity, or practice.
4
Apply to the dispute
See that both methods provide a valid response to the objection. The Maliki can either combine both reports (jam') or assert the superiority of his own evidence (tarjih).
The choice depends on which method is more appropriate for the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنكبين في تكبيرة الإحرام بالصلاة بما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رفع يديه حذو منكبيه فيعارضه الحنفي بما روى وائل بن حجر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رفع يديه حيال أذنيه. والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض، إما أن يكون بالجمع بين الدليلين، فيحمل ما رواه أبو حميد على الكفين، وما رواه وائل على أطراف الأصابع أنها بلغت الأذنين، ليكون ذلك جمعا بين الخبرين واستعمالا للدليلين.. أو يكون الجواب بالترجيح، بأن يقول : حديثنا أولى فإنه رواه مع أبي حميد عشرة من أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ووافقوه عليه، فهو بالصواب أولى.
The example of the shoulders in the takbir of prayer by what was narrated that the Prophet ﷺ raised his hands to the level of his shoulders. Then the Hanafi opposes him with what Wā'il ibn Ḥujr narrated that the Prophet ﷺ raised his hands opposite his ears. The answer to this objection is either by combining the two evidences, interpreting what Abū Ḥumayd narrated as referring to the palms, and what Wā'il narrated as referring to the tips of the fingers reaching the ears, so that would be a combination of the two reports and a utilization of both evidences; or the answer is by preference, saying: our hadith is more worthy because it was narrated by Abū Ḥumayd along with ten of the Companions of the Prophet ﷺ and they agreed with him, so it is more deserving of correctness.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical example from jadal. The author presents a practical case of mu'āraḍa bi al-fi'l (opposition by action) regarding the raising of hands during the opening takbir. The Maliki argues using a hadith that the Prophet raised his hands to his shoulders. The Hanafi counters with another hadith that he raised them to his ears. The author then explains the two standard responses in jadal: (1) al-jam' (harmonization): the two narrations are reconciled by interpreting the first as referring to the palms and the second to the fingertips, thus both are valid and non-contradictory. (2) al-tarjīḥ (preference): the Hanafi's hadith is deemed weaker because it is narrated by a single companion, while the Maliki's is supported by ten, so it is preferred. This illustrates how dialecticians resolve apparent conflicts in transmitted evidence.

Key Terms

الترجيح

C) Citing a third hadith to break the tie.
D) Abandoning both hadiths and using qiyas.

Key Takeaway
Jam' and tarjih are essential jadal methods for resolving apparent contradictions in transmitted evidence.

Responding to a Textual Objection by Denying a Linguistic Premise
Author shows how to respond by denying that the ba' in the verse implies partiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the jadal strategy of man‘ (denial) to counter a mu‘āraḍa bi al-qawl objection by identifying and challenging the opponent’s linguistic premise.

Core Idea
In jadal, denying the opponent's linguistic premise can dissolve an apparent contradiction between textual evidence.

In this lesson, we examine a classic jadal scenario where a Maliki jurist uses a prophetic hadith to argue that wiping the entire head in wudu is obligatory. The opponent objects by citing the Quranic verse "wa imsaḥū bi ru'ūsikum," claiming the ba' implies partiality (tab'īḍ). The Maliki's response is not to abandon his evidence but to deny the linguistic premise—showing that the ba' does not necessarily mean partiality. This is the technique of man‘ (denial) in mu‘āraḍa bi al-qawl (opposition by textual statement). Learning this dialectical move is essential for defending positions in Islamic legal and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Find the specific linguistic claim made by the opponent: that the ba' in the verse implies partiality (tab'īḍ).
2
Locate the premise being denied
See how the Maliki's denial (man‘) targets this linguistic assumption rather than the verse itself or its authenticity.
3
Understand the burden of proof shift
By denying, the Maliki forces the opponent to prove that ba' always means partiality in this context, preserving his own evidence for complete wiping.
4
Apply the same technique to a new example
Practice constructing a man‘ response in a similar jadal exchange, e.g., raising hands in prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة بالقول : فمثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن استيعاب الرأس واجب في الوضوء بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه مسح رأسه بيديه فأقبل مسح بهما وأدبر، بدأ بمقدم رأسه حتى ذهب بهما إلى قفاه ثم ردهما إلى الموضع الذي منه بدأ. فيعارضه المخالف بقوله تعالى : وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ . والباء تقتضي التبعيض في كلام العرب. والطريق في الجواب عن ذلك أن يمنع المالكي، وهو المستدل، ما ذكره المعترض ليسلم له دليله فيبين أن الباء لا تقتضي التبعيض في كلام العرب بحسب مقتضى الكلام، وإذا ثبت ذلك لم يكن بين القول والفعل وإنما هي تعارض.
As for opposition by statement: for example, the Maliki argues that complete wiping of the head is obligatory in wudu based on what was narrated from the Prophet ﷺ that he wiped his head with his hands, moving forward and backward, starting from the front of his head until he reached the back of his neck, then returning to where he started. Then the opponent opposes him with the verse: '...and wipe your heads...' and the ba' implies partiality in the speech of the Arabs. The way to answer this is that the Maliki, who is the arguer, denies what the objector mentioned in order to preserve his evidence, and shows that the ba' does not imply partiality in Arabic according to the requirement of discourse, and if that is established, there is no contradiction between the statement and the action; it is merely opposition.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage presents a case of mu'āraḍa bi al-qawl (opposition by textual statement) in jadal. The Maliki uses the Prophet's action to argue that the entire head must be wiped. The opponent counters with the Quranic verse '...wa amsaḥū bi ru'ūsikum...', claiming that the letter ba' indicates partiality (tab'īḍ), meaning only part of the head needs wiping. The Maliki's response is man' (denial): he denies the opponent's linguistic claim that the ba' in this context implies partiality. He demonstrates that the ba' does not necessarily mean partitive in all contexts; it can be for other purposes like the 'ba' of accompaniment' or clarification. Once this linguistic premise is debunked, there is no real conflict between the verse and the action. This illustrates the importance of challenging presuppositions about language in dialectical debate.

Key Terms

المعارضة بالقول

Key Takeaway
Denying a linguistic premise (man‘) is a powerful jadal tool that preserves one's own evidence by putting the burden of proof on the objector.

Mu'arada bi 'Illat al-Qiyas: The Probable Action Example
Author explains opposition by analogy to a probable action and how to answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a mu'arada bi 'illat al-qiyas in a jadal exchange and evaluate the strength of the response.

Core Idea
Opposition by counter-analogy challenges the interpretation of a prophetic action, not its authenticity.

In jadal (dialectical debate), one common type of opposition is mu'arada bi 'illat al-qiyas, where a disputant offers a counter-analogy to challenge the opponent's inference. Here, the author illustrates this with the case of a probable prophetic action (al-fi'l al-muhtamil). The Maliki argues that the Prophet's single wipe of the head indicates no repetition, but the Shafi'i counters by interpreting the action as permissibility and using qiyas to the hands. Understanding how to answer such opposition is crucial for usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original evidence and claim
Locate the Maliki's evidence (the hadith of the Prophet wiping his head once) and his claim that repetition is not prescribed for the head.
The claim is that the action directly indicates the ruling (no repetition).
2
Recognize the nature of the probable action
Why is this action considered 'probable' (muhtamil)? Because it can be interpreted in multiple ways: as a ruling of permissibility or as the actual prescribed ruling. This ambiguity opens the door for opposition.
The action's ambiguity is the basis for the Shafi'i's objection.
3
Analyze the opposition (mu'arada) by the Shafi'i
The Shafi'i gives a different interpretation (the action indicates permissibility, not obligation) and provides a counter-analogy (qiyas) to the hands: the head is a limb of purification like the hands, and repetition is prescribed for the hands, so repetition should also be allowed for the head.
The Shafi'i uses the same evidence but reinterprets it and strengthens his position with an analogy.
4
Evaluate the response (jawab) by the Maliki
The Maliki argues that the Prophet performed wudu in the most complete manner; his deviation to one wipe indicates the actual ruling (no repetition). The Maliki thus denies that the action was for permissibility, breaking the foundation of the Shafi'i's argument.
Assess whether this response adequately addresses the Shafi'i's qiyas. Does it invalidate the analogy or only the interpretation?
5
Determine the rules of jadal in this exchange
According to adab al-bahth, who has the burden of proof after the opposition? The Maliki must provide a reason why the action is not for permissibility. He does so by appealing to the 'most complete manner' principle. This shifts the burden back to the Shafi'i to show why the analogy still holds.
In jadal, the responder must directly address the objector's point without changing the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك أن الفعل لا يخلو أن يكون محتملا أو غير محتمل. فأما الفعل المحتمل، فمثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن مسح الرأس في الوضوء لم يشرع فيه التكرار، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه توضأ ثلاثا ثلاثا ومسح رأسه مرة واحدة. فعارضه الشافعي قائلا هذا يحتمل الجواز، ويحتمل أن يكون ذلك حكمه، فيحمل على الجواز بدليل أنه عضو من أعضاء الطهارة، فشرع فيه التكرار كاليدين. والطريق في الجواب عنه أن يبين المالكي أنه لا يكون ذلك للجواز، لأنه قد شرع في الطهارة على أكمل وجوهها، ولما عدل في الرأس إلى المرة الواحدة علمنا أن المراد بذلك بيان حكمه
And that is because an action cannot be but probable or non-probable. As for the probable action, for example, the Maliki argues that repeating the wiping of the head in wudu is not prescribed, based on the narration that the Prophet ﷺ performed wudu three times each and wiped his head once. The Shafi'i opposed him, saying: this is open to interpretation as permissibility, and it may be that this is its ruling; thus it is interpreted as permissibility on the evidence that it is a limb of purification, and repetition is prescribed for it like the two hands. The way to answer this is for the Maliki to show that it is not for permissibility, because he performed the purification in its most complete manner, and when he deviated to a single wipe for the head, we know that what is intended is to clarify its ruling.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage describes the first type of mu'āraḍa bi 'illat al-qiyas, which deals with a 'probable action' (al-fi'l al-muhtamil). The author first divides actions into probable and non-probable. In the example, the Maliki argues that the Prophet's wiping his head only once during wudu proves that repetition is not prescribed for the head. The Shafi'i counters by interpreting the action as merely indicating permissibility, not obligation, and uses qiyas: since the head is a limb of purification like the hands, and repetition is prescribed for the hands (as the Prophet washed them three times), then repetition should also be allowed for the head. The Maliki then refutes this by arguing that the Prophet's action was not for permissibility because he performed wudu in the most complete manner; by deliberately doing only one wipe for the head, it indicates the actual ruling (i.e., no repetition). This demonstrates the dialectical process of challenging the interpretation of a prophetic action.

Key Terms
الفعل المحتمل
المعارضة بعلة قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He denies the authenticity of the hadith.
B) He argues the action could be permissibility and uses qiyas to the hands for repetition.
C) He claims the action was abrogated.
D) He says the hadith is ambiguous and cannot be used.

Key Takeaway
The mu'arada bi 'illat al-qiyas uses a counter-analogy to challenge the interpretation of a prophetic action; the response must show why the action's interpretation is definitive.

Mu'arada bi-'Illat al-Qiyas: Opposing by the Cause in Dialectics
Author explains opposition by analogical cause with fiqh examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the two types of mu'arada bi-'illat al-qiyas (opposition by analogical cause) in fiqh debates.

Core Idea
Opposing an analogy by interpreting the prophetic act as probable or non-probable.

In jadal (dialectics), one powerful method of opposition is to challenge the analogical cause itself by reinterpreting the evidence. The author divides this into two types: when the prophetic action is probable (muhtamil) and when it is non-probable (ghayr muhtamil). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the strength of objections and responses in Islamic legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dialectical move
The opponent (mu'arid) attacks the analogical cause ('illat al-qiyas) by arguing that the prophetic action used as evidence is open to another interpretation.
2
Identify whether the act is probable or non-probable
Determine if the action can bear multiple interpretations (muhtamil) or is decisive (ghayr muhtamil). This classification affects how the objection is answered.
3
Analyze the probable action example
In the Maliki-Shafi'i debate on wiping the head, note how the Shafi'i claims the act is probable (could be for permissibility) and the Maliki counters by showing that the act was done in a context of completing purification, so it must indicate the ruling.
4
Analyze the non-probable action example
In the Maliki-Hanafi debate on blood not nullifying wudu, the Hanafi uses analogy to urine, but the Maliki responds that a clear text (nas la yahtamil al-ta'wil) cannot be overridden by analogy.
5
Evaluate the strength of each response
For probable acts, the respondent must argue that the context clarifies the act's intended meaning. For non-probable acts, the respondent invokes the supremacy of unambiguous texts over analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - المعارضة بعلة قياس:
وذلك أن الفعل لا يخلو أن يكون محتملا أو غير محتمل. ب - 1 - فأما الفعل المحتمل، فمثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن مسح الرأس في الوضوء لم يشرع فيه التكرار، بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه توضأ ثلاثا ثلاثا ومسح رأسه مرة واحدة. فيعارضه الشافعي قائلا هذا يحتمل الجواز، ويحتمل أن يكون ذلك حكمه، فيحمل على الجواز بدليل أنه عضو من أعضاء الطهارة، فشرع فيه التكرار كاليدين. والطريق في الجواب عنه أن يبين المالكي أنه لا يكون ذلك للجواز، لأنه قد شرع في الطهارة على أكمل وجوهها، ولما عدل في الرأس إلى المرة الواحدة علمنا أن المراد بذلك بيان حكمه. ب - 2 - وأما الفعل غير المحتمل، فمثاله أن يستدل المالكي في الخارج من غير السبيلين أنه لا ينقض الوضوء بما روى جابر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان في غزوة ذات الرقاع فرمي رجل بسهم فنزفه الدم فركع وسجد ومضى صلاته. وقال ابن عمر والحسن فيمن يحتجم: ليس عليه إلا غسل محاجمه. فيعارض الحنفي هذا الاستدلال بأن الدم نجاسة خارجة من البدن، فأوجب الوضوء كالبول. وجواب المالكي عن هذا الاعتراض أن القياس لا يعارض النص الذي لا يحتمل التأويل، فيسقط الاعتراض.
B - Opposition by the cause of analogy: That is because the action is either probable or non-probable. B-1 - As for the probable action, example: the Maliki argues that wiping the head in ablution was not prescribed to be repeated, based on what is narrated from the Prophet that he performed ablution three times three times and wiped his head once. The Shafi'i opposes him saying: This admits the possibility of permissibility, and it admits the possibility that this is its ruling, so it is interpreted as permissibility based on evidence that it is a limb of purification, so repetition is prescribed for it like the hands. The method of answering this is that the Maliki shows that this is not for permissibility, because he performed purification in its most complete form, and when he deviated in the head to a single time, we know that the intention was to show its ruling. B-2 - As for the non-probable action, example: the Maliki argues regarding what exits from other than the two passages that it does not nullify ablution, based on Jabir's report that the Prophet was in the Ghazwa of Dhat al-Riqa', and a man was shot with an arrow and bled profusely, yet he bowed and prostrated and continued his prayer. And Ibn 'Umar and al-Hasan said regarding one who cups: nothing is required of him except washing the cupping spots. The Hanafi opposes this argument by saying that blood is impurity exiting the body, so it necessitates ablution like urine. The Maliki's answer to this objection is that analogy does not oppose a text that does not admit interpretation, so the objection falls.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical strategy of 'mu'arada bi-'illat al-qiyas' (opposition by the cause of analogy). The author first divides the opposing party's evidence (a prophetic action) into two kinds: probable (muhtamil) and non-probable (ghayr muhtamil). For the probable action, the opponent challenges the interpretation: the Shafi'i argues that the Prophet's wiping once could be for permissibility (indicating it's allowed to repeat) rather than being the ruling itself. The Maliki's response is to show that since the Prophet performed ablution in the most complete manner (three times for other parts), his wiping once must be to indicate the ruling (not just permissibility). For the non-probable action, the example is blood from cupping or a wound not nullifying wudu. The Hanafi opposes by analogizing blood to urine as impurity exiting the body. The Maliki's response is that analogy cannot override a clear textual evidence (the hadith and companions' statements) that does not admit interpretation. Thus, the objection falls.

Key Terms
معارضة
علة القياس
فعل محتمل
فعل غير محتمل
نص لا يحتمل التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the probable action (مسح الرأس), what is the Shafi'i's objection?
A) The Prophet's wiping once is definitive and cannot be challenged.
B) The action admits the possibility of permissibility, so it is interpreted as recommended not obligatory.
C) The action is not authentic.
D) The action is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The effectiveness of opposing by the analogical cause depends on whether the prophetic action is probable or non-probable, and the response must address that classification.

Classification of Objections against Qiyas in Jadal
Author classifies objections against qiyas into three types in the context of jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify objections against qiyas (analogical reasoning) into the three main categories identified by usul al-fiqh scholars: objections against the inference, against the pillars, and against the cause (subdivided into effectiveness and consistency).

Core Idea
Objections to qiyas are classified by what they target: the inference, the pillars, or the cause.

In Islamic legal theory, qiyas (analogical reasoning) is a central method for deriving rulings when no explicit text is available. Because it is so widely used, it also attracts many objections from opposing scholars. In this lesson, you will learn how usul al-fiqh scholars systematically categorize these objections, which will help you analyze and respond to them in debate. The passage below from a work on jadal (dialectic) lays out this classification, distinguishing between challenges to the inference itself, to the pillars of analogy, and to the cause from two angles: effectiveness and consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories of objections
The author lists three types: objections against the inference, against the entire set of pillars (asl, far', 'illa, hukm), and against the cause. Note that objections against the cause are further divided into two subtypes.
The Arabic uses 'jihāt al-ta'thīr' and 'jihāt al-ṭard' for effectiveness and consistency.
2
Understand the 'inference' category
Objections against the inference itself challenge the validity of using qiyas in general or in a specific case. This could involve arguing that qiyas is not applicable because of a text (nas) or that the analogical reasoning is flawed.
3
Understand the 'pillars' category
These objections target one or more of the four pillars of qiyas: the original case (asl), the derived case (far'), the cause ('illa), or the ruling (hukm). For example, an opponent might deny that the 'illa exists in the derived case.
The phrase 'jumlat arkān al-qiyās' means the whole set of pillars.
4
Understand the 'cause' categories
Objections against the cause are split into two: (a) effectiveness (ta'thir) – whether the cause actually produces the ruling; (b) consistency (tard) – whether the cause applies uniformly in all relevant cases. An objection from effectiveness might claim the cause does not generate the ruling, while an objection from consistency might show a counterexample where the cause exists but the ruling does not apply.
These two angles are fundamental in usul al-fiqh debates.
5
Apply the classification to a sample objection
Take a real objection: 'The prohibition of wine is based on intoxication. But nabidh (date wine) is not like wine because its intoxication is not settled.' Does this objection target the inference, pillars, or cause? It targets the cause's effectiveness: does intoxication truly cause prohibition in nabidh?
This shows how the classification helps analyze objections quickly.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توطئة :
الفصل الثالث
الجدل في الاستدلال بالقياس
شاع الاستدلال بالقياس حتى صار أكثر الأدلة استعمالا، وخاصة عند عدم وجود نص من الكتاب أو السنة وما ذلك إلا لغناه وثرائه وتنوع صوره وكثرة تطبيقاته، لذلك لم يكن غريبا أن يكون أبرز الأدلة التي يدور حولها الجدل، وأكثرها إثارة للاعتراضات بين الأصوليين.
وإذا نحن استقرينا صور الاعتراضات الواردة على القياس في مؤلفات علماء أصول الفقه، نجدها :
- إما اعتراضات على الاستدلال به من جهة
- أو اعتراضات على جملة أركان القياس .
- أو اعتراضات على العلة من جهة التأثير .
أو اعتراضات عليها من جهة الطرد.
ونتناول كل نوع من هذه الأنواع في مبحث مستقل، كل مبحث تنضوي تحته مجموعة من المطالب بحسب صور الاعتراضات المتعلقة بكل نوع .
Preamble: The Third Chapter: Dialectic in Reasoning by Analogy. Reasoning by analogy became widespread until it became the most used evidence, especially when there is no text from the Book or the Sunnah. This is only because of its richness, abundance, variety of forms, and multiplicity of applications. Therefore, it was not surprising that it is the most prominent evidence around which dialectic revolves, and the most provoking of objections among the legal theorists. And if we enumerate the forms of objections occurring against analogy in the works of the scholars of legal theory, we find them: either objections against the inference from it in terms of [its validity], or objections against the entire set of pillars of analogy, or objections against the cause from the perspective of effectiveness, or objections against it from the perspective of consistency. And we will treat each of these types in an independent section, each section encompassing a set of demands according to the forms of objections related to each type.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a classification of objections against qiyas (analogical reasoning) in the context of jadal (dialectic). The author begins by noting the central importance of qiyas in Islamic legal reasoning: it is the most frequently used evidence when no direct text from the Qur'an or Sunnah is available. Due to its richness and variety, it naturally becomes a focal point for debate and objection among usuliyyun (legal theorists). The author then enumerates the forms of objections found in the works of usul al-fiqh. The objections are divided into three main categories: first, objections against the inference itself (al-istidlal bihi), meaning challenges to the validity of using qiyas in general or in a specific case; second, objections against the pillars of qiyas (arkan al-qiyas), which include the original case, the derived case, the cause ('illa), and the ruling; and third, objections against the cause ('illa) from two perspectives: from the perspective of effectiveness (ta'thir) and from the perspective of consistency (tard). The author then announces that each type will be treated in a separate section, with sub-sections according to the specific forms of objections. This passage sets up the structure of the subsequent discussion.

Dialectical Exchange on Qiyas for Usul
Author presents objection to qiyas for usul and the response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a jadal exchange (objection and response) and evaluate whether a response successfully defends the use of qiyas for qat'i matters.

Core Idea
The certainty of the 'illa determines whether qiyas yields certainty or speculation.

In Islamic legal theory, a common objection is that qiyas (analogical reasoning) only produces speculation (zann), so it cannot be used to establish foundational principles (usul) that require certainty (qat'). This passage presents a classic jadal exchange: the objector claims that qiyas is inappropriate for qat'i matters, and the respondent (the Malikis or Shafi'is) counters by distinguishing between certain and speculative 'illa. The debate illustrates how the permissibility of qiyas hinges on the epistemological status of the 'illa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant and objector
Determine who is asserting the use of qiyas (the claimant) and who is objecting (the mu'tarid). In both examples, the claimant is a Maliki or Shafi'i, and the objector is a Hanafi, Maliki, or Hanbali who rejects qiyas for usul.
Notice that the objector does not necessarily reject qiyas entirely, but only its use for qat'i matters.
2
Extract the objection (mu'tarid)
The objector says: 'This is establishing usul by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because the way to establish this principle is definitive proof, and qiyas only yields speculation.' Identify the precise logical reason: qiyas is zanni, usul require qat'.
The objection assumes that qiyas is always zanni, which the respondent will challenge.
3
Analyze the response (jawab)
The respondent argues that qiyas is not inherently speculative; it depends on the 'illa. If the 'illa is certain (mawtuq biha), then qiyas can produce certainty. Find the key phrase: 'إذا كانت علة الأصل مقطوعا بها'.
This response shifts the focus from qiyas itself to the certainty level of the 'illa.
4
Assess the strength of the response
The response is valid only if the 'illa is indeed certain. The respondent provides examples where the 'illa is consensus or widespread practice, which are considered qat'i. Evaluate whether this adequately addresses the objection or if the objector could still argue that the 'illa itself is not certain.
In jadal, the respondent must show that the 'illa meets the required epistemic standard.
