⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو ما يعني أن الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات الأصول سيثير الاعتراض عليه ممن يقولون بعدم جوازه، وذلك مثل استدلال المالكية على أن إجماع التابعين حجة، لأنه إجماع من أهل عصر من أعصار المسلمين على حكم الحادثة. فيخالفهم من يقول : هذا من إثبات الأصول بالقياس، وذلك غير جائز، لأن طريق إثبات هذا الأصل الدليل القطعي، وطريق القياس لا يفيد سوى الظن، فلا يجوز أن يثبت به ما طريقه العلم والقطع. وجواب المالكية : أن ذلك جائز، إذا كانت علة الأصل مقطوعا بها، وإنما يكون القياس طريقا إلى الظن إذا كانت علته مظنونة، فأما إذا كانت علته معلومة، ودل الدليل على صحتها على وجه يوجب العلم، جاز أن يثبت به ما طريقه العلم ومثاله أيضًا أن يستدل شافعي على إثبات كون البسملة من الفاتحة وكل سورة، بأنها مكتوبة في المصاحف بلا نكير متلوة في المحاريب بغير تغيير، فهي كسائر الآي من السور. فيعارضه حنفي أو مالكي أو حنبلي بأنك احتججت بالقياس في غير موضعه، لأنه دليل ظني، وقد استدللت به في محل لا يقبل فيه إلا الدليل القطعي فيجيب الشافعي بأن يقول : إنني غير مستدل بقياس، لكني مستدل بدليل قطعي، وهو الإجماع، وقال بعضهم: إني مستدل بعلة توجب العلم، وهي إجماعهم على كتابتها في المصاحف، وتلاوتهم لها في المحاريب، فإذا كانت علة توجب العلم ثبت بها ما طريقه العلم
And that means that using qiyas in establishing the foundational principles will give rise to objection from those who say it is not permissible, such as the Malikis' argument that the consensus of the Followers is a proof, because it is a consensus of the people of an era of the Muslim eras concerning the ruling on an incident. Then they [the Malikis] are opposed by one who says: This is establishing foundational principles by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because the way to establish this principle is definitive proof, and the way of qiyas only yields speculation, so it is not permissible to establish by it that whose way is knowledge and certainty. And the response of the Malikis: That is permissible if the cause of the principle is certain; qiyas only becomes a way to speculation if its cause is speculative; but if its cause is known and the proof indicates its correctness in a way that necessitates knowledge, then it is permissible to establish by it that whose way is knowledge. Another example: that a Shafi'i argues to prove that the Basmala is part of al-Fatiha and every sura, because it is written in the mushafs without objection and recited in the mihrabs without alteration, so it is like the other verses of the suras. Then a Hanafi or Maliki or Hanbali opposes him saying: You have argued by qiyas in an inappropriate place, because it is a speculative proof, and you have used it in a situation that only accepts definitive proof. Then the Shafi'i responds by saying: I am not arguing by qiyas, but I am arguing by a definitive proof, which is consensus. And some said: I am arguing by a cause that necessitates knowledge, which is their consensus on writing it in the mushafs and reciting it in the mihrabs. So if it is a cause that necessitates knowledge, then that which requires knowledge is established by it.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange discussing the objection that using qiyas (analogical reasoning) to establish foundational principles (usul) is invalid because qiyas only yields speculative knowledge (zann) while these principles require certain knowledge (qat'). The author presents two examples: first, the Malikis' argument that the consensus of the Followers is a proof by analogy with other consensuses, which is objected to on grounds that it is using qiyas for a qat'i matter. The Malikis respond that if the underlying cause ('illa) is certain, then qiyas can produce certainty. Second, the Shafi'i argument that the Basmala is part of the sura by analogy with other verses, opposed by a Hanafi/Maliki/Hanbali who says such qiyas is inappropriate. The Shafi'i responds that he is not using qiyas but a definitive proof: consensus or a cause that necessitates knowledge. This illustrates that the permissibility of qiyas depends on the certainty of the 'illa.

القطع

Mapping the Dispute: Which Names Can Qiyas Apply To?
Author presents the dispute and defines the point of contention with examples of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific scope of the dispute over using qiyas to establish names and languages, and distinguish it from non-controversial categories.

Core Idea
The dispute over qiyas for names only applies to attribute-based names, not proper nouns or grammatical rules.

In this passage, the author outlines a key disagreement among usul scholars: whether analogical reasoning (qiyas) can be used to extend names and linguistic terms. Muhammad Hasan Hitu clarifies that the disagreement is limited to names that are based on attributes, such as 'khamr' (wine). This lesson will help you map the boundaries of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of the dispute
Find which schools permit and deny using qiyas for names and languages. The passage mentions some Shafi'is (al-Shirazi, al-Razi, al-Baydawi) allow it, while Hanafis and many others (al-Baqillani, al-Juwayni, al-Ghazali, most Shafi'is) deny it.
2
Find the definition of the disputed category
Look for Hitu's definition: 'names placed for specific meanings that revolve with attributes present in them existentially and non-existentially'. Example: 'khamr' is defined by intoxication.
3
List the excluded categories
Proper nouns (a'lam), inductively established grammatical rules (rafa' al-fa'il, nasb al-maf'ul), and conventionally generalized names (asma' al-fa'ilin, al-mufa''ilin, al-sifat like 'alim and qadir) are excluded from the dispute.
4
Examine the example given
The passage uses the question: can nabidh (date wine) be called khamr by qiyas due to shared intoxication, thus receiving all rulings of khamr? This is the core point of contention.

المطلب الثاني : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات الأسماء واللغات معلوم أن الأصوليين لم يتفقوا في شأن إثبات الأسماء واللغات بالقياس، فذهب بعض الشافعية إلى جوازه منهم الشيرازي والرازي والبيضاوي، وأنكره الحنفية وجماعة من الأصوليين منهم القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني وإمام الحرمين والغزالي وأكثر الشافعية. وقد حدد محمد حسن هيتو محل النزاع في المسألة، فقال: اعلم أن محل الخلاف في هذه المسألة في الأسماء الموضوعة للمعاني المخصوصة الدائرة مع الصفات الموجودة فيها وجودا وعدما، كالخمر فإنها اسم للمسكر المعتصر من العنب، وهذا الاسم دائر مع الإسكار وجودا وعدما . فهل يقاس عليه النبيذ في كونه مشاركا له في وصف الإسكار ويقال له خمر، فتثبت له جميع أحكام الخمر؟ فيه الخلاف . فليس الخلاف في أسماء الأعلام لأنها غير معقولة المعاني، ولا فيما ثبت بالاستقراء كرفع الفاعل ونصب المفعول، ولا فيما ثبت تعميمه بالوضع نحو أسماء الفاعلين والمفعولين وأسماء الصفات كالعالم والقادر، لأنها واجبة الاطراد نظرا إلى تحقيق معنى الاسم، فإن اسم العالم لمن قام به العلم، فإطلاقه على كل من قام به العلم بالوضع لا بالقياس وما دامت المسألة محل خلاف بهذه الصورة، فلا ريب أن يكون استدلال بعض العلماء بالقياس في إثبات الأسماء واللغات مثارا للجدل والاعتراض. وذلك مثل استدلال الشافعي على أن لفظ (التسريح) صريح في الطلاق، بالقياس على لفظ (الطلاق). أو يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في أن نبيذ التمر يسمى خمرا بالقياس على العنب، باعتبار مخامرته للعقل وتغطيته له وفعله كفعل الخمر، فيعترض الحنفي أو المالكي قائلا : هذا إثبات لغة بالقياس، وذلك لا يجوز، لأن اللغة إنما طريقها التوقيف والإجماع .. والأسماء واللغات مفروغ منها، وذلك إما بالإلهام
The Second Topic: The objection to using qiyas in establishing names and languages. It is known that the usul scholars did not agree regarding the establishment of names and languages by qiyas. Some of the Shafi'is went to its permissibility, among them al-Shirazi, al-Razi, and al-Baydawi, and the Hanafis and a group of usul scholars denied it, including al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, Imam al-Haramayn, al-Ghazali, and most of the Shafi'is. And Muhammad Hasan Hitu defined the point of dispute in the issue, saying: Know that the point of disagreement in this issue concerns names that are placed for specific meanings that revolve with attributes present in them existentially and non-existentially, like 'khamr' - it is a name for the intoxicating liquid pressed from grapes, and this name revolves with intoxication existentially and non-existentially. So can it be analogized to nabidh (date wine) in its sharing the attribute of intoxication, and it be called khamr, so that all the rulings of khamr apply to it? Therein lies the disagreement. There is no disagreement concerning proper nouns because their meanings are not intelligible, nor concerning what is established by induction like raising the subject and putting the object in accusative, nor concerning what is established as generalized by convention like names of agents and patients and names of attributes such as al-'alim (the knowing) and al-qadir (the powerful), because they are necessarily consistent considering the verification of the meaning of the name; for the name 'alim is for one in whom knowledge resides, so its application to everyone in whom knowledge resides is by convention, not by qiyas. As long as the issue is a point of disagreement in this manner, there is no doubt that some scholars' use of qiyas in establishing names and languages becomes a cause of jadal and objection. That is like the Shafi'i's argument that the word 'tasrih' is clear in divorcing, by analogy with the word 'talaq'. Or the Shafi'i or Hanbali argues that date wine is called khamr by analogy with grapes, considering its intoxication of the mind and covering it and its effect like the effect of khamr, so the Hanafi or Maliki objects saying: This is establishing a language by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because language's only way is by revelation (tawqif) and consensus... and names and languages are already established, either by inspiration...
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical presentation of the dispute over using qiyas to establish names and languages (asma' wa lughat). The author first notes the disagreement among usul scholars: some Shafi'is allow it, while the Hanafis and many others deny it. Then Muhammad Hasan Hitu clarifies the specific point of contention: it concerns names that are based on attributes, such as 'khamr' (wine) which is defined by the attribute of intoxication. The question is whether a new beverage (nabidh) that shares the attribute can be called 'khamr' by qiyas, thereby receiving the same rulings. Hitu excludes several categories: proper nouns, inductively established grammatical rules, and conventionally generalized names (like agent nouns). So the dispute is only about names that are based on attributes. The passage then gives two examples of scholars using qiyas to extend names: (1) the Shafi'i argument that 'tasrih' is clear in divorce by analogy with 'talaq', and (2) the Shafi'i or Hanbali argument that date wine is called khamr by analogy with grape wine due to shared intoxication. The objection from a Hanafi or Maliki follows: such qiyas is invalid because language is established only by tawqif (divine revelation or convention) and consensus, and names and languages are already settled, either by inspiration or other means. The response to this objection is not included in the text, leaving the argument incomplete.

Key Terms
القياس al-qiyas
الأسماء al-asma'
اللغات al-lughat
التوقيف al-tawqif

Analyzing the Objection: Language by Tawqif Only
Author presents the dispute and defines the point of contention with examples of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that language is established only by tawqif, and identify its premises and conclusion.

Core Idea
The objection uses the principle that language is known only by revelation, making qiyas irrelevant.

The passage presents an objection from Hanafi and Maliki scholars: using qiyas to establish names is invalid because language is based on tawqif (revelation) and consensus. This lesson breaks down the logical structure of that objection, even though the passage ends without the response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's main claim
The objector (Hanafi or Maliki) says: 'This is establishing a language by qiyas, and that is not permissible.' Find this statement in the passage.
2
Find the reason given for the claim
The objector argues that language's only way is by tawqif (revelation) and ijma' (consensus). So qiyas has no role in establishing linguistic terms.
3
Determine the implicit premise about how language is known
The objector assumes that language is fixed and pre-determined (either by divine inspiration or human convention), not subject to analogical extension. This is an epistemological stance.
4
Recognize that the argument is incomplete without the response
The passage ends mid-sentence at 'al-ilham' (inspiration). The response from the Shafi'i side is missing. In jadal, this objection would need a jawab to complete the exchange.

المطلب الثاني : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات الأسماء واللغات معلوم أن الأصوليين لم يتفقوا في شأن إثبات الأسماء واللغات بالقياس، فذهب بعض الشافعية إلى جوازه منهم الشيرازي والرازي والبيضاوي، وأنكره الحنفية وجماعة من الأصوليين منهم القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني وإمام الحرمين والغزالي وأكثر الشافعية. وقد حدد محمد حسن هيتو محل النزاع في المسألة، فقال: اعلم أن محل الخلاف في هذه المسألة في الأسماء الموضوعة للمعاني المخصوصة الدائرة مع الصفات الموجودة فيها وجودا وعدما، كالخمر فإنها اسم للمسكر المعتصر من العنب، وهذا الاسم دائر مع الإسكار وجودا وعدما . فهل يقاس عليه النبيذ في كونه مشاركا له في وصف الإسكار ويقال له خمر، فتثبت له جميع أحكام الخمر؟ فيه الخلاف . فليس الخلاف في أسماء الأعلام لأنها غير معقولة المعاني، ولا فيما ثبت بالاستقراء كرفع الفاعل ونصب المفعول، ولا فيما ثبت تعميمه بالوضع نحو أسماء الفاعلين والمفعولين وأسماء الصفات كالعالم والقادر، لأنها واجبة الاطراد نظرا إلى تحقيق معنى الاسم، فإن اسم العالم لمن قام به العلم، فإطلاقه على كل من قام به العلم بالوضع لا بالقياس وما دامت المسألة محل خلاف بهذه الصورة، فلا ريب أن يكون استدلال بعض العلماء بالقياس في إثبات الأسماء واللغات مثارا للجدل والاعتراض. وذلك مثل استدلال الشافعي على أن لفظ (التسريح) صريح في الطلاق، بالقياس على لفظ (الطلاق). أو يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في أن نبيذ التمر يسمى خمرا بالقياس على العنب، باعتبار مخامرته للعقل وتغطيته له وفعله كفعل الخمر، فيعترض الحنفي أو المالكي قائلا : هذا إثبات لغة بالقياس، وذلك لا يجوز، لأن اللغة إنما طريقها التوقيف والإجماع .. والأسماء واللغات مفروغ منها، وذلك إما بالإلهام
The Second Topic: The objection to using qiyas in establishing names and languages. It is known that the usul scholars did not agree regarding the establishment of names and languages by qiyas. Some of the Shafi'is went to its permissibility, among them al-Shirazi, al-Razi, and al-Baydawi, and the Hanafis and a group of usul scholars denied it, including al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, Imam al-Haramayn, al-Ghazali, and most of the Shafi'is. And Muhammad Hasan Hitu defined the point of dispute in the issue, saying: Know that the point of disagreement in this issue concerns names that are placed for specific meanings that revolve with attributes present in them existentially and non-existentially, like 'khamr' - it is a name for the intoxicating liquid pressed from grapes, and this name revolves with intoxication existentially and non-existentially. So can it be analogized to nabidh (date wine) in its sharing the attribute of intoxication, and it be called khamr, so that all the rulings of khamr apply to it? Therein lies the disagreement. There is no disagreement concerning proper nouns because their meanings are not intelligible, nor concerning what is established by induction like raising the subject and putting the object in accusative, nor concerning what is established as generalized by convention like names of agents and patients and names of attributes such as al-'alim (the knowing) and al-qadir (the powerful), because they are necessarily consistent considering the verification of the meaning of the name; for the name 'alim is for one in whom knowledge resides, so its application to everyone in whom knowledge resides is by convention, not by qiyas. As long as the issue is a point of disagreement in this manner, there is no doubt that some scholars' use of qiyas in establishing names and languages becomes a cause of jadal and objection. That is like the Shafi'i's argument that the word 'tasrih' is clear in divorcing, by analogy with the word 'talaq'. Or the Shafi'i or Hanbali argues that date wine is called khamr by analogy with grapes, considering its intoxication of the mind and covering it and its effect like the effect of khamr, so the Hanafi or Maliki objects saying: This is establishing a language by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because language's only way is by revelation (tawqif) and consensus... and names and languages are already established, either by inspiration...
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical presentation of the dispute over using qiyas to establish names and languages (asma' wa lughat). The author first notes the disagreement among usul scholars: some Shafi'is allow it, while the Hanafis and many others deny it. Then Muhammad Hasan Hitu clarifies the specific point of contention: it concerns names that are based on attributes, such as 'khamr' (wine) which is defined by the attribute of intoxication. The question is whether a new beverage (nabidh) that shares the attribute can be called 'khamr' by qiyas, thereby receiving the same rulings. Hitu excludes several categories: proper nouns, inductively established grammatical rules, and conventionally generalized names (like agent nouns). So the dispute is only about names that are based on attributes. The passage then gives two examples of scholars using qiyas to extend names: (1) the Shafi'i argument that 'tasrih' is clear in divorce by analogy with 'talaq', and (2) the Shafi'i or Hanbali argument that date wine is called khamr by analogy with grape wine due to shared intoxication. The objection from a Hanafi or Maliki follows: such qiyas is invalid because language is established only by tawqif (divine revelation or convention) and consensus, and names and languages are already settled, either by inspiration or other means. The response to this objection is not included in the text, leaving the argument incomplete.

Key Terms
القياس al-qiyas
التوقيف al-tawqif
الاعتراض al-i'tirad
الجواب al-jawab

The Qiyas Linguistic Analogy Debate
Dialectical exchange on analogical extension of names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange regarding the use of qiyas to extend linguistic names, and evaluate the opposing positions using the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
Can analogy extend a name? This is the heart of a fierce usuli dispute.

This passage defines the precise scope of the debate over using qiyas (analogical reasoning) to establish new linguistic names. It then presents a typical exchange: a Shafi'i or Hanbali argues that date wine should be called 'khamr' by analogy to grape wine, while a Hanafi or Maliki objects that language is solely based on divine teaching or convention. You will learn to dissect such dialectical exchanges using the tools of jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the locus of dispute
The passage first specifies which names are under dispute: those based on attributes that are present or absent, like 'khamr' (intoxicant). Proper names and terms already generalized by convention are excluded. Note this limitation carefully.
2
Extract the claimant's analogical argument
The claimant (Shafi'i/Hanbali) argues that the name 'khamr' and its rulings apply to date wine (nabidh) by qiyas. Identify the original case (asl: grape wine), the new case (far': date wine), and the shared attribute ('illa: intoxication).
This is an analogical argument in the realm of linguistics, not just legal rulings.
3
Identify the objector's grounds
The objector (Hanafi/Maliki) rejects qiyas in language, asserting that language is only known through tawqif (divine teaching) or consensus. They claim all necessary names are already established, leaving no room for analogy.
This objection attacks the very possibility of linguistic analogy.
4
Analyze the response and assess the dialectical outcome
The claimant responds by simply asserting that establishing language by qiyas is permissible and a principle of their school. In jadal, this response does not provide new evidence; it merely reaffirms the position. Assess whether the objector's challenge has been effectively met.
The passage does not declare a winner; it illustrates an unresolved methodological clash.

بعض الشافعية إلى جوازه منهم الشيرازي والرازي والبيضاوي، وأنكره الحنفية وجماعة من الأصوليين منهم القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني وإمام الحرمين والغزالي وأكثر الشافعية (1) . وقد حدد محمد حسن هيتو محل النزاع في المسألة، فقال: اعلم أن محل الخلاف في هذه المسألة في الأسماء الموضوعة للمعاني المخصوصة الدائرة مع الصفات الموجودة فيها وجودًا وعدما، كالخمر فإنها اسم للمسكر المعتصر من العنب، وهذا الاسم دائر مع الإسكار - وجودًا وعدما . فهل يقاس عليه النبيذ في كونه مشاركًا له في وصف الإسكار ويقال له خمر، فتثبت له جميع أحكام الخمر؟ فيه الخلاف . فليس الخلاف في أسماء الأعلام لأنها غير معقولة المعاني، ولا فيما ثبت بالاستقراء كرفع الفاعل ونصب المفعول، ولا فيما ثبت تعميمه بالوضع نحو أسماء الفاعلين والمفعولين وأسماء الصفات كالعالم والقادر، لأنها واجبة الاطراد نظرًا إلى تحقيق معنى الاسم، فإن اسم العالم لمن قام به العلم، فإطلاقه على كل من قام به العلم بالوضع لا بالقياس وما دامت المسألة محل خلاف بهذه الصورة، فلا ريب أن يكون استدلال بعض العلماء بالقياس في إثبات الأسماء واللغات مثارًا للجدل والاعتراض. وذلك مثل استدلال الشافعي على أن لفظ (التسريح) صريح في الطلاق، بالقياس على لفظ (الطلاق). أو يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في أن نبيذ التمر يسمى خمرا بالقياس على العنب، باعتبار مخامرته للعقل وتغطيته له وفعله كفعل الخمر، فيعترض الحنفي أو المالكي قائلا : هذا إثبات لغة بالقياس، وذلك لا يجوز، لأن اللغة إنما طريقها التوقيف والإجماع .. والأسماء واللغات مفروغ منها، وذلك إما بالإلهام والتعليم من الله سبحانه لآدم، حيث علمه أسماء الأشياء، والأعمال كلها داخلة فيها، أو من حيث إن أهل اللغة لم يتركوا شيئًا إلا وقد وضعوا له اسما، فلم يبق للقياس مساغ . والجواب للشافعي أو الحنبلي أن يقول أن إثبات اللغة بالقياس جائز، وهو أصل عندنا (1) .
Some Shafi'is permit it (using qiyas to establish names), among them al-Shirazi, al-Razi, and al-Baydawi. But the Hanafis and a group of usuliyyun, including al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, Imam al-Haramayn, al-Ghazali, and most Shafi'is, rejected it. Muhammad Hasan Haytu defined the locus of dispute in this issue, saying: Know that the locus of disagreement in this issue is concerning names that are placed for specific meanings that revolve with attributes present in them, whether existing or not, like 'khamr' which is a name for the intoxicating (juice) pressed from grapes, and this name revolves with intoxication, whether existing or not. So, is date wine (nabidh) analogized to it in sharing the attribute of intoxication and called 'khamr', thus all the rulings of khamr are established for it? That is the dispute. The dispute is not about proper names because they are not meaningful, nor about what is established by induction (istiqra') like raising the subject and object, nor about what is established by generalization by linguistic convention (wad') such as agent and patient nouns and attribute nouns like 'alim' and 'qadir', because they are necessarily consistent in view of realizing the meaning of the name; the name 'alim' is for the one in whom knowledge resides, so applying it to everyone in whom knowledge resides is by convention, not by qiyas. As long as the issue is disputed in this form, there is no doubt that some scholars' reasoning by qiyas in establishing names and languages is a source of dialectic and objection. For example, al-Shafi'i's reasoning that the term 'tasrih' is explicit in divorce, by analogy to the term 'talaq'. Or the Shafi'i or Hanbali reasons that date wine is called khamr by analogy to grape wine, considering its effect on the mind and covering it, and its action is like that of khamr. Then the Hanafi or Maliki objects, saying: This is establishing language by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because the only way for language is tawqif (divine instruction) and consensus... and names and languages are already established, either by divine inspiration and teaching from Allah to Adam, where He taught him the names of things, and all actions are included in them, or because the people of language left nothing without naming it, so there is no room for qiyas. The response of the Shafi'i or Hanbali is to say that establishing language by qiyas is permissible, and it is a principle with us.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about whether qiyas (analogical reasoning) can be used to extend the application of names and linguistic terms to new cases. The author begins by stating the positions of different schools: some Shafi'is permit it, but Hanafis and most other usuliyyun reject it. Then Muhammad Hasan Haytu specifies the exact locus of the dispute: it concerns names that are given to meanings based on attributes that come and go (like intoxication for khamr). The question is whether a new substance (like date wine) that shares the attribute (intoxication) can be called by the same name (khamr) via qiyas, thereby receiving all its rulings. He clarifies that the dispute does not include proper names (which lack meaning), terms established by induction (like grammatical rules), or terms where the generalization is already explicit in the linguistic convention (like agent nouns). After setting the scope, the author gives two examples: al-Shafi'i's use of qiyas to claim that 'tasrih' (setting free) is explicit in divorce by analogy to 'talaq' (divorce), and the claim that date wine is called khamr by analogy to grape wine. The objection from the opposing side is that language is only known through tawqif (divine teaching) or consensus of the language community; all necessary names have already been established, so there is no role for qiyas. The response is that establishing language by qiyas is permissible and is a principle accepted by those who allow it.

قياس
توقيف
خمر

Dialectics of Qiyas in Customary Matters
Author explains the objection to qiyas for matters reliant on custom and provides a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a jadal exchange regarding the scope of qiyas in customary matters and evaluate the validity of the response.

Core Idea
Custom matters resist analogy unless a clear indication is found.

This passage presents a classic debate between Maliki and Hanafi jurists over whether analogical reasoning can establish customary rulings. The objection is that custom is known by experience, not qiyas. The Maliki response attempts to find an indication (amara) to justify the analogy. This lesson guides you through the dialectical structure and the underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's position
Determine what the Maliki is trying to prove: that a pregnant woman can menstruate, using analogy with istihada blood or non-pregnant women.
2
Identify the objector's challenge
Find the Hanafi objection: menstruation is a customary matter known by experience, so analogical reasoning is impermissible.
3
Analyze the response
See how the Maliki concedes the principle but argues that here there is an indication (amara): the similar behavior of menstrual and istihada blood, and pregnancy not preventing istihada.
4
Assess the indication
Evaluate whether the analogy is valid: does the pattern of occurrence and cessation provide a reliable connection?
5
Evaluate the outcome
Consider the dismissive remark that this is 'qiyas of custom' and custom may differ. Does the objector win the exchange?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الضرب الذي لا أمارة عليه، وذلك كإثبات أقل الحيض وأكثره فما أشبه ذلك مما طريقه العادة والخلقة ولا أمارة عليه فلا مدخل للقياس فيه. هذا الاتجاه الغالب إلى رفض الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات العادات، فقد ضرب الباجي مثالا للاستدلال به والاعتراض عليه. وذلك أن يستدل المالكي في أن الحامل تحيض بالقياس، مبررا ذلك بأن الحمل عارض لا يمنع دم الاستحاضة، فلم يمنع دم الحيض كالرضاع .. أو أنه دم رأته في وقته على صفته ونعته فكان حيضًا، كالدم الذي تراه غير الحامل. فيقول الحنفي أو الحنبلي معترضا : هذا إثبات أمر طريقه العادة والتجربة، فلا يجوز إثباته بالقياس .. والجواب أن يقول المستدل : أسلم أنه لا يجوز أن يثبت ذلك بالقياس ما لا أمارة عليه ولا دلالة فأما إن كانت عليه أمارة ودلالة فإن ذلك جائز، وذلك أن دم الحيض ودم الاستحاضة دمان متشاكلان لا أحدهما إلا من يرى الآخر، فالصغيرة لا ترى دم الحيض ولا دم الاستحاضة، لكنها إذا أصبحت ممن تحيض فإنها ترى دم الحيض ودم الاستحاضة، فإذا يئست من المحيض ارتفع دم الحيض وارتفع دم الاستحاضة فلما أثبتت العادة والتجربة أنهما على طريق واحد في الوجود، وكان الحمل لا يمنع أحدهما وهو الاستحاضة، دل على أنه لا الآخر يمنع. وقال في التعليق على مسألة قياس دم الحيض بالنسبة للحامل على دم الاستحاضة: «هذا قياس العادة، والعادة قد تختلف»
As for the type that has no indication—such as establishing the minimum or maximum of menstruation and the like, which are matters of custom and nature and have no indication—then analogy has no entry in it. This is the predominant tendency to reject analogical reasoning in establishing customary matters. Al-Baji gave an example of using it and objecting to it. That is, the Maliki argues that a pregnant woman menstruates by analogy, justifying that pregnancy is an incidental condition that does not prevent blood of istihada, so it does not prevent menstrual blood, like breastfeeding... Or that it is blood she sees at its proper time with its description and attribute, so it is menstruation, like the blood that a non-pregnant woman sees. The Hanafi or Hanbali objects saying: This is establishing a matter whose way is custom and experience, so it is not permissible to establish it by analogy. The response is that the arguer says: I concede that it is not permissible to establish by analogy that which has no indication or evidence; but as for that which has an indication and evidence, it is permissible. That is because menstrual blood and istihada blood are two similar types of blood—one is not seen except by one who sees the other. A young girl sees neither menstrual blood nor istihada blood, but when she becomes one who menstruates, she sees both. When she reaches menopause, menstrual blood ceases and istihada blood ceases. Since custom and experience have established that they follow the same path in existence, and pregnancy does not prevent one of them, namely istihada, it indicates that it does not prevent the other either. And he said in commentary on the issue of analogizing menstrual blood of the pregnant woman to istihada blood: 'This is an analogy of custom, and custom may differ.'
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange regarding the use of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in matters that are established by custom ('adah) and experience (tajriba). The author first states the general rule: for matters that have no observable indication (amara) such as the minimum and maximum duration of menstruation, qiyas is not applicable. Then he gives an example from al-Baji: a Maliki jurist argues that a pregnant woman can menstruate by qiyas, analogizing her situation to that of a breastfeeding woman or to a non-pregnant woman who sees blood at the correct time. The Hanafi or Hanbali objector counters that this is a matter of custom and experience, not to be proven by qiyas. The Maliki responds by accepting the principle but insisting that here there is an indication: menstrual blood and istihada blood are similar in their pattern of appearance and cessation, and since pregnancy does not prevent istihada, it likely does not prevent menstruation either. The passage ends with a quote from al-Tilimsani who dismisses this as a 'qiyas of custom' which is unreliable because custom can vary. The lesson illustrates how jurists debated the scope of qiyas and the role of customary knowledge.

Key Terms
القياس
العادة
الأمارة

C) The pregnant woman has no womb.
D) The analogy uses a weak 'illa.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, establishing a customary matter by qiyas requires a clear indication; otherwise, custom itself is the primary evidence.

Objection to Qiyas in Establishing Substitutes
Author discusses the objection to using qiyas to establish substitutes with a Hajj example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key moves in a jadal exchange about the scope of qiyas and reconstruct the charge of inconsistency (tanāquḍ).

Core Idea
The Hanafi objection fails if they themselves use qiyas for substitutes.

This passage illustrates a classic dialectical maneuver in uṣūl al-fiqh: pointing out that your opponent's position is internally inconsistent. The debate concerns whether qiyas can establish substitute rulings (abdal). A Maliki/Shafi'i jurist uses qiyas to argue that a pilgrim prevented from completing Hajj may substitute fasting for the sacrificial animal. The Hanafi objector insists that substitutes are matters of tawqīf (divine designation) and cannot be known by qiyas. The Maliki/Shafi'i responds by accusing the Hanafi of contradiction, citing that the Hanafi school itself uses qiyas to permit nabīdh as a substitute for water in wudū'. Let's dissect this dialectical exchange step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
First, locate the two opposing claims: the Maliki/Shafi'i asserts that substitutes can be established by qiyas; the Hanafi denies this, saying qiyas is invalid for substitutes.
The Hanafi position is attributed to 'some companions of Abu Hanifa'.
2
Extract the qiyas argument
The Maliki/Shafi'i's specific argument: the sacrificial animal (hady) is an obligation linked to iḥrām, so if one cannot find it, one may transfer to fasting. This is a qiyas: the hady is the original case, and fasting is the substitute.
This qiyas assumes that other obligations with substitutes also allow transfer when the primary cannot be fulfilled.
3
Understand the Hanafi objection
The Hanafi replies that substitutes are matters of tawqīf—they can only be known from revelation, not human reasoning like qiyas. Therefore, the Maliki/Shafi'i's use of qiyas is invalid.
The key premise: 'what stands in place of another in terms of interest cannot be known by analogy.'
4
Analyze the response – charge of contradiction
The Maliki/Shafi'i counters by pointing out that the Hanafis themselves use qiyas to permit nabīdh as a substitute for water in wudū'. This is also an example of establishing a substitute by qiyas, so the Hanafi is inconsistent.
The charge of tanāquḍ (contradiction) is a strong dialectical move: if the Hanafi accepts qiyas for one substitute, he must accept it for others unless he provides a distinguishing factor.
5
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
The Hanafi's objection is weakened by the inconsistency. The passage does not record a further response, so in this exchange the Maliki/Shafi'i's argument stands as a successful refutation of the objection.
The lesson is not about which school is correct, but about the logical structure of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات الأبدال ذهب عامة المالكية وأغلب الشافعية إلى جواز إثبات الأبدال بالقياس، وقال بعض أصحاب أبي حنيفة : لا يجوز إثباتها بالقياس ، لذلك فإن استدلال مالكي أو شافعي بالقياس في إثبات الأبدال سيثير اعتراض الحنفي بدعوى عدم جواز هذا الاستدلال، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي في أن المحصر في الحج ينتقل إلى الصوم إذا لم يجد الهدي، بإجراء القياس، وذلك أنه هدي يتعلق وجوبه بالإحرام، فجاز الانتقال عنه إلى الصوم فيقول الحنفي : هذا إثبات بدل بالقياس، ولا يجوز إثبات الأبدال بالقياس، فإن ما يقوم مقام الغير في المصلحة لا يعلم بالقياس وإنما طريقه التوقيف.. والجواب أن يقول المستدل، عندنا يجوز ذلك، ثم إنكم تناقضون أنفسكم في هذا، فإنكم قستم في إجازة الوضوء بالنبيذ على الوضوء بالماء، وهذا إثبات بدل بالقياس فسقط ما قلتموه
Fourth Section: The Objection to Analogical Reasoning in Establishing Substitutes. The majority of Malikis and most Shafi'is hold that it is permissible to establish substitutes by analogy. Some of Abu Hanifa's companions said: It is not permissible to establish them by analogy. Therefore, a Maliki or Shafi'i's use of analogy to establish substitutes will provoke the objection of the Hanafi on the grounds that this reasoning is not permissible. An example is that the Maliki or Shafi'i argues, regarding the one who is prevented (muhsar) during Hajj, that he transfers to fasting if he cannot find the sacrificial animal, by performing analogy: that it is a sacrificial animal whose obligation is linked to ihram, so it is permissible to transfer from it to fasting. The Hanafi says: This is establishing a substitute by analogy, and it is not permissible to establish substitutes by analogy. For what stands in place of another in terms of interest cannot be known by analogy; its method is only divine designation (tawqif). The response is that the arguer says: According to us, this is permissible. Moreover, you contradict yourselves in this, for you have used analogy in permitting ablution with nabidh (date-drink) by analogy with ablution with water—and this is establishing a substitute by analogy. Thus your argument collapses.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of using qiyas to establish substitutes (abdal) in Islamic law. It begins by stating the positions: the majority of Malikis and Shafi'is permit it, while some Hanafis reject it. The author then presents a concrete example: when a pilgrim is prevented from completing Hajj and cannot find a sacrificial animal, the Maliki/Shafi'i argues that he may substitute fasting, analogizing the sacrificial animal to other obligations that have substitutes. The Hanafi objector responds that substitutes are matters of divine designation (tawqif) and cannot be known through human reasoning. The Maliki/Shafi'i then counters by accusing the Hanafi of inconsistency: the Hanafi school itself uses qiyas in permitting ablution with nabidh as a substitute for water, which is itself an example of establishing a substitute by qiyas. This highlights a key dialectical move: charging the opponent with internal contradiction (tanâqud). The lesson underscores the methodological debate about the scope of qiyas in legal theory.

Hanafi Objection to Qiyas for Substitutes and the Dialectical Response
Dialectical exchange on permissibility of qiyas for establishing substitutes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the dialectical exchange between Hanafis and Malikis/Shafi'is on using qiyas to establish substitutes.

Core Idea
Substitutes are known by revelation, not analogy — unless your opponent uses analogy elsewhere.

In this passage, the author presents a classic dialectical move: one school objects to a qiyas-based argument on the grounds that the subject matter (substitutes) is not open to analogy, and the other school counters by exposing a contradiction in the objector's own practice. This exchange illustrates how munaẓara (debate) requires not only knowledge of legal principles but also tactical awareness of an opponent's commitments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Who permits using qiyas for substitutes? Who prohibits it? Note the schools: majority of Malikis and most Shafi'is permit; some Hanafis prohibit.
2
Extract the specific example and the qiyas argument
The example: a pilgrim prevented from completing Hajj (muḥṣar) who cannot find a sacrificial animal (hady). The Maliki/Shafi'i argues by qiyas that since the obligation of hady is tied to iḥrām, transfer to fasting is allowed.
3
Identify the Hanafi objection and its underlying principle
The Hanafi objects: this is establishing a substitute by qiyas, which is invalid. Principle: substitutes are known only by tawqīf (revelation), not by human reasoning.
4
Identify the Maliki/Shafi'i response and the contradiction
The response is twofold: (1) According to our school it is permissible. (2) You Hanafis contradict yourselves by using qiyas to permit ablution with nabīdh based on ablution with water—that is also establishing a substitute by qiyas.
5
Assess the dialectical effectiveness
Does the response neutralize the objection? Yes, by showing the Hanafi position is inconsistent. The objection fails because the Hanafis themselves rely on qiyas in a parallel case.

The fourth section: Objection to using qiyas in establishing substitutes. The majority of Malikis and most Shafi'is held the permissibility of establishing substitutes by qiyas, and some of Abu Hanifa's companions said: It is not permissible to establish them by qiyas. Therefore, a Maliki or Shafi'i's use of qiyas in establishing substitutes will provoke a Hanafi objection on the grounds that such inference is not permissible. An example is when a Maliki or Shafi'i uses qiyas to argue that a pilgrim prevented from completing Hajj may transfer to fasting if he cannot find a sacrificial animal, because it is a sacrificial animal whose obligation is tied to ihram, so transfer to fasting is permitted. Then the Hanafi says: This is establishing a substitute by qiyas, and establishing substitutes by qiyas is not permissible. For what stands in the place of another in terms of benefit is not known by qiyas; its method is only revelation. The answer is that the arguer says: According to us, that is permissible. Moreover, you contradict yourselves in this, for you used qiyas in permitting ablution with nabidh based on ablution with water, and this is establishing a substitute by qiyas. So your objection falls.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange regarding the legitimacy of using qiyas (analogical reasoning) to establish substitutes (abdal) in Islamic law. The author first states the majority position of Malikis and Shafi'is who permit establishing substitutes via qiyas, while some Hanafis prohibit it. The example given is the case of a pilgrim prevented from completing Hajj (muhsar) who cannot find a sacrificial animal (hady). The Maliki or Shafi'i argues by qiyas that because the obligation of the sacrificial animal is tied to ihram, transfer to fasting is permitted, just as in other cases. The Hanafi objects that this is establishing a substitute by qiyas, which is invalid because substitutes are known only by revelation (tawqif), not by human reasoning. The answer from the Maliki/Shafi'i arguer is twofold: first, according to their school it is permissible; second, the Hanafis contradict themselves because they themselves use qiyas to permit ablution with nabidh (date-water) based on ablution with water, which is also establishing a substitute by qiyas. Thus the Hanafi objection is self-defeating.

Objection to Using Qiyas for Muqaddarat
Author presents the dispute over using qiyas to establish legally determined quantities, with a Shafi'i response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Hanafi objection to using qiyas for muqaddarat and evaluate the Shafi'i response of inconsistency.

Core Idea
The dispute over muqaddarat reveals that even opponents of qiyas in quantity matters rely on it elsewhere.

This lesson examines a key dialectical exchange in Usul al-Fiqh: the use of qiyas to establish legally determined quantities (muqaddarat). The Shafi'is permit it; the Hanafis reject it, claiming such quantities must be based on revelation or consensus. The Shafi'i response points to cases where Hanafis themselves used qiyas for quantities, revealing an internal contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Read the passage and find the two positions: Shafi'is permit using qiyas for muqaddarat; Hanafis reject it.
Muqaddarat are fixed legal thresholds like nisab or age of majority.
2
Extract the examples
Note the two analogical arguments: (1) Maliki on theft: quarter dinar analogy with four dinars. (2) Shafi'i on majority: sixteenth year for boy analogy with girl's eighteenth year.
These are the qiyas arguments being objected to.
3
Analyze the Hanafi objection
The Hanafi says attachment of a ruling to a specific quantity must be by tawqif (revelation) or ittifaq (consensus), not ijtihad or qiyas.
This is a principled rejection of reasoning from analogy for quantities.
4
Evaluate the Shafi'i response
The Shafi'i responds that Hanafis themselves use qiyas for quantities: (a) size of hole in khuff by three fingers based on wiping, (b) number for Friday prayer as four by qiyas.
This points to inconsistency: if qiyas is invalid for quantities, why do Hanafis use it in these cases?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الخامس : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات المقدرات هذه مسألة خلافية أيضًا، فقد ذهب الشافعية إلى جواز إثبات المقدرات بالقياس، لكن أصحاب أبي حنيفة قالوا لا يجوز إثبات المقدرات بالقياس، وعليه فإن الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات المقدرات، سيكون محل اعتراض من قبل المخالفين، وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن سرقة ربع دينار يجب بها القطع، بأن الدينار قدر يحقن الكافر به دمه، فوجب أن يتعلق القطع بسرقة ربعه كالأربعة دنانير . ومثل أن يستدل الشافعي في حد البلوغ : إن السنة السادسة عشرة سنة يحكم فيها ببلوغ الجارية، فيحكم فيها ببلوغ الغلام كالثامنة عشرة، فيقول الحنفي : هذا إثبات مقدر بالقياس، وذلك لا يجوز لأن تعلق الحكم بقدر دون قدر طريقه التوقيف أو الاتفاق لا الاجتهاد أو القياس والجواب للشافعي أن يقول : إن إثبات المقدرات عندنا جائز بالقياس ، ثم إن الحنفية يناقضون أنفسهم في هذا إذ قدروا الخرق في الخف بثلاثة أصابع قياسًا على المسح ؛ كما قدروا العدد في الجمعة بأربعة بالقياس، وليس في ذلك شيء من التوقيف أو الاتفاق
The fifth section: Objection to using qiyas in establishing determined quantities. This is also a disputed issue. The Shafi'is held the permissibility of establishing determined quantities by qiyas, but the companions of Abu Hanifa said it is not permissible to establish determined quantities by qiyas. Therefore, using qiyas in establishing determined quantities will be subject to objection by opponents. An example is when a Maliki argues that stealing a quarter of a dinar necessitates amputation, because a dinar is a quantity that preserves the life of the infidel, so it is necessary that amputation be attached to stealing its quarter, like four dinars. Another example is when a Shafi'i argues regarding the age of majority: the sixteenth year is a year in which a girl is judged to have attained majority, so a boy should be judged to have attained majority in it, like the eighteenth year. Then the Hanafi says: This is establishing a determined quantity by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because the attachment of a ruling to one quantity rather than another is by revelation or consensus, not by ijtihad or qiyas. The answer for the Shafi'i is to say: According to us, establishing determined quantities by qiyas is permissible. Moreover, the Hanafis contradict themselves in this, for they determined the hole in the khuff by three fingers based on qiyas with wiping, and they determined the number for Friday prayer as four by qiyas, and there is no revelation or consensus in that.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical theme, now addressing the use of qiyas to establish legally determined quantities (muqaddarat) such as thresholds for amputation or ages of majority. The Shafi'is permit determining such quantities via qiyas, while the Hanafis reject it. Two examples are given: a Maliki argues that stealing a quarter of a dinar triggers amputation by analogy with four dinars; a Shafi'i argues that the age of majority for a boy should be sixteenth year by analogy with a girl's eighteenth year. The Hanafi objects that such quantities are matters of revelation or consensus, not ijtihad. The Shafi'i response again points to Hanafi inconsistency: they themselves determined the size of a hole in leather socks (khuff) as three fingers by qiyas with wiping, and the number of congregants for Friday prayer as four by qiyas, without textual basis.

Rebuttal by Contradiction in Hudud Qiyas Debate
Author presents the dispute over using qiyas to establish fixed punishments, with a response pointing to Hanafi inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a rebuttal by contradiction (mu'arada bi al-naqd) in a jadal exchange and evaluate whether it successfully undermines the opponent's position.

Core Idea
Inconsistent application of a principle destroys its authority in debate.

This passage presents a classic jadal move: the Hanafi opponent objects to using qiyas for hudud, but the respondent points out that the Hanafis themselves use qiyas for a similar case – highway robbery. This is not a logical refutation of the objection, but a powerful dialectical move that exposes inconsistency. We will analyze this strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claimant's Position (Mudda'i)
The Maliki or Shafi'i claimant asserts that the punishment for liwat (sodomy) is obligatory by qiyas on the punishment for zina (adultery/fornication). This is a claim that a fixed punishment (hadd) can be established through analogical reasoning.
The claimant is using qiyas to extend a known ruling to a new case.
2
Identify the Objector's Objection (Mu'tarid)
The Hanafi objector responds that qiyas cannot be used to establish hudud because the sins requiring fixed punishments are known only by revelation or consensus, not by ijtihad. Therefore, the qiyas for liwat is invalid.
This is a principled objection grounded in Hanafi usul.
3
Find the Respondent's Answer (Jawab)
The respondent (the original claimant) replies that establishing hudud by qiyas is permissible according to his own school, and then points out that the Hanafis themselves have contradicted their principle. They used qiyas to establish the deterrent punishment for highway robbers (qat' al-tariq) by analogy with the sharing of spoils (ghanima), which also involves a fixed punishment.
This is a rebuttal by contradiction (mu'arada bi al-naqd): showing the opponent's own practice violates their stated rule.
4
Analyze the Type of Response
The respondent does not directly argue that qiyas for hudud is valid in itself; instead, he exposes an inconsistency. Under jadal rules, if the opponent cannot explain away the inconsistency, this counts as a winning move.
The burden now shifts to the Hanafi to either abandon their objection or explain why highway robbery is different.
5
Evaluate the Strength of the Rebuttal
The rebuttal is dialectically strong because it uses the opponent's own practice against them. It does not prove the permissibility of qiyas, but it undermines the consistency of the Hanafi position. The outcome depends on whether the Hanafi can provide a principled distinction between the two cases.
In jadal, consistency (itibār) is a key virtue; inconsistency (tanāquḍ) is a flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب السادس : الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات الحدود والخلاف في هذه المسألة أيضًا، كالخلاف في التي قبلها، فقد ذهب جمهور الأصوليين إلى جواز الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات الحدود، وخالف الحنفية فقالوا بعدم جوازه . وهو ما يدعو أيضًا إلى اعتراضهم على من يستدل بالقياس في إثبات الحدود. وذلك مثل استدلال المالكي أو الشافعي في إيجاب الحد في اللواط قياسًا على الزنا . فيعترض الحنفي بأن هذا إيجاب حد بالقياس، وذلك لا يجوز، لأن المعصية التي يحتاج في الزجر عنها إلى الحد لا تعلم بالاجتهاد وإنما تعلم بالتوقيف أو الاتفاق، فلا يجوز إثباتها بالقياس . والجواب أن يقول المستدل : إن إثبات الحدود بالقياس عندنا جائز، ثم إنكم ناقضتم في هذا إذ أوجبتم الحد على ردع قطاع الطريق قياسًا على الردع في الغنيمة، فبطل ما اعترضتم به
The sixth section: Objection to using qiyas in establishing fixed punishments. The dispute in this matter is also like the dispute in the previous one. The majority of legal theorists held the permissibility of using qiyas in establishing fixed punishments, but the Hanafis opposed this, saying it is not permissible. This also leads to their objection against those who use qiyas to establish fixed punishments. An example is when a Maliki or Shafi'i argues that the punishment for sodomy (liwat) is obligatory by qiyas on zina. The Hanafi objects that this is establishing a fixed punishment by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because the sin that requires a deterrent fixed punishment is not known by ijtihad but only by revelation or consensus, so it is not permissible to establish it by qiyas. The answer is that the arguer says: Establishing fixed punishments by qiyas is permissible according to us. Moreover, you contradicted yourselves in this, for you made the deterrent for highway robbers obligatory by qiyas on the sharing of spoils of war, so your objection is invalid.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage continues the pattern: the dispute over using qiyas to establish fixed punishments (hudud). The majority of usul scholars permit it, while Hanafis prohibit it. The example is extending the punishment for zina (adultery/fornication) to liwat (sodomy) by qiyas. The Hanafi objects that the sins requiring hudud are determined only by revelation or consensus, not by ijtihad. The response again points out inconsistency: Hanafis themselves used qiyas to establish the deterrent punishment for highway robbers (qat' al-tariq) by analogy with the distribution of spoils (ghanima), which involves a fixed punishment. Thus their objection is self-contradictory.

لواط

Key Takeaway
Inconsistent application of a principle can be used as a powerful dialectical counter even if the principle itself is not logically refuted.

Using Qiyas for Estimated Quantities: Hanafi Objection and Shafi'i Refutation
Hanafi objection to age estimation by qiyas and Shafi'i response via naqd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dialectical contradiction (naqd) in a legal debate by spotting inconsistency in applying qiyas to muqaddarat.

Core Idea
Consistency in legal method is tested by whether you apply qiyas equally to all cases.

In usul al-fiqh debates, one powerful tactic is to expose a contradiction (naqd) in an opponent's position. Here, the Hanafi argues that estimated quantities (muqaddarat) cannot be established by qiyas, only by tawqif or ittifaq. The Shafi'i responds by pointing out that the Hanafis themselves use qiyas for other estimates, such as the size of a hole in leather socks and the number of rak'ats for Friday prayer. This refutation relies on exposing inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim of the objection
The Hanafi claims that estimated quantities (muqaddarat) cannot be established by qiyas, only by tawqif or ittifaq.
This is the principle being challenged.
2
Locate the Shafi'i's counter-evidence
The Shafi'i lists two examples where Hanafis themselves use qiyas: the hole in khuff (three fingers) and the number of rak'ats for Jumu'ah (four).
These are legal rulings from Hanafi fiqh.
3
Test for contradiction (naqd)
If the Hanafi principle were true, the Hanafis should not have used qiyas in those examples. Since they did, their objection is inconsistent.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Assess the force of the naqd
The naqd shows that the Hanafi's own school practice violates his stated principle, so his objection is not valid unless he can explain why those cases are different.
The burden shifts back to the Hanafi.

فيها ببلوغ الجارية، فيحكم فيها ببلوغ الغلام كالثامنة عشرة، فيقول الحنفي : هذا إثبات مقدر بالقياس، وذلك لا يجوز لأن تعلق الحكم بقدر دون قدر طريقه التوقيف أو الاتفاق لا الاجتهاد أو القياس والجواب للشافعي أن يقول : إن إثبات المقدرات عندنا جائز بالقياس ، ثم إن الحنفية يناقضون أنفسهم في هذا إذ قدروا الخرق في الخف بثلاثة أصابع قياسًا على المسح ؛ كما قدروا العدد في الجمعة بأربعة بالقياس، وليس في ذلك شيء من التوقيف أو الاتفاق
In it regarding the reaching of puberty of the female, so he judges in it the reaching of puberty of the male like eighteen years, so the Hanafi says: This is an establishment of an estimate by qiyas, and that is not permissible because the attachment of the ruling to a particular quantity without another is based on explicit stipulation or consensus, not ijtihad or qiyas. And the answer for the Shafi'i is to say: Indeed, establishment of estimates is permissible by qiyas in our view. Then the Hanafiyya contradict themselves in this, for they estimated the hole in the khuff by three fingers by analogy to wiping, just as they estimated the number for Friday prayer as four by qiyas, and there is no explicit stipulation or consensus in that.

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange (jadal) between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i over the use of qiyas (analogical reasoning) to determine specific ages (muqaddarat) for legal rulings, such as the age of majority. The Hanafi argues that such estimations can only be based on tawqif (explicit textual stipulation) or ittifaq (consensus), not on ijtihad or qiyas. The Shafi'i responds by asserting that establishing estimates by qiyas is permissible in his school, and then points out a contradiction in the Hanafi position: the Hanafis themselves used qiyas to determine the size of a hole in leather socks (three fingers) by analogy to wiping (mash), and the number of rak'ats for Friday prayer (four) by analogy, without any explicit text or consensus. This refutation (naqd) demonstrates that the Hanafi objection is inconsistent.

Hanafi Objection to Kaffarah by Qiyas and Shafi'i Response
Hanafi objection to qiyas for kaffarat and Shafi'i response with naqd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi objection to using qiyas for kaffarat and reconstruct the Shafi'i counter-argument using naqd.

Core Idea
Hanafis reject qiyas for kaffarat but are caught using it inconsistently.

This passage presents a classic usul al-fiqh debate: can expiation penalties (kaffarat) be established by analogical reasoning (qiyas)? The Hanafis say no, because kaffarat are tawqifiyya (stipulated). The Shafi'is respond by pointing out that Hanafis themselves apply qiyas to the case of breaking fast by eating, contradicting their own principle. Understanding this dialectical exchange sharpens your skill in identifying inconsistencies (naqd) in legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested rule
The debate centres on whether kaffarah (expiation) can be established through qiyas (analogy). The majority says yes; Hanafis say no. Locate where this disagreement is stated in the passage.
The passage begins by stating the majority view and Hanafi opposition.
2
Extract the Hanafi objection
Find the Hanafi argument: 'This is establishing an expiation by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because expiation is intended to cover sins, and the amount that covers sins is not known by ijtihad.' This objection appeals to the tawqifi (stipulative) nature of kaffarat.
The Hanafi objection is a principled one: kaffarat amounts are not subject to ijtihad.
3
Find the Shafi'i response
The Shafi'i first affirms their own school's permissibility, then launches a naqd (contradiction) against the Hanafis: they point out that Hanafis themselves established kaffarah for breaking the fast by eating, by analogy to intercourse, despite lacking explicit text or consensus for that specific amount.
The naqd uses a counterexample from Hanafi fiqh itself.
4
Assess the naqd's strength
Evaluate whether the Shafi'i counterexample successfully shows inconsistency. Does the Hanafi position have a way to distinguish the two cases? The naqd seems to hit the principle: if qiyas is impermissible in kaffarat, why did Hanafis use it for eating in Ramadan?
The naqd is a powerful dialectical move because it exposes a contradiction within the opponent's practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب السابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات الكفارات. وهذه المسألة كذلك الخلاف فيها كالخلاف في سابقتيها، حيث يذهب جمهور الأصوليين إلى جواز إثبات الكفارات بالقياس ويعارض الحنفية ذلك، ذاهبين إلى عدم الجواز ومعترضين على هذا النوع من الاستدلال . وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن من حفر بئرا في طريق المسلمين، فوقع فيها رجل فمات وجبت عليه الكفارة، بأن هذا قتل يجب به دية كاملة، فوجبت به الكفارة كمن باشر القتل الخطأ. أو أن يستدل الشافعي في إيجاب الكفارة في القتل العمد بالقياس على إيجابها في القتل الخطأ. فيعترض الحنفي قائلا : هذا إثبات كفارة بالقياس، وذلك لا يجوز، فإن الكفارة تراد لتغطية المآثم، ومقدار ما يغطي المآثم لا يعلم بالاجتهاد، وإنما يثبت بتوقيف أو اتفاق . والجواب أن يقول المستدل عندنا يجوز إثبات الكفارات بالقياس، ثم إن الأحناف ناقضوا أصلهم هذا بإثبات الكفارة على من أفطر بالأكل في رمضان، قياسًا على من أفطر بالجماع مع أنه ليس في ذلك توقيف أو اتفاق
The Seventh Demand: Objection to using qiyas in establishing expiations. This issue also has disagreement like the two preceding ones. The majority of usuliyyun go to the permissibility of establishing expiations by qiyas, while the Hanafiyya oppose that, going to the impermissibility and objecting to this type of inference. For example, the Maliki argues that whoever digs a well in the Muslims' path, and a man falls into it and dies, then expiation is obligatory upon him, because this is a killing that requires full blood money, so expiation is obligatory by analogy to one who directly commits accidental killing. Or the Shafi'i argues for making expiation obligatory in intentional killing by analogy to accidental killing. The Hanafi objects saying: This is establishing an expiation by qiyas, and that is not permissible, because expiation is intended to cover sins, and the amount that covers sins is not known by ijtihad, but is established by explicit stipulation or consensus. The answer is that the proponent says: In our view, establishing expiations by qiyas is permissible. Moreover, the Hanafis contradicted their own principle by establishing expiation for one who breaks his fast by eating in Ramadan by analogy to one who breaks his fast by intercourse, although there is no explicit stipulation or consensus in that.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage treats the dialectical issue of using qiyas to establish kaffarat (expiation penalties). The majority opinion permits it, but Hanafis reject it. Two examples are given: the Maliki argument that digging a well causing death obligates kaffarah by analogy to direct accidental killing, and the Shafi'i argument for extending kaffarah from accidental killing to intentional killing. The Hanafi objection is that kaffarah covers sins and its amount cannot be known by ijtihad, only by tawqif or ittifaq. The response (jawab) first affirms the permissibility of qiyas in kaffarat for the proponent's school, and then points out a contradiction: the Hanafis themselves used qiyas to obligate kaffarah for breaking the fast by eating, by analogy to breaking it by intercourse, despite having no explicit text or consensus on that specific (the amount of kaffarah for eating). This refutation (naqd) highlights the inconsistency of the Hanafi position.

Objection to Qiyas for General Principles
Hanafi and Mu'tazili objection to qiyas for general principles with musaqat example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a jadal objection to using qiyas for general principles and reconstruct a likely response based on usul principles.

Core Idea
The dispute over qiyas for general principles is a classic jadal collision of usul methodology.

This passage presents a fundamental dialectical divide: can qiyas establish general legal categories, or only particular rulings? The Hanafi and Mu'tazili position restricts qiyas to particulars, while the majority (including Malikis and Shafi'is) permit it for general principles. The example of musaqat analogized to mudaraba serves as the battlefield. We'll dissect the objection and reconstruct the missing response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Note that the majority of usuliyyun permit establishing jumal al-usul by qiyas, while some Hanafis and Mu'tazila restrict qiyas to tafasil only.
This is a methodological dispute, not a substantive one.
2
Find the proponent's argument
The Maliki or Shafi'i argues for musaqat's permissibility by analogy to mudaraba, citing that palm trees are wealth that grows by work, and if lease for benefit is not permitted... (text cuts off). The core analogy: both musaqat and mudaraba involve capital (trees/capital) and labor with shared return.
The 'illa might be the productive partnership (sharika) based on work and capital.
3
Analyze the objector's likely challenge
The objector would argue that musaqat's permissibility is a general principle (the contract type itself) and cannot be established by qiyas; it requires a text or consensus. They would challenge the analogy by questioning the 'illa or by arguing that the general principle is already established by other means.
This mirrors the typical jadal move: attacking the validity of qiyas in this context.
4
Reconstruct the proponent's response
The proponent would defend the analogy by showing the 'illa is present and that qiyas for general principles is valid when the 'illa is clear and the principle is not explicitly in a text. They might also point out that the objector uses qiyas for details inconsistently.
This reconstructs the missing part of the passage based on standard usul arguments.
5
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
Assess which party has the stronger argument by the rules of jadal: the proponent must show the analogy is sound; the objector must show a genuine difference that breaks the analogy or a prohibition on qiyas for such principles.
The passage's incompleteness leaves the outcome unresolved, but the logical structure is clear.

Objection to Using Qiyas for General Principles: A Dialectical Exchange
Dialectical exchange on qiyas for general principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Hanafi objection against using qiyas for general principles and evaluate the response using the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
In jadal, an objection is only valid if consistent; inconsistency in the objector's own practice invalidates the objection.

This passage presents a classic dialectical exchange in usul al-fiqh. The majority of usul scholars permit using qiyas to establish general principles (jumal al-usul). However, some Hanafis and Mu'tazilites object, restricting qiyas to details. The argument plays out with a concrete example: the validity of musaqah (irrigation contract) by analogy to mudarabah. The response reveals a potential inconsistency in the Hanafi position, which is the crux of the jadal critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central dispute
Determine the exact point of disagreement: Is it permissible to use qiyas to establish general principles of law, or only details?
The Hanafis and Mu'tazilites argue it is not permissible for general principles.
2
Extract the Hanafi objection
Find the specific argument the Hanafi uses to object. He compares establishing a general principle by qiyas to establishing a sixth daily prayer or fasting a non-Ramadan month, which are universally rejected.
The objection is that if qiyas could establish general principles, it could invent new religious obligations, but consensus and text prevent that.
3
Extract the respondent's reply
The majority responds by distinguishing between cases prohibited by consensus/text (like a sixth prayer) and cases where qiyas is valid. They also point out that the Hanafis themselves used qiyas to establish the obligation of witr prayer, which is a general principle.
This is a key move: showing inconsistency in the objector's position.
4
Evaluate the outcome by jadal rules
Assess which side has the stronger argument. The Hanafi objection is undermined because their own practice contradicts their claimed principle.
In jadal, consistency is a core requirement for a valid objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثامن: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات جمل الأصول

مذهب جمهور الأصوليين جواز إثبات جمل الأصول بالقياس، وقد خالف في ذلك بعض الحنفية وبعض المعتزلة، فقالوا: لا يجوز إثبات حمل الأحكام بالقياس وإنما يجوز إثبات التفاصيل ولذلك من الطبيعي أن الاستدلال بالقياس في إثبات جمل الأصول ممن يرون جوازه سيثير الاعتراض عليه من قبل الذين يرون عدم جوازه. وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي في إثبات جواز المساقاة قياسًا على المضاربة، أو بأن النخل مال يزكو بالعمل، فإذا لم تجز إجارته لمنفعته المقصودة، جاز أن يعامل عليه ببعض الثمار الخارج منه، قياسًا على الدراهم والدنانير. فيقول الحنفي معترضا: هذا إثبات جملة أصل بالقياس، وهو لا يجوز، كما لا يجوز إثبات صلاة سادسة بالقياس ولا إثبات صوم شهر غير رمضان بالقياس، فيقول المستدل: يجوز عندنا إثبات الأصول والجمل بالقياس، كما يجوز إثبات تفاصيلها، وأما إثبات صلاة سادسة أو إثبات صوم شهر غير رمضان بالقياس، فإنما منع منه الإجماع والنص، ولولا ذلك لجاز إثباتهما بالقياس الصحيح إن وجد. ثم إن الحنفية قد ناقضوا أصلهم الذي اعترضوا به، فاثبتوا وجوب الوتر بالقياس، وهو أصل، فسقط ما اعترضوا به.
The Eighth Topic: Objection to Using Analogical Reasoning to Establish General Principles of the [Legal] Sources. The position of the majority of usul scholars is the permissibility of establishing general principles by qiyas, and some Hanafis and some Mu'tazilites opposed that, saying: It is not permissible to establish general legal rulings by qiyas, but only details. So naturally, using qiyas to establish general principles by those who permit it will provoke objection from those who consider it impermissible. For example, a Maliki or Shafi'i argues for the permissibility of musaqah (irrigation contract) by analogy to mudarabah (profit-sharing partnership), or that palm trees are wealth that increases with work, so if renting them for their intended benefit is not permissible, then it is permissible to contract for some of its produce, by analogy to dirhams and dinars. The Hanafi objects, saying: This is establishing a general principle by qiyas, and it is not permissible, just as it is not permissible to establish a sixth prayer by qiyas, nor to establish fasting a month other than Ramadan by qiyas. The arguer says: According to us, it is permissible to establish general principles and their details by qiyas. As for establishing a sixth prayer or fasting a month other than Ramadan by qiyas, that is prevented by consensus and textual evidence; otherwise, it would be permissible to establish them by valid qiyas if found. Then the Hanafis have contradicted their own principle that they objected with, for they established the obligation of witr by qiyas, which is a general principle; thus their objection is invalidated.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical theme. It discusses whether qiyas can be used to establish general principles (jumal al-usul) as opposed to merely details. The majority allow it; some Hanafis and Mu'tazilites restrict qiyas to details. An example: the permissibility of musaqah (a contract for watering trees in exchange for a share of fruit) is argued by analogy to mudarabah. The Hanafi objects by comparing to the impossibility of establishing a sixth daily prayer or fasting a non-Ramadan month by qiyas. The respondent argues that the prohibition in those cases is due to consensus and text, not a general bar against qiyas for principles. Furthermore, the Hanafis themselves have contradicted their stance by establishing the obligation of witr prayer (which is a general principle) by qiyas, thereby undermining their own objection.

Objection to proving a general principle by qiyas and its refutation
A dialectical exchange on the validity of using qiyas to establish general principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange between a Hanafi objector and a proponent on the validity of using qiyas for general principles, and identify the self-contradiction in the objection.

Core Idea
Qiyas for principles is valid unless blocked by text or consensus; self-contradiction defeats an objection.

In this passage, we see a classic jadal exchange over whether analogical reasoning (qiyas) can establish a general legal principle (asl). The Hanafi objector claims it is invalid, citing examples like a sixth prayer or extra Ramadan fast. The proponent counters that qiyas is valid for principles and that the cited examples are prohibited only by text/consensus, not a general ban. The refutation then exposes a self-contradiction: the Hanafis themselves used qiyas to prove the obligation of witr, which is a principle. This lesson will guide you through the structure of this dialectical argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Find the Hanafi mu'tarid's assertion: that establishing a general principle (asl) by qiyas is not allowed, likened to impossible cases like a sixth prayer or extra Ramadan fast.
The objector uses analogy to support his objection, ironically.
2
Extract the proponent's response (jawab)
The mustadill argues that qiyas for principles is permissible, just as for details, and the given exceptions are blocked by specific evidence (text/consensus), not by a rule against qiyas for principles.
The proponent distinguishes between a general ban and specific prohibitions.
3
Analyze the naqd (refutation by contradiction)
The proponent points out that the Hanafis themselves proved the obligation of witr prayer by qiyas, which is a general principle. This contradicts their own objection.
The contradiction is between their stated principle and their actual practice.
4
Evaluate the outcome
Because the objectors violated their own rule, their objection is invalid (sāqit). The proponent's position remains unchallenged.
In jadal rules, a self-contradiction defeats the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جاز أن يعامل عليه ببعض الثمار الخارج منه، قياسًا على الدراهم والدنانير. فيقول الحنفي معترضا: هذا إثبات جملة أصل بالقياس، وهو لا يجوز، كما لا يجوز إثبات صلاة سادسة بالقياس ولا إثبات صوم شهر غير رمضان بالقياس. فيقول المستدل: يجوز عندنا إثبات الأصول والجمل بالقياس، كما يجوز إثبات تفاصيلها، وأما إثبات صلاة سادسة أو إثبات صوم شهر غير رمضان بالقياس، فإنما منع منه الإجماع والنص، ولولا ذلك لجاز إثباتهما بالقياس الصحيح إن وجد. ثم إن الحنفية قد ناقضوا أصلهم الذي اعترضوا به، فأثبتوا وجوب الوتر بالقياس، وهو أصل، فسقط ما اعترضوا به
It is permissible to deal with it by giving some of the fruits produced from it, by analogy to dirhams and dinars. The Hanafi says objecting: This is establishing a general principle by analogy, which is not allowed, just as it is not allowed to establish a sixth prayer by analogy nor to establish fasting a month other than Ramadan by analogy. The proponent says: It is permissible in our view to establish principles and general rules by analogy, just as it is permissible to establish their details. As for establishing a sixth prayer or establishing fasting a month other than Ramadan by analogy, they are only prevented by consensus and the [revealed] text, and were it not for that, it would be permissible to establish them by sound analogy if found. Then the Hanafis have contradicted their own principle with which they objected, so they established the obligation of witr by analogy, which is a principle, so their objection fell.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage presents a typical dialectical exchange (jadal) regarding the validity of using qiyas (analogical reasoning) to establish a general principle (asl). The objector, a Hanafi, argues that proving a general rule by qiyas is invalid, citing examples like establishing a sixth prayer or fasting in a non-Ramadan month, which are prohibited by consensus and text. The respondent (the proponent) counters that qiyas is valid for principles, just as for details; the specific prohibitions are due to consensus/text, not a general ban on qiyas for principles. Moreover, the respondent points out that the objector's own school (Hanafis) have contradicted their claim by proving the obligation of witr prayer through qiyas, which is a principle, thus undermining their objection. The example used is from fiqh: dealing with fruit trees by giving some produce, analogized to currency. All examples are from Islamic legal reasoning.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning in legal theory, extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause.
أصل (asl): A foundational principle or general rule, also the original case in an analogy. [Note: In jadal, asl often refers to the original case in qiyas; in usul al-fiqh, it can also mean the primary source.]
مستدل (mustadill): The proponent who presents the argument (qiyas) and defends its validity.
معترض (mu'tarid): The objector who raises a challenge against the argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the Hanafis' objection fall apart?
A) Because they unknowingly used a false analogy.
B) Because they themselves proved the obligation of witr by qiyas, contradicting their principle.
C) Because the proponent showed that qiyas on principles is always invalid.
D) Because the example of fruits was flawed.

Key Takeaway
A dialectical objection fails if it is self-contradictory; here, the Hanafis' own use of qiyas for principles undermined their objection.
