The objection of abrogation in qiyas and its refutation
The author defines the objection of abrogation with a fiqh example and the respondent's method of defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection that the original case (asl) is abrogated in a dialectical exchange and reconstruct the respondent's defense by distinguishing between the abrogation of the ruling and the essence of the act.

Core Idea
Abrogation of an obligation does not nullify the act itself for analogical reasoning.

In the dialectical tradition of usul al-fiqh, every step of qiyas can be challenged. One powerful objection is that the original case (asl) relied upon by the proponent has been abrogated (mansukh). This passage presents a classic example from the debate between Hanafis and Malikis on the validity of fasting Ramadan with intention before noon. By understanding how the Hanafi defends his asl, you will grasp a subtle but critical distinction: abrogation may target the ruling (hukm), not the essence of the act itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (mu'tarid)
Find the part where the Maliki claims that the asl is abrogated. Note the exact claim: the fast of Ashura is abrogated, so it cannot be an asl.
2
Extract the distinction made by the Hanafi (mudda'i)
The Hanafi does not deny that Ashura fast was abrogated; instead, he distinguishes between the abrogation of the obligation (wujub) and the persistence of the act's essence (asl al-sawm). What does this mean?
3
Apply the distinction to the qiyas
Since the act itself (the fast) remains valid in essence, the analogy based on the act's nature (fast tied to a specific time) is unaffected. The obligation being abrogated only means the act is no longer required, but its structure for qiyas remains.
4
Formulate a rule for responding to this objection
When an objector claims the asl is abrogated, check whether the abrogation is of the ruling (hukm) or of the underlying act. If only the ruling, the asl may still be valid for qiyas if the relevant attribute ('illa) persists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - أن يدعي المعترض أن أصل المستدل منسوخ: ومثاله: أن يستدل الحنفي في جواز صوم رمضان بنية قبل الزوال، بأنه صوم متعلق بزمان معين فصح بنية من النهار، كصوم عاشوراء. فيقول المالكي معترضًا: هذا قياس على أصل منسوخ، فإن صوم عاشوراء قد نسخ، فلا يجوز أن يصير أصلا للأحكام الثابتة. وطريق الحنفي في الجواب أن يبين أن أصله لم ينسخ، وإنما نسخ وجوبه، وأصل الصوم باق، فصح القياس عليه
a) That the objector claims that the proponent's original case is abrogated: Example: The Hanafi argues for the permissibility of fasting Ramadan with an intention before noon, saying it is a fast tied to a specific time, so it is valid with an intention from the daytime, like the fast of Ashura. The Maliki says objecting: This is an analogy on an abrogated original case, for the fast of Ashura has been abrogated, so it cannot become an original case for established rulings. The Hanafi's method in reply is to show that his original case was not abrogated; rather, its obligation was abrogated, while the essence of the fast remains, so the analogy upon it is sound.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a classification of objections in jadal, specifically the first type of objection to the asl (original case) in qiyas: that the asl is abrogated (mansukh). The author provides a concrete example from fiqh: a Hanafi uses the fast of Ashura as an asl to prove that intention before noon is valid for Ramadan fasts. The Maliki objects that Ashura fast is abrogated, so it cannot serve as an asl. The Hanafi's response shows that only the obligation of Ashura was abrogated, not the fast itself as a valid act of worship; thus the essence of the fast remains, making qiyas permissible. This highlights a nuanced understanding of abrogation: partial abrogation (of obligation) does not nullify the underlying act's validity for analogical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical objections, an asl is not fully invalidated by abrogation if only its ruling was removed, not the underlying act.

Objection that the 'illa of the Original is Unknowable
Objection that the 'illa of the asl is unknowable with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the dialectical objection that the 'illa of the original is unknowable, and evaluate its impact on the validity of qiyas.

Core Idea
If the 'illa of the original is known only through an unrepeatable event, qiyas becomes epistemologically impossible.

In jadal, one powerful objection is to argue that the effective cause ('illa) of the original case cannot be known, making analogical extension invalid. This passage presents a concrete example from a Shafi'i-Maliki debate on ihram and death. By analyzing this objection, you will learn how epistemological limits on 'illa knowledge can block qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties and their positions
The Shafi'i argues that ihram is not broken by death, using qiyas to a historical case. The Maliki objects that qiyas on that case is invalid. Note the specific ruling and the original case.
The Shafi'i's qiyas: ihram (far') is like ihram of the man whose camel broke his neck (asl), so the ruling of continuity applies.
2
Extract the central objection
The Maliki's objection is that the 'illa of the original cannot be known. The 'illa, according to him, is that the man will be raised saying Labbayk, which is known only through that specific prophetic event.
The 'illa is tied to a unique, unrepeatable occurrence.
3
Analyze the epistemological basis
The objection is not about the validity of the 'illa itself, but about its knowability. Because the knowledge of this 'illa depends on a prophetic era revelation that has ended, it cannot be used for qiyas to new cases.
This shows that qiyas requires the 'illa to be accessible and generalizable, not tied to a singular historical moment.
4
Consider the implications for the debate
If the Maliki's objection holds, the Shafi'i's qiyas fails. This illustrates how dialectical objections target the foundations of qiyas, not just the analogy itself.
The text does not give the Shafi'i's response, but one could argue the 'illa is broader (e.g., ihram continues until burial) derived from other texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - أن يدعي المعترض أن علة الأصل لا يمكن أن تعلم: وذلك مثل أن يستدل الشافعي على أن الإحرام لا ينقطع بالموت، بأنه إحرام فلا ينقطع بالموت، كإحرام الرجل الذي وقصت به ناقته على عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم. فيقول المالكي معترضا: لا يصح القياس على هذا الأصل، لأن معنى القياس أن يشترك الفرع والأصل في علة الحكم المعلق عليهما، والعلة في الأصل أنه يبعث يوم القيامة ملبيا ولا طريق إلى معرفة ذلك إلا في زمان النبوة، وهذا قد انقطع، فبطل القياس على هذا الأصل
b) That the objector claims that the 'illa (effective cause) of the original cannot be known: Such as when the Shafi'i argues that ihram is not broken by death, because it is ihram so it is not broken by death, like the ihram of the man whose she-camel broke his neck during the time of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him). The Maliki says objecting: Analogy upon this original is not valid, because the meaning of analogy is that the branch and the original share the 'illa of the ruling attached to them, and the 'illa in the original is that he will be raised on the Day of Judgment saying 'Labbayk', and there is no way to know that except in the time of prophethood, and this has ceased, so analogy upon this original is invalid.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage describes the second type of objection to the asl: that the effective cause ('illa) of the original ruling cannot be known. The example is from a Shafi'i argument that ihram (the state of pilgrimage consecration) is not broken by death, analogizing to a specific historical case: a man whose camel crushed him during the Prophet's time, and the Prophet said he would be resurrected in ihram saying 'Labbayk'. The Maliki objects that the 'illa here is that the man will be raised as a muhrim, and this can only be known through revelation from that era; since prophethood has ended, we cannot confirm that 'illa applies to any new case. Therefore qiyas fails. The objection points to the epistemological problem: if the 'illa is tied to a unique, unrepeatable event, it cannot serve as a basis for analogical extension to other cases. The text does not provide the Shafi'i's response, but in practice, one might argue that the 'illa is not the specific prophetic statement but the general principle that ihram continues until burial or something similar, which can be derived from other texts.

إحرام
فرع

A) Because the case is abrogated.
B) Because the 'illa of the original is known only through the time of prophethood, which has ended.
C) Because the Shafi'i used a weak hadith.
D) Because qiyas is not allowed in matters of worship.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the objection that the 'illa of the asl is unknowable targets the epistemological basis of qiyas and can invalidate an analogy if the 'illa is tied to an unrepeatable event.

Objection: The Original Is Abrogated
Objection of abrogation with Hanafi-Maliki example and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection to qiyas is based on abrogation of the original and construct a valid response by distinguishing partial abrogation.

Core Idea
An objection of abrogation fails if only a part of the original's ruling is abrogated.

In dialectical debates (jadal) within usul al-fiqh, one common objection to analogical reasoning is that the original relied upon has been abrogated. This passage illustrates a classic debate between a Hanafi and a Maliki over fasting Ramadan. We will dissect the objection and the Hanafi's precise response, learning how to defend analogies against claims that the precedent is no longer applicable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original and the analogy
The Hanafi argues that fasting Ramadan with an intention before noon is valid because it resembles the fast of Ashura, which is a fast tied to a specific time and valid with a daytime intention. The original (asl) is the fast of Ashura, and the branch (far') is the fast of Ramadan.
The 'illa (effective cause) is being a fast connected to a specific time.
2
Find the objector's claim
The Maliki objects that the original (Ashura) is abrogated (naskh), so it cannot serve as a valid precedent for current rulings. This challenges the first pillar of qiyas: the soundness of the original.
The objector does not deny the analogy itself but attacks the foundation.
3
Extract the respondent's distinction
The Hanafi's response distinguishes between the obligation of Ashura (which was abrogated) and the act of fasting itself (which remains a valid precedent). Only the obligation was removed, not the entire ruling structure.
This is a precise logical move: partial abrogation does not destroy the original's utility as a benchmark.
4
Evaluate the objection's validity
The objection is invalid because the original still exists in its essence; only one attribute (obligation) was abrogated. For qiyas, the relevant aspect is the permissibility of fasting with a daytime intention, which remains intact.
This illustrates the rule: an objection of naskh must show that the entire ruling being analogized is abrogated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الأول : الاعتراض عليه بأن ما جعل المستدل أصلا لا يجوز أن يكون أصلا أي أن يعترض السائل على المستدل بأن ما اعتبره أصلا في القياس لا يجوز اعتباره أصلا يقاس عليه وهذا يتوجه على القياس من وجهين : أ - أن يدعي المعترض أن أصل المستدل منسوخ : ومثاله : أن يستدل الحنفي في جواز صوم رمضان بنية قبل الزوال، بأنه صوم متعلق بزمان معين فصح بنية من النهار، كصوم عاشوراء . فيقول المالكي معترضًا هذا قياس على أصل منسوخ، فإن صوم عاشوراء قد نسخ، فلا يجوز أن يصير أصلا للأحكام الثابتة. وطريق الحنفي في الجواب أن يبين أن أصله لم ينسخ، وإنما نسخ وجوبه، وأصل الصوم باق، فصح القياس عليه (1).
The first demand: The objection that what the arguer made an original is not permissible to be an original. That is, the questioner objects to the arguer that what he considered an original in analogy is not permissible to be considered an original to be measured against. This applies to the analogy in two ways: A - That the objector claims that the arguer's original is abrogated. Its example: that the Hanafi argues for the permissibility of fasting Ramadan with an intention before noon, by saying it is a fast connected to a specific time, so it is valid with an intention from the daytime, like the fast of Ashura. Then the Maliki says objecting: this is an analogy upon an abrogated original, for the fast of Ashura has been abrogated, so it is not permissible for it to become an original for established rulings. The Hanafi's method in response is to show that his original was not abrogated, rather its obligation was abrogated, and the essence of fasting remains, so the analogy upon it is valid (1).
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents the first type of objection against qiyas (analogical reasoning) regarding the original (asl). The author classifies objections against the pillars of qiyas in general. Here, the objection is that the original used by the arguer is invalid because it has been abrogated (naskh). The example illustrates a debate between a Hanafi and a Maliki. The Hanafi argues that fasting Ramadan with an intention before noon is valid because it resembles the fast of Ashura, which is also a specific-time fast. The Maliki objects that Ashura fasting is abrogated, so it cannot serve as an original. The response from the Hanafi is to differentiate: the obligation of Ashura was abrogated, but the act of fasting itself remains a valid precedent. Thus the analogy is not on an abrogated original but on the remaining essence. This shows the importance of precision in identifying what is abrogated.

نسخ

D) The original's 'illa cannot be known.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the original is abrogated only invalidates qiyas if the entire ruling of the original is abrogated; partial abrogation (e.g., of obligation) does not remove the precedent's validity.

The Hukm Majhul Objection and Its Resolution via Context
Objection that the ruling is unknown in qiyas, with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'hukm majhul' objection in a jadal exchange and explain how dalalat al-hal resolves it.

Core Idea
An unknown ruling invalidates qiyas, but context can make it known and effective.

In this passage, we see a classical usuli objection: the ruling used in an analogy is said to be unknown, making the argument invalid. The Hanafi critic claims the Maliki's resemblance claim is ambiguous. The Maliki's defense relies on a powerful principle—contextual indication (dalalat al-hal) giving clarity. This lesson walks through the dialectic and the logical move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original qiyas claim
What is the Maliki trying to prove? He argues that removing impurity with vinegar is impermissible because it is a purification intended for prayer, resembling wudu, which cannot be done with vinegar.
2
Extract the Hanafi objection
The Hanafi charges that the ruling is unknown (majhul). It is not clear in what way the resemblance holds, so it is like an ambiguous expression (mujmal) and cannot be used in argument.
The objection targets the clarity of the hukm, not the analogy itself.
3
Analyze the Maliki's response
The Maliki invokes contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). Since the entire debate is about using vinegar, saying 'it resembles wudu' unmistakably means that vinegar is prohibited for impurity removal just as it is for wudu. Thus the ruling is known, and contextual understanding is equivalent to explicit speech.
The response distinguishes this from arbitrary ambiguous wording.
4
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
The Maliki's response effectively removes the ambiguity. According to the rules of jadal, the objection fails because the context supplies the needed specification. The ruling is no longer unknown.
The author also counters the procedural objection about discussing an unknown ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الاعتراض عليه بأن ما جعله حكمًا لا يجوز أن يكون حكمًا وذلك على وجهين : أ - الاعتراض على الحكم بكونه مجهولاً : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أنه لا يجوز إزالة النجاسة بالخل بأن هذه طهارة تراد للصلاة فأشبهت طهارة الحدث.. فيقول الحنفي : هذا حكم مجهول لم يبين في أي شيء شابه إزالة النجاسة في الوضوء، فيلحق هذا بالمجمل، فلا يصح العمل به، وأيضًا فإن الكلام يختلف باختلاف الحكم، فلما لم يتبين لم يمكن الكلام عليه . وجواب المستدل أن يقول : إن هذا ليس بمجهول بل هو معلوم مبين، وذلك أن الخلاف إنما كان في إزالة النجاسة بالخل فإذا قال: فأشبه الوضوء، علم أنه أراد به أنه لا يجوز ذلك بالخل كما لا يجوز به الوضوء، فقد تبين المراد وعرف المقصود، وما عرف بدلالة الحال كان بمنزلة المنطوق به، ويخالف هذا المجمل من الألفاظ لأن ذلك لم يرد على حالة معقولة فيحمل عليها، وهذا ورد على حالة معقولة وحكم معروف، فرجع الإطلاق إليه .. وأما قولهم : إن الكلام يختلف باختلاف الحكم، فلا يمكنه أن يتكلم عليه قبل البيان . . فلا ، لأنه يفصل عليه، فيتكلم على ما يحتمله من الوجوه (1).
The second demand: The objection that what he made a ruling is not permissible to be a ruling. This is in two ways: A - Objection to the ruling being unknown. For example: the Maliki argues that it is not permissible to remove impurity with vinegar because it is a purification intended for prayer, so it resembles purification from minor ritual impurity (hadath). Then the Hanafi says: this is an unknown ruling; it has not been clarified in what way it resembles the removal of impurity in wudu; so it is attached to the ambiguous (mujmal), so acting upon it is invalid. Also, speech differs depending on the ruling, and since it was not clarified, it cannot be discussed. The arguer's response is to say: This is not unknown; rather it is known and clear. That is because the disagreement was only about removing impurity with vinegar. When he said 'it resembles wudu', it is understood that he meant that it is not permissible with vinegar just as wudu is not permissible with it. So the intent is clear and the purpose is known. What is known through contextual indication is like that which is explicitly stated. This differs from ambiguous expressions because the latter were not presented in a rational context to be interpreted accordingly, whereas this was presented in a rational context and a known ruling, so the absolute is referred back to it. As for their saying that speech differs depending on the ruling, and he cannot speak on it before clarification, it is not so, because he details it and speaks on the various possibilities (1).
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage begins the second matlab, concerning objections against the ruling (hukm) in qiyas. The first sub-type is when the ruling is claimed to be unknown (majhul). The example involves a Maliki arguing that removing impurity with vinegar is impermissible because it is a purification for prayer similar to wudu. The Hanafi objects that the ruling is ambiguous: it is not clear in what way it resembles wudu (e.g., in the manner of removal or in the prohibition). Thus it is mujmal (ambiguous) and cannot be used. The Maliki's response is that the ruling is known from the context (dala alat al-hal): since the disagreement is specifically about using vinegar, saying 'it resembles wudu' clearly implies that it is not allowed with vinegar, just as wudu cannot be done with vinegar. This contextual understanding is equivalent to explicit speech. The author also addresses the objection that one cannot discuss an ambiguous ruling: the arguer can detail the ruling and discuss its possible aspects. This passage highlights the importance of clarity in the ruling for qiyas to be valid.

Key Terms
حكم مجهول
مجمل
دلالة الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Maliki arguer respond to the Hanafi's objection that the ruling is unknown?
A) He admits the ruling is unknown and asks for clarification.
B) He argues that the context clarifies the ruling, and it is like explicit speech.
C) He changes his original argument.
D) He claims the Hanafi is not qualified to object.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an objection that a ruling is unknown can be countered by showing that contextual indication makes the ruling explicit, thus preserving the qiyas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Take a similar scenario: The Shafi'i argues that combining prayers during rain is permissible because it resembles travel (which permits combining). A critic objects that the resemblance is unknown. Construct the objection and the response using dalalat al-hal based on the context of rain being a hardship.
?
Task 2: Identify the conditions under which dalalat al-hal can be used to clarify a ruling, referring to the conditions mentioned in the passage (e.g., the context must be rational and the ruling known).

Objection of Lack of Equivalence in Qiyas
Author presents objection that the ruling in branch and original are not equivalent, with example from sale of unseen goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the objection of lack of equivalence (عدم التساوي) in an analogical argument (qiyas) from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
An analogy fails if the ruling in branch and original are not equivalent — a single analogy cannot yield two opposite rulings.

This passage presents a specific objection (i'tirad) in jadal: the mu'tarid claims that the ruling in the branch and original are not equivalent ('adam al-tasawi), making the qiyas invalid. The example involves the sale of absent goods (al-a'yan al-gha'iba) without description. Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to critique analogies used in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's analogy
The Maliki argues that selling absent goods without description is permissible because it is analogous to a will (wasiyya), where lack of description and lack of specification are both treated similarly.
The original is the will (wasiyya), the branch is the sale of absent goods without description.
2
Spot the objector's challenge
The Hanafi points out that the analogy leads to contradictory rulings: either both lack of description and lack of specification are valid in the original, or both are invalid in the branch. They cannot be derived from each other.
The key is that the ruling in the original (wasiyya) permits both lack of description and lack of specification, but the branch might require invalidity for both.
3
Understand the logical flaw
An analogy must apply the same ruling uniformly to both sides; if the analogy yields two opposite rulings depending on how it is applied, it is unsound.
This is the fallacy of equivocation in the ruling (hukm).
4
Recognize the response framework
The Maliki responds that such reasoning is permissible within his school because it follows their principles. The text cuts off, but the response would typically clarify which ruling is intended.
The objection is not always fatal; it can be answered by showing that the ruling is indeed equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاعتراض على الحكم بعدم التساوي في الفرع والأصل: ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على أنه لا يجوز بيع الأعيان الغائبة من غير صفة، بأن هذا عقد يجوز جنسه في معين وغير معين، فوجب أن يستوي فيه عدم الصفة وعدم التعيين قياسا على الوصية. فيقول الحنفي : هذا غير صحيح، لأنك تريد التسوية بين عدم الصفة وعدم التعيين في الأصل في تصحيحهما، أو تريد التسوية بينهما في إبطالهما في الفرع، وهذان حكمان متضادان فلا يجوز أخذ أحدهما من الآخر. والجواب أن يقال: إن مثل هذا يجوز عندنا، لأنه من أصولنا .. ثم إن (1).
B - The objection to the ruling because of lack of equivalence between the branch and the original. Its example: that the Maliki argues that it is not permissible to sell absent goods without description, because it is a contract whose type is permissible in specified and unspecified, so it must be equal in it the lack of description and the lack of specification by analogy to a will. The Hanafi says: this is incorrect, because you either intend equality between the lack of description and lack of specification in the original in validating both, or you intend equality between them in invalidating both in the branch, and these are two contradictory rulings, so it is not permissible to derive one from the other. The response is said: That such is permissible according to us, because it is from our principles... then indeed (1).
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents the second sub-type of objection against the ruling: that the ruling is not equivalent (musawi) between branch and original. The example involves the sale of absent goods (al-a'yan al-gha'iba) without description. The Maliki argues that it is analogous to a will (wasiyya), where the lack of specification and description are both treated similarly. The Hanafi objects that the analogy leads to equivocation: either the Maliki wants to apply the ruling of validity (both lack of description and specification are valid) from the original to the branch, or the ruling of invalidity from the branch to the original. These are contradictory, so the analogy is invalid. The response mentions that according to the Maliki school, such reasoning is permissible because it follows their principles. However, the text cuts off. This objection highlights the need for the ruling to be applicable uniformly; if the analogy yields two opposite rulings, it is unsound.

Key Takeaway
An analogy in usul al-fiqh is invalid if it implies two opposite rulings for the same branch situation.

Answering the Objection of Non-Equality Between Root and Branch
Author presents objection on lack of equality and the answer using a Maliki-Hanafi debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and respond to the objection that the root and branch lack equality in an analogical argument from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Equality in the effective cause ('illa) suffices for analogy even if detailed rulings differ.

In jadal, one common objection against qiyas is that the root and branch are not equal in the relevant ruling. The Hanafi objection to the Maliki's analogy on selling absent items illustrates this. This lesson unpacks the objection and shows how it is answered by distinguishing between the 'illa and the detailed ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The mu'tarid (Hanafi) claims that the mudda'i (Maliki) intends equality between root and branch in a way that leads to contradictory rulings. Specifically, either both the absence of description and lack of specification are valid in the root, or both are invalid in the branch.
The objection is that the mudda'i is trying to derive two opposite conclusions from the same cause.
2
Extract the Mu'tarid's Reasoning
The Hanafi argues that if the Maliki wants equality in validation, then the branch should also validate; if equality in invalidation, then the branch should also invalidate. Since both cannot hold, the analogy fails.
This reasoning assumes that the cause (taswiya) directly determines the final ruling (validation/invalidation).
3
Locate the Mudda'i's Answer
The Maliki responds that the cause only establishes equality in the attribute (absence of description and lack of specification) in the root, not in the final ruling. The validation/invalidation is not part of the cause; it is a separate detail.
The answer relies on a principle of the Maliki school that allows combining root and branch as long as the cause is shared.
4
Distinguish Between 'Illa and Detailed Ruling
The key insight: if the root and branch are equal in the ruling dependent on the cause (i.e., the attribute itself), the analogy is valid even if the detailed rulings (like validation or invalidation) differ. The cause is only about the equality of the attribute, not the outcome.
This distinction guards against the fallacy of assuming that perfect identity of all details is required for analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاعتراض على الحكم بعدم التساوي في الفرع والأصل: ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على أنه لا يجوز بيع الأعيان الغائبة من غير صفة، بأن هذا عقد يجوز جنسه في معين وغير معين، فوجب أن يستوي فيه عدم الصفة وعدم التعيين قياسا على الوصية. فيقول الحنفي : هذا غير صحيح، لأنك تريد التسوية بين عدم الصفة التعيين في الأصل في تصحيحهما، أو تريد التسوية بينهما في إبطالهما في الفرع، وهذان حكمان متضادان فلا يجوز أخذ أحدهما من الآخر. والجواب أن يقال: إن مثل هذا يجوز عندنا، لأنه من أصولنا .. ثم إن التعليل في الأصل إنما هو للتسوية بين عدم الصفة وعدم التعيين في الأصل، وهذا المعنى موجود في الفرع ؛ وأما تصحيح العقد أو إبطاله فلم نعرض له في العلة، ومتى استوى الأصل والفرع في الحكم المعلق على العلة، صح الجمع وإن اختلفا في التفصيل
B - Objection that there is no equality between the branch and the root. Example: The Maliki argues that it is not permissible to sell absent items without description, because this is a contract whose genus is permissible for specified and unspecified items, so the absence of description and lack of specification must be equal, analogically to a will. The Hanafi says: This is incorrect, because you intend equality between absence of description and absence of specification in the root in validating both, or you intend equality between them in invalidating both in the branch—these are two contradictory rulings, so one cannot be derived from the other. The answer is to say: This is permissible according to us, because it is from our principles... Then, the reasoning in the root is only for equality between absence of description and absence of specification in the root, and this meaning exists in the branch. As for validating or invalidating the contract, we did not bring it into the cause. Whenever the root and branch are equal in the ruling that is dependent on the cause, the combination is valid even if they differ in detail.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of objection against the ruling (hukm): that there is no equality (tasawi) between the root (asl) and the branch (far'). The example involves selling absent items (a'yan gha'iba) without description. The Maliki argues that this is impermissible by analogy to a will (wasiyya), where both absence of description and lack of specification are treated equally. The Hanafi objects that the Maliki is trying to derive two contradictory rulings: either both are valid or both invalid, which is impossible. The answer explains that the reasoning ('illa) is only about the equality of the absence of description and lack of specification in the root, and this meaning is present in the branch. The actual validation or invalidation is not part of the cause; if the root and branch share the ruling linked to the cause, the analogy holds even if detailed rulings differ. This lesson guards against the misunderstanding that an analogy fails if the branch ruling has details different from the root; the key is the shared 'illa, not the exact outcome.

أصل
تسوية

C) The Hanafi objection is always correct.
D) The analogy is invalid because the rulings are contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Equality in the effective cause ('illa) between root and branch suffices for qiyas; differences in detailed rulings do not invalidate the analogy.

Objection to Consensus as a Cause in Jadal
Objection to consensus as a cause with Maliki-Shafi'i example and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a jadal exchange where an objector challenges the temporal priority of a cause and the respondent defends using qiyas al-dalala.

Core Idea
A cause in jadal need not be temporally prior; it can be a later indicator of a pre-existing ruling.

In this lesson, we examine a classic jadal objection: the claim that consensus (ijma') cannot serve as a cause ('illa) because it is temporally later than the ruling it is supposed to cause. Through a Maliki-Shafi'i debate, we see how the respondent reframes the cause as an indicator (dalil) rather than a temporal antecedent. This illustrates a key move in jadal: separating ontological causation from evidential indication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's position
What is the Maliki's claim? He argues that zakat is obligatory on the offspring of a sheep and a gazelle (when the mother is a sheep) because the animal is 'separated from an animal that requires zakat by consensus' (infisal min hayawan tajibu fihi al-zakat bi al-ijma'). The consensus is the cause ('illa) for zakat on the parent, and the offspring resembles the mixed grazing case.
The Maliki uses the principle that the ruling of the root (the sheep or similar animal) extends to the branch (hybrid) via analogy (qiyas).
2
Find the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i objects: 'This is incorrect, because consensus occurred after the death of the Prophet, while the obligation of zakat was established in the Prophet's era. The ruling cannot precede its cause.' The temporal order violates the principle that a cause must be prior to its effect.
This objection targets the very validity of the cause, not just its application.
3
Analyze the Maliki's response
The Maliki answers: (1) A cause can be later and still 'prove' the ruling (yuthbitu bihi al-hukm), just as consensus on a ruling may occur after the ruling and then reinforce it. (2) In qiyas al-dalala (indicative analogy), some branch rulings can serve as evidence for the original ruling, so temporal order is irrelevant.
The respondent distinguishes between causal connection (the 'illa must be prior to the malul) and evidential connection (the dalil can be later).
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
According to jadal rules, the Maliki's response successfully defends the position because he provides a reasoned distinction. The Shafi'i's objection is neutralized by showing that consensus can be a sign (dalil) even if subsequent.
This exchange illustrates a common move: reframe a causal argument as an indicative one to avoid temporal priority objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث : الاعتراض عليه بأن ما جعله علة لا يجوز أن يكون علة وذلك من وجهين أيضا : أ - الاعتراض على جعل الاتفاق علة : وذلك مثل : أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب الزكاة في المتولد بين الظباء والغنم إذا كانت الأمهات غنمًا، لأنه منفصل من حيوان تجب فيه الزكاة بالإجماع، فأشبه المتولد بين السائمة والمعلوفة. فيقول الشافعي معترضا : هذا غير صحيح، لأن الإجماع حدث بعد موت رسول الله ، ووجوب الزكاة عندكم ثبت في عهد رسول الله ، ولا يجوز أن يتقدم الحكم قبل العلة. والجواب بالنسبة للمستدل المالكي أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون متأخرا ويثبت به الحكم، كالإجماع في نفس الحكم بتأخير عنه ثم يثبت به الحكم، وأيضا فإنه لا خلاف في جواز جعل بعض أحكام الفرع دليلا على الحكم في قياس الدلالة
The third demand: Objection that what he made a cause cannot be a cause. This also in two ways: A - Objection to making consensus a cause. Example: The Maliki argues for the obligation of zakat on the offspring between gazelles and sheep if the mothers are sheep, because it is separated from an animal that requires zakat by consensus, so it resembles the offspring between free-grazing and fodder-fed animals. The Shafi'i objects: This is incorrect, because consensus occurred after the death of the Messenger of Allah, while the obligation of zakat according to you was established in the era of the Messenger of Allah, and it is not permissible for the ruling to precede the cause. The answer from the Maliki arguer is to say: It is permissible for it to be later and still establish the ruling, like consensus regarding the same ruling being delayed from it and then establishing the ruling. Also, there is no dispute about the permissibility of making some rulings of the branch evidence for the ruling in qiyas al-dalala.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the validity of the cause ('illa) itself, specifically when consensus (ittifaq/ijma') is used as the cause. The example concerns zakat on hybrid animals (between gazelles and sheep). The Maliki argues that because the mother is a sheep, zakat is obligatory by consensus, analogous to the case of mixed grazing animals. The Shafi'i objects that consensus came after the Prophet's time, yet the zakat obligation was established earlier, so the ruling cannot precede its cause. The Maliki response argues that a cause can be subsequent and still indicate the ruling, just as consensus on a ruling can occur after the initial ruling and then reinforce it. Additionally, in qiyas al-dalala (indicative analogy), some branch rulings can serve as evidence for the original ruling. This lesson guards against the misconception that a cause must always be temporally prior to its effect; in jadal, consensus can be a sign (dalil) even if subsequent.

قياس الدلالة

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a cause need not be temporally prior if it functions as a sign (dalil) rather than a temporal cause; the Maliki's response illustrates this by invoking qiyas al-dalala.

Analyzing a Dialectical Exchange: Disagreement as Cause
Maliki uses disagreement as cause; Shafi'i objects; Maliki responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange by identifying the claimant's position, the objection, and the response, and evaluate whether the response successfully refutes the objection.

Core Idea
Disagreement as cause must be shown to be contemporaneous with the ruling, not posterior.

In this passage, we see a classic jadal exchange between a Maliki and a Shafi'i over whether disagreement (khilaf) about permissibility can serve as a cause for ritual purification of a dog's skin. The Maliki uses an analogy, the Shafi'i objects on temporal grounds, and the Maliki responds by reinterpreting disagreement as ijtihad. Understanding this exchange will sharpen your ability to follow and evaluate dialectical reasoning in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's position (mudda'i)
The Maliki claims that a dog's skin is purified by slaughter because it is an animal whose meat is subject to disagreement, analogous to the hyena.
The analogy is based on the 'illa (cause) of disagreement.
2
Find the objection (mu'tarid)
The Shafi'i objects that the ruling on dog's skin was established in the Prophet's time, but the disagreement about permissibility arose after the Prophet's death, so the cause cannot be posterior to the ruling.
This is a temporal precedence objection.
3
Analyze the response (jawab)
The Maliki responds by reinterpreting 'disagreement' to mean ijtihad, which existed even in the Prophet's time among the Companions, so the cause (disagreement as ijtihad) is contemporaneous.
The response redefines the key term to maintain temporal alignment.
4
Assess who wins by jadal rules
Evaluate whether the Maliki's response successfully refutes the Shafi'i's objection. Does the redefinition of khilaf as ijtihad hold? Is the analogy still valid?
The text does not declare a winner; this is an open evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاعتراض على جعل الخلاف علة : و مثاله؛ أن يستدل المالكي على طهارة جلد الكلب بالذكاة، بأن هذا حيوان مختلف في جواز أكله فوجب أن يطهر جلده بالذكاة كالضبع. فيقول الشافعي معترضا : هذا لا يصح، لأن الاختلاف حدث بعد وفاة رسول الله ، والحكم ثبت في زمانه والحكم لا يجوز أن يتقدم على علته. والجواب أن يقول المالكي: يجوز أن يكون هذا حادثا ويدل على الحكم، مثلا حدث بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ومع ذلك يجوز أن يجعل دليلا على الحكم .. ثم إن المراد بقولنا في أكل الكلب أنه مختلف فيه) : هو الاجتهاد، وهذا كان حاله في عهد رسول الله له حين ثبت له هذا الحكم، فلم يتقدم الحكم على علته.
B - Objection to making disagreement a cause: Example: The Maliki deduces the purity of a dog's skin through slaughter, because it is an animal about which there is disagreement regarding the permissibility of eating it, so its skin should be purified through slaughter like the hyena. The Shafi'i objects: This is not correct, because the disagreement occurred after the death of the Messenger of God, and the ruling was established in his time, and the ruling cannot precede its cause. The answer is that the Maliki says: It is permissible for this to be an incident and indicate the ruling, for example, it occurred after the Messenger of God and yet it is permissible to make it evidence for the ruling. Then what is meant by our saying about the dog's meat that it is disagreed upon is ijtihad, and this was its state in the era of the Messenger of God when this ruling was established for him, so the ruling did not precede its cause.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This is another dialectical exchange, this time concerning disagreement (khilaf) as a cause. The Maliki argues for the purity of a dog's skin by analogy: the dog is an animal whose meat is subject to disagreement, thus it should be purified by slaughter like the hyena. The Shafi'i objects that the disagreement arose after the Prophet's death, so the ruling (purity) cannot precede its cause. The Maliki responds that the disagreement can be understood as ijtihad, which was present even in the Prophet's time, so the cause is contemporaneous. This shows how temporal precedence is argued in jadal.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a respondent can counter an objection about temporal precedence by showing that the cause, when properly understood, is contemporaneous with the ruling.

Responding to Man' al-Hukm: Two Dialectical Strategies
Objection by denying the original ruling and two response strategies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection of man' al-hukm in an analogical argument and apply the two responses (establishing a sound original and reinterpreting the ruling) to defend the qiyas.

Core Idea
Man' al-hukm is overcome by showing a universally accepted original or reinterpreting the disputed ruling.

In jadal, when an opponent denies the ruling in the original case (man' al-hukm), the argument is not lost. This passage from usul al-fiqh teaches two powerful responses: either prove the original ruling is accepted by all (taslim) or reinterpret it so the opponent concedes. Through examples from Shafi'i and Maliki debates, you will learn to defend analogical reasoning against this common objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qiyas structure
Locate the original case (asl), branch (far'), 'illa, and ruling. In the Shafi'i example: wudu order analogized to prayer order.
2
Find the man' al-hukm objection
The opponent says 'la usallim al-asl' (I do not accept the original). Note how they attack the ruling in the original, not the analogy itself.
In the passage, the Hanafi denies order in prayer using the case of delayed prostrations.
3
Apply the first response: show a sound original
Point to a universally accepted sub-case within the original that forces the opponent to concede the ruling. The Shafi'i uses the consensus that prostrating before bowing is invalid.
This establishes the principle of order without disputing the opponent's counterexample.
4
Apply the second response: reinterpret the ruling
Define the disputed ruling in a way the opponent accepts. The passage mentions interpreting the ruling so that it is accepted (e.g., the Hanafi's contract example).
This is a linguistic or conceptual shift to secure taslim.
5
Evaluate the outcome
Both strategies force the opponent into a concession (taslim) that saves the qiyas. The responder wins if the original ruling is accepted even in one sound place.
As stated: 'whenever the ruling of the cause is positive, one place suffices for contentment.'

Source Text

المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض عليه بمنع الحكم في الأصل والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض من ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: أن يبين الأصل في موضع مسلم وذلك مثل أن يقيس الشافعي في إيجاب الترتيب في الوضوء على إيجابه في الصلاة. فيقول الحنفي : لا أسلم الأصل، فإن الترتيب لا يجب في الصلاة، فإنه لو ترك أربع سجدات في أربع ركعات فأتى بهن في آخر صلاته أجزأه من غير ترتيب. فيقول الشافعي مجيبا : لا خلاف أنه إذا قدم السجود على الركوع أن ذلك لا يجوز، وهذا يكفيني ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل المالكي على أن قراءة أم القرآن لا تجب على المأموم، بأن هذا مأموم فوجب أن يسقط عنه فرض القراءة، أصله قياسه على حالة ما إذا أدرك الإمام راكعا . فيقول الشافعي: لا أسلم فإنه إذا أدرك الإمام راكعا ورجا أن يقرأها ويدرك الإمام قبل فوات الركعة وجب عليه قراءتها . وجواب المالكي أن يقول : إنه لا خلاف أنه إذا خاف فوات الركعة، لم تجب عليه قراءة أم القرآن وهذا يكفينا في التسلي، ومتى كان حكم العلة الإثبات، فإنه يكفي في التسلي موضع واحد الثاني : أن يفسر الحكم بما يسلم : ومثاله : أن يقول الحنفي: إن الإجارة تبطل بالموت، لأنه عقد على المنفعة
Fourth demand: Objection of preventing the ruling in the original. The answer to this objection is from three aspects: The first: that he clarifies the original in a sound place. Example: The Shafi'i analogizes the obligation of order in wudu to its obligation in prayer. The Hanafi says: I do not accept the original, for order is not obligatory in prayer, for if he leaves four prostrations in four rak'ahs and performs them at the end of his prayer, it suffices without order. The Shafi'i responds: There is no disagreement that if he prostrates before bowing, it is not permissible, and this is sufficient for me. Another example: The Maliki deduces that reciting Umm al-Qur'an is not obligatory on the follower, because he is a follower, so the obligation of recitation should fall from him, its origin being analogized to the case when he catches the imam bowing. The Shafi'i says: I do not accept, for if he catches the imam bowing and hopes to recite it and catch the imam before the rak'ah is lost, then recitation is obligatory on him. The Maliki's answer: There is no disagreement that if he fears missing the rak'ah, reciting Umm al-Qur'an is not obligatory, and this suffices us for contentment. And whenever the ruling of the cause is positive, one place suffices for contentment. The second: that he interprets the ruling so that it is accepted. Example: The Hanafi says: Hire is annulled by death, because it is a contract for benefit...
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage deals with the objection of 'preventing the original ruling' (man' al-hukm fi al-asl), where the opponent denies the ruling in the original case used in the qiyas. The answer has three aspects, but only the first two are provided here. The first aspect is to establish the original ruling in a universally accepted case. The Shafi'i uses the example of order (tartib) in wudu analogized to prayer. The Hanafi denies that order is obligatory in prayer, citing the case of delayed prostrations. The Shafi'i counters by pointing out that there is consensus that prostrating before bowing is impermissible, which establishes the principle of order. Similarly, in the Maliki's example about the follower's recitation, the Shafi'i denies the original ruling, but the Maliki responds by showing that in cases of fear of missing the rak'ah, there is agreement that recitation is not obligatory. The second aspect is to interpret the ruling in a way that the opponent accepts. The example given is the Hanafi's claim that hire is annulled by death, but the passage cuts off. This demonstrates how dialectical responses can salvage a qiyas by either establishing a sound original or reinterpreting the ruling.

Three Ways to Answer an Objection Denying the Ruling in the Original
Author lists and exemplifies three responses to an objection that denies the ruling in the original case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three methods for responding when an opponent denies the ruling in the original case during a jadal debate.

Core Idea
A single conceded instance can validate the original; if not, reinterpret or cite direct evidence.

When an opponent denies the ruling (hukm) in the original case (asl) during analogical reasoning (qiyas), the proponent must respond effectively. This passage from a jadal manual lists three distinct strategies: pointing to a conceded instance, reinterpreting the disputed ruling, or providing explicit evidence. Mastering these responses is essential for any debater defending a qiyas in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The opponent denies the ruling in the original case (man' al-hukm fi al-asl). Locate where the Hanafi says 'I do not concede the original' in the first example.
2
Understand the first method: pointing to a conceded instance
The proponent shows one clear case where the ruling holds, even if the opponent disputes others. Al-Shafi'i notes that placing prostration before bowing is unanimously invalid, which suffices to establish the principle of tartib.
This method is called al-tasalli – acceptance that a single instance is enough.
3
Understand the second method: reinterpreting the ruling
The proponent clarifies the meaning of the ruling so the opponent concedes it. When the Hanafi says lease invalidates by death (like marriage), the opponent objects that marriage ends, not invalidates. The Hanafi reinterprets 'invalidation' as 'does not persist after death', making it acceptable.
This method is tafsir al-hukm bima yusallam.
4
Understand the third method: providing evidence
The proponent directly proves the original ruling with textual evidence (e.g., a hadith). For the pig analogy, the Shafi'i cites the hadith about washing after a dog's lapping, establishing the dog's ruling, which is then analogized to the pig.
This method is al-dalala 'ala al-asl.
5
Apply the methods to new scenarios
Given a juristic dispute, identify which method would be appropriate. For example, if the opponent denies that riba applies to a commodity, you could cite a hadith or find a conceded analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض عليه بمنع الحكم في الأصل والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول: أن يبين الأصل في موضع مسلم وذلك مثل أن يقيس الشافعي في إيجاب الترتيب في الوضوء على إيجابه في الصلاة . فيقول الحنفي : لا أسلم الأصل، فإن الترتيب لا يجب في الصلاة، فإنه لو ترك أربع سجدات في أربع ركعات فأتى بهن في آخر صلاته أجزأه من غير ترتيب. فيقول الشافعي مجيبا : لا خلاف أنه إذا قدم السجود على الركوع أن ذلك لا يجوز، وهذا يكفيني ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل المالكي على أن قراءة أم القرآن لا تجب على المأموم، بأن هذا مأموم فوجب أن يسقط عنه فرض القراءة، أصله قياسه على حالة ما إذا أدرك الإمام راكعا . فيقول الشافعي: لا أسلم فإنه إذا أدرك الإمام راكعا ورجا أن يقرأها ويدرك الإمام قبل فوات الركعة وجب عليه قراءتها . وجواب المالكي أن يقول : إنه لا خلاف أنه إذا خاف فوات الركعة، لم تجب عليه قراءة أم القرآن وهذا يكفينا في التسلي، ومتى كان حكم العلة الإثبات، فإنه يكفي في التسلي موضع واحد الثاني : أن يفسر الحكم بما يسلم : ومثاله : أن يقول الحنفي: إن الإجارة تبطل بالموت، لأنه عقد على المنفعة فبطل بموت المعقود له قياسا على النكاح فيقول له المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي : لا أسلم الأصل، فإن النكاح لا يبطل بالموت وإنما ينتهي بالموت كما تنتهي الإجارة بانقضاء المدة، فيفسر الحنفي الحكم بأنه يريد أنه يبقى بعد الموت، وهذا مسلم، فصح الأصل وسقط المنع الثالث : الدلالة على الأصل: وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي أو بعض المالكية في غسل الإناء من ولوغ الخنزير سبعًا : إنه حيوان نجس في حال حياته، فوجب غسل الإناء من ولوغه سبعًا كالكلب . فيقول الحنفي : لا أسلم الأصل.. فيقول المستدل أدل عليه بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : «طهور إناء أحدكم إذا ولغ فيه الكلب أن يغسله سبع مرات أولاهن بالتراب (2)؛ فإذا ثبت الأصل بالخبر، صح قياس الخنزير عليه ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل المالكي على أن بيع مدي عجوة(4) بمد عجوة ودرهم لا يجوز، لأن هذا جنس يجري فيه الربا، فلم يجز أن يكون عوضه من جنسه ومن غير جنسه كدينارين بدينار وسلعة . فيقول المستدل : إن لم تسلم به دللنا عليه والدليل ما روي عن فضالة بن عبيد قال : اشتريت يوم خيبر قلادة باثني عشر دينارًا فيها ذهب وخرز، ففصلتها فوجدت فيها أكثر من اثني عشر دينارًا، فذكرت ذلك للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فقال : لا تباع
The fourth demand: Objection by denial of the ruling in the original. The answer to this objection is from three aspects: First: That he shows the original in a conceded place. An example of that is al-Shafi'i analogizing the obligation of order (tartib) in wudu to its obligation in prayer. The Hanafi says: I do not concede the original, for order is not obligatory in prayer because if one leaves four prostrations in four rak'ahs and performs them at the end of his prayer, it suffices without order. Al-Shafi'i answers, saying: There is no disagreement that if one places prostration before bowing, it is not permissible, and that suffices for me. Another example: The Maliki argues that reciting Umm al-Qur'an is not obligatory on the follower (ma'mum), because he is a follower so the obligation of recitation falls from him, its origin being analogy to the case when he catches the imam bowing. Al-Shafi'i says: I do not concede, for if he catches the imam bowing and hopes to recite it and catch the imam before missing the rak'ah, recitation is obligatory on him. The Maliki's answer is to say: There is no disagreement that if he fears missing the rak'ah, recitation of Umm al-Qur'an is not obligatory, and this suffices for us in acceptance (tasalli). And whenever the ruling of the effective cause is affirmative, then one place suffices in acceptance. Second: That he interprets the ruling as something conceded. An example: The Hanafi says that a lease contract is invalidated by death because it is a contract for benefit, so it is invalidated by the death of the lessee by analogy to marriage. The Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali says to him: I do not concede the original, for marriage is not invalidated by death but rather ends with death just as the lease ends with expiry of term. So the Hanafi interprets the ruling as meaning that it remains after death, and this is conceded, so the original is correct and the denial falls. Third: Providing evidence for the original. An example: Al-Shafi'i or some Malikis concerning washing a utensil from the lapping of a pig seven times: It is an animal najis in its life, so it is obligatory to wash the utensil from its lapping seven times like the dog. The Hanafi says: I do not concede the original. The proponent says: I provide evidence for it by what is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The purification of the utensil of any of you when a dog laps from it is to wash it seven times, the first of them with earth'; so when the original is established by the report, analogy of the pig to it is correct. Another example: The Maliki argues that selling a mudd of 'ajwa dates for a mudd of 'ajwa dates and a dirham is not permissible because it is a genus in which riba runs, so it is not permissible that its compensation be from its genus and from another genus like two dinars for a dinar and a commodity. The proponent says: If you do not concede, we provide evidence for it, and the evidence is what is narrated from Fadala ibn 'Ubayd: I bought a necklace for twelve dinars containing gold and beads; I separated it and found more than twelve dinars' worth of gold, and I mentioned that to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he said: 'Do not sell it' until he mentioned the ruling.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth type of objection in jadal: the opponent denies the ruling (hukm) in the original case (asl) when a qiyas is presented. The author presents three methods to answer such an objection. The first method is to point out a conceded instance where the ruling holds. For example, when al-Shafi'i analogizes the obligation of order in wudu to prayer, the Hanafi denies that order is obligatory in prayer because missing prostration order is permissible. Al-Shafi'i responds by noting that placing prostration before bowing is unanimously impermissible, providing a single conceded case, which suffices to establish the principle. Similarly, in the Maliki's argument that the follower need not recite, al-Shafi'i denies the original by citing an exception, and the Maliki replies that the exception (fear of missing rak'ah) is uncontested and suffices for acceptance. The second method is to reinterpret the disputed ruling so that it becomes acceptable to the opponent. For instance, the Hanafi claims that lease contracts are invalidated by death, analogizing to marriage. The opponent objects that marriage is not invalidated but ends. The Hanafi then explains that by 'invalidation' he means the contract does not persist after death, a meaning conceded by all, thus removing the objection. The third method is to provide direct evidence for the ruling in the original case, such as a prophetic hadith. The Shafi'i/Maliki compares the pig to the dog for washing utensils; when the Hanafi denies the original ruling (that dog's lapping requires seven washes), the proponent cites the hadith about washing from a dog's lapping. Once the original is proven by textual evidence, the analogy to the pig stands. Similarly, the Maliki's argument about selling dates with a dirham is supported by the hadith of Fadala about selling a necklace containing gold and beads, which prohibits such mixed transactions. Each method aims to neutralize the objection by confirming the original ruling's validity through concession, reinterpretation, or proof.

Key Terms

الحكم
المنع
التسلي
العلة

Key Takeaway
When an opponent denies the ruling in the original, a proponent can respond by showing a conceded instance, reinterpreting the ruling, or providing direct evidence—three indispensable tools for defending analogical arguments.

Proving the Original Case in Qiyas: Textual Evidence
Author explains the method of proving the original case when denied, with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the step of providing evidence for the original case (al-dalala 'ala al-asl) in a jadal argument and apply it by constructing a similar response when the original case is denied in a qiyas debate.

Core Idea
In jadal, the original case must be proven with textual evidence before analogy proceeds.

In qiyas, the arguer often relies on an original case (asl) whose ruling is extended to a new case. However, the opponent may deny that the original case has the claimed ruling. The arguer must then provide evidence (dalala) from the primary sources—Qur'an or Sunna—to establish the ruling of the original case. This passage illustrates this dialectical move with two concrete fiqh examples: the pig licking analogy to the dog, and the sale of dates with dirham analogy to gold and silver.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original case (asl) and its claimed ruling
In each example, find the asl (e.g., dog's licking ruling) and what the arguer claims about it (impure animal, wash seven times).
2
Recognize the opponent's denial (adam al-taslim)
The Hanafi says 'I do not accept the original case' — identify exactly what is being denied: the ruling of the asl or its applicability to the new case.
3
Locate the textual evidence (nass) adduced
The arguer cites a hadith. What is the content of the hadith? Does it directly establish the claimed ruling?
4
Verify that the evidence establishes the asl
Confirm that the hadith provides the ruling for the dog (first example) or for gold/silver (second example). Then note that the analogy (tamthil) to the new case becomes valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : الدلالة على الأصل:
وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي أو بعض المالكية في غسل الإناء من ولوغ الخنزير سبعًا : إنه حيوان نجس في حال حياته، فوجب غسل الإناء من ولوغه سبعًا كالكلب .
فيقول الحنفي : لا أسلم الأصل.. فيقول المستدل أدل عليه بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : «طهور إناء أحدكم إذا ولغ فيه الكلب أن يغسله سبع مرات أولاهن بالتراب»؛ فإذا ثبت الأصل بالخبر، صح قياس الخنزير عليه ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل المالكي على أن بيع مدي عجوة بمد عجوة ودرهم لا يجوز، لأن هذا جنس يجري فيه الربا، فلم يجز أن يكون عوضه من جنسه ومن غير جنسه كدينارين بدينار وسلعة . فيقول المستدل : إن لم تسلم به دللنا عليه والدليل ما روي عن فضالة بن عبيد قال : اشتريت يوم خيبر قلادة باثني عشر دينارًا فيها ذهب وخرز، ففصلتها فوجدت فيها أكثر من اثني عشر دينارًا، فذكرت ذلك للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فقال : لا تباع حتى تفصل، وإذا ثبت هذا الحكم في الذهب والفضة بالخبر المذكور، صح أن يقيس عليه التمر بالعلة التي ذكرنا .
The third: Evidence for the original case: And that is like when the Shafi'i or some of the Malikis say regarding washing the vessel from the licking of a pig seven times: 'It is an impure animal while alive, so it is obligatory to wash the vessel from its licking seven times like the dog.' Then the Hanafi says: 'I do not accept the original case.' Then the arguer says: 'I will provide evidence for it by what is related from the Prophet (SAW) that he said: "The purification of the vessel of any one of you when a dog licks it is to wash it seven times, the first of them with earth." So when the original case is established by the report, the analogy of the pig upon it is valid. Another example: The Maliki argues that selling a mudd of ajwa dates for a mudd of ajwa dates and a dirham is not permissible, because this is a genus in which riba applies, so it is not permissible for its exchange to be from its own genus and from another genus, like two dinars for a dinar and a commodity. Then the arguer says: 'If you do not accept it, we will provide evidence for it, and the evidence is what is related from Fadala ibn Ubayd who said: I bought a necklace at Khaybar for twelve dinars containing gold and beads, then I separated it and found more than twelve dinars worth of gold, so I mentioned that to the Prophet (SAW), and he said: "Do not sell until you separate." And when this ruling is established for gold and silver by the mentioned report, it is valid to analogize dates upon it by the cause we mentioned.'
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage discusses the third type of objection in qiyas: providing evidence (dalala) for the original case (asl) when the opponent denies it. The author gives two examples from fiqh. The first example is about washing the vessel from the licking of a pig seven times, analogized to the dog. The arguer (Maliki) claims the pig is impure. The Hanafi denies the original ruling. The arguer then provides a hadith about the dog. Once the original case is established via textual evidence, the analogy to the pig is sound. The second example concerns selling dates for dates and dirham, where the Maliki argues it is riba. The opponent denies. The arguer provides the hadith of Fadala about gold and silver, establishing that selling a mixed lot must be separated. Then by analogy, dates are compared because of the cause (riba). So the passage illustrates how to defend the original case in a dialectical argument.

Preventing the Attribute (Man' al-Wasf) in Jadal
Author defines and exemplifies the objection of preventing the attribute in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a man' al-wasf objection and distinguish its three subtypes: preventing the attribute in the original case, in the branch, or in both.

Core Idea
Man' al-wasf denies the existence or effectiveness of the cause's attribute, a key jadal move.

This lesson introduces the fifth type of objection in jadal: preventing the attribute (man' al-wasf). The opponent argues that the attribute used as the cause does not exist in the original case, the branch, or both. The passage provides a detailed example from fiqh regarding continuity (muwalat) in purification. You will learn to identify this objection and the two main responses: reinterpretation (tafsir) and evidence (dalala).

Guided Path
1
Identify the attribute (wasf) in the arguer's cause
In the example, the arguer claims that purification is invalidated by hadath (impurity), so continuity is required like in prayer. The attribute 'being invalidated by hadath' is the wasf.
2
Determine where the attribute is being denied
The opponent denies that hadath directly invalidates prayer; he says it only invalidates purification. This is preventing the attribute in the original case (prayer).
The three subtypes: in the original, in the branch, or in both.
3
Analyze the response: tafsir
Tafsir reinterprets the wording of the cause: 'invalidates' is explained as 'prevents continuation and counting,' which the opponent accepts.
The reinterpretation must be consistent with the wording.
4
Analyze the response: dalala
Dalala provides evidence: a scenario where hadath directly invalidates prayer even without an intervening purification, proving the attribute exists.
Evidence can be textual or rational.
5
Apply to another fiqhi example
Test your understanding by constructing a man' al-wasf objection in a different analogy, such as intoxicants, and propose a response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الخامس : الاعتراض عليه بمنع الوصف
سواء كان هذا الوصف في الأصل أو في الفرع أو فيهما. وهذا الاعتراض قد يكون على أصل المستدل أو على أصل المعترض. أ - منع الوصف على أصل المعترض:
وهو على ثلاثة أوجه : منع الوصف في الأصل، ومنع الوصف في الفرع، ومنع الوصف فيهما. أ - 1 - منع الوصف في الأصل ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي على أن الموالاة شرط في الطهارة، لأن هذه عبادة يبطلها الحدث، فكانت الموالاة شرطًا في صحتها كالصلاة .
فيقول المخالف : لا أسلم أن الحدث يبطل الصلاة، وإنما يبطل الطهارة، ثم ببطلان الطهارة تبطل الصلاة .
والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض من وجهين :
الأول : التفسير : وهو أن يفسر ذلك بمعنى مسلم ويكون موافقا للفظ العلة، وذلك مثل أن يقول : أريد بقولي إنه يبطلها الحدث أن الحدث يمنع من التمادي فيها، والاعتداد به، وهذا مُسَلَّم. الثاني : الدلالة وهو أن يدل على أن الحدث يبطلها، وذلك بأن يقول : لا تصح الممانعة على أصلك لأنك تقول (إنه لو سبقه الحدث فانصرف ليتوضأ ثم أحدث في طريقه إن صلاته تبطل، وليس هناك طهارة، فثبت أن الحدث يبطل الصلاة)
The fifth request: Objecting to it by preventing the attribute, whether this attribute is in the original case or in the branch or in both. And this objection may be against the original case of the arguer or against the original case of the objector. A - Preventing the attribute against the original case of the objector: And it has three forms: preventing the attribute in the original case, preventing the attribute in the branch, and preventing the attribute in both. A-1- Preventing the attribute in the original case, and its example: The Maliki or Shafi'i or Hanbali argues that continuity (muwalat) is a condition in purification, because it is an act of worship that is invalidated by the occurrence of impurity, so continuity is a condition for its validity like prayer. The opponent says: 'I do not accept that the occurrence of impurity invalidates prayer; rather it invalidates purification, and then the invalidation of purification invalidates prayer.' The answer to this objection is from two aspects: The first: Interpretation (tafsir): It is to interpret that with an accepted meaning that agrees with the wording of the cause, such as saying: 'I mean by my statement "impurity invalidates it" that impurity prevents continuing it and counting it, and this is accepted.' The second: Evidence (dalala): It is to provide evidence that impurity invalidates it, by saying: 'The denial is not valid according to your principle, because you say that if impurity preceded him so he left to perform wudu then he had an impurity on his way, his prayer is invalid, and there is no purification there, so it is established that impurity invalidates the prayer.'
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth type of objection: preventing the attribute (man' al-wasf). It explains that the attribute may be in the original case, the branch, or both. The objection can target the arguer's original case or the objector's. Then it details the first subtype: preventing the attribute in the original case. The example concerns muwalat (continuity) in purification. The arguer claims that purification is an act of worship invalidated by hadath (impurity), so continuity is required. The opponent objects that hadath does not directly invalidate prayer; it invalidates purification, which then leads to invalidity. The author provides two answers: tafsir (reinterpretation) and dalala (evidence). Tafsir redefines 'invalidates' to mean 'prevents continuation', which is accepted. Dalala shows a scenario where hadath directly invalidates prayer even without an intervening purification, proving the point.

Key Terms
منع الوصف (man' al-wasf)
التفسير (al-tafsir)
الدلالة (al-dalala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example about muwalat, what is the opponent's objection?
A) That impurity does not invalidate prayer directly
B) That continuity is not required in purification
C) That the analogy to prayer is invalid
D) That the hadith is weak

Key Takeaway
Man' al-wasf objects to the existence or applicability of the cause's attribute in the original, branch, or both, and can be answered through reinterpretation (tafsir) or evidence (dalala).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a man' al-wasf objection in a fiqh analogy. For example, argue that nabidh (date wine) is forbidden by qiyas on khamr because both are intoxicating. Then have the opponent prevent the attribute of intoxication in nabidh. Provide a tafsir or dalala response.

Man' al-Wasf fi al-Asl: Denying the Attribute in the Original Case
Author explains man' al-wasf fi al-asl with the muwalah example and two rebuttal methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection that denies the attribute in the original case and respond using tafsir and dalala.

Core Idea
Denying the attribute in the original case attacks the cause's presence, not its validity.

In jadal, when an opponent denies the attribute in the original case, they are claiming the cause does not hold in the root. This lesson examines the first type of man' al-wasf using the muwalah example and the two main rebuttal methods: tafsir (interpretation) and dalala (indication).

Guided Path
1
Identify the mustadill's claim and the attribute
Find the attribute (wasf) that the mustadill uses as the cause. In the example, the attribute is that 'hadath invalidates prayer'.
The attribute is the middle term in the analogical argument.
2
Recognize the mu'tarid's denial in the asl
The objector concedes the attribute in the branch but denies it in the original case. Here, he claims hadath only invalidates purification, not prayer directly.
This is man' al-wasf fi al-asl.
3
Distinguish tafsir and dalala as rebuttals
Tafsir reinterprets the wording of the attribute to a mutually accepted meaning. Dalala provides evidence from the opponent's own principles to prove the attribute holds.
Both methods aim to re-establish the attribute in the asl.
4
Apply tafsir to the example
Reinterpret 'invalidates' as 'prevents continuation and counting', which the opponent concedes.
The interpretation must still fit the original wording of the cause.
5
Apply dalala using the opponent's example
Use the opponent's own scenario (a person who leaves to perform wudu and has another hadath on the way) to show that hadath independently invalidates prayer, without involving purification.
This demonstrates that the attribute exists in the asl despite the opponent's denial.
