المطلب الخامس : الاعتراض عليه بمنع الوصف سواء كان هذا الوصف في الأصل أو في الفرع أو فيهما. وهذا الاعتراض قد يكون على أصل المستدل أو على أصل المعترض. أ - منع الوصف على أصل المعترض: وهو على ثلاثة أوجه : منع الوصف في الأصل، ومنع الوصف في الفرع، ومنع الوصف فيهما. أ - 1 - منع الوصف في الأصل ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي على أن الموالاة شرط في الطهارة، لأن هذه عبادة يبطلها الحدث، فكانت الموالاة شرطًا في صحتها كالصلاة . فيقول المخالف : لا أسلم أن الحدث يبطل الصلاة، وإنما يبطل الطهارة، ثم ببطلان الطهارة تبطل الصلاة . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض من وجهين : الأول : التفسير : وهو أن يفسر ذلك بمعنى مسلم ويكون موافقا للفظ العلة، وذلك مثل أن يقول : أريد بقولي إنه يبطلها الحدث أن الحدث يمنع من التمادي فيها، والاعتداد به، وهذا مُسَلَّم. الثاني : الدلالة وهو أن يدل على أن الحدث يبطلها، وذلك بأن يقول : لا تصح الممانعة على أصلك لأنك تقول إنه لو سبقه الحدث فانصرف ليتوضأ ثم أحدث في طريقه إن صلاته تبطل، وليس هناك طهارة، فثبت أن الحدث يبطل الصلاة على الانفراد والطهارة على الانفراد. أ - 2 - منع الوصف في الفرع : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن قتل العبد خطأ لا تجب فيه الكفارة بأن هذا حيوان تجب بقتله القيمة، فلم تجب بقتله الكفارة، كالبهائم . فيقول الحنفي معترضا : لا أسلم أنه تجب بقتله القيمة لأن عندنا أن بدله يتقدر بما دون دية الحر لعشرة دراهم. والطريق للمالكي في الجواب على هذا الاعتراض من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : التفسير : مثل أن يقول : أريد بقولي القيمة ؛ أن هذا بدل غير مقدر، بل تعتبر فيه صفات المبدل منه وهذا معنى القيمة . الثاني : بيان موضع التسليم ؛ وذلك مثل أن يقول : أردت الذي يبلغ نصف الدية أو أقل من ذلك، فإنه مضمون بقيمة بالغة ما بلغت، وإنما يتقدر عندكم بدل من زادت قيمته على دية الحر. الثالث : الدلالة ؛ وذلك أن من ضمنت أطرافه بالقيمة، فنفسه مضمونة بالقيمة، فإذا ثبت ذلك صح الوصف واستمر القياس. أ - 3 - منع الوصف في الأصل والفرع معًا ومثاله، أن يستدل المالكي على أن التيمم واجب إلى المرفقين بأن كل طهارة شرعت بالصعيد في محل الوضوء أن تستوعب المحل، كتطهير الوجه . فيقول الحنفي : لا أسلم الوصف بأنها طهارة في فرع ولا أصل، لأن الطهارة ما طهر المحل ورفع الحدث، وقد أجمعنا على أن التيمم لا يرفع الحدث، فلم يوصف بأنه طهارة إلا على سبيل المجاز والاتساع والتعليل موضع تحقيق . والجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : التفسير : وهو أن يقول المستدل : إن المراد بقولنا طهارة أنه تستباح الصلاة، لا معنى له أكثر من ذلك، وهذا غير ممنوع. الثاني : الدلالة، وذلك بما روي عن النبي الله أنه قال : جعلت لي الأرض
The fifth sub-topic: Objection to it by denying the attribute, whether this attribute is in the original case, in the branch, or in both. This objection may be against the principle of the one adducing evidence or against the principle of the objector. A - Denying the attribute on the objector's principle: It has three forms: (1) Denying the attribute in the original case, (2) Denying the attribute in the branch, (3) Denying the attribute in both. A-1: Denying the attribute in the original case, example: The Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues that continuity (muwalah) is a condition in purification (taharah) because it is an act of worship invalidated by hadath (ritual impurity), so continuity is a condition for its validity like prayer. The opponent says: I do not accept that hadath invalidates prayer; rather it invalidates purification, then by the invalidity of purification prayer becomes invalid. The response to this objection is from two aspects: First: Interpretation (tafsir): that he interprets it according to an accepted meaning that agrees with the wording of the cause, such as saying: By my saying 'hadath invalidates it' I mean that hadath prevents continuation in it and counting it, and this is conceded. Second: Indication (dalala): that he indicates that hadath invalidates it, such as saying: The objection is not valid according to your principle because you say that if hadath overtakes him and he leaves to perform wudu then he experiences hadath on the way, his prayer becomes invalid, and there is no purification there, so it is established that hadath invalidates prayer independently and purification independently. A-2: Denying the attribute in the branch: Example: The Maliki argues that killing a slave by mistake does not require expiation (kaffarah) because this is a living creature for which compensation (qimah) is required when killed, so expiation is not required when killed, like animals. The Hanafi objects: I do not accept that compensation is required when killed because in our view its equivalent is assessed at less than the blood-money of a free person up to ten dirhams. The Maliki's method of response to this objection has three aspects: First: Interpretation: such as saying: By my saying 'compensation' I mean that this is an unassessed substitute, rather the qualities of the substituted thing are considered, and this is the meaning of compensation. Second: Clarification of the point of concession (bayan mawdi' al-taslim): such as saying: I mean that which reaches half the blood-money or less than that, for it is guaranteed by compensation whatever it reaches, but in your view only a substitute for one whose value exceeds the blood-money of a free person is assessed. Third: Indication (dalala): that if its limbs are guaranteed by compensation, then its soul is guaranteed by compensation, so when this is established the attribute is valid and the analogy continues. A-3: Denying the attribute in both original and branch together: Example: The Maliki argues that tayammum is obligatory up to the elbows because every purification legislated with pure earth in place of ablution (wudu) must cover the place, like cleaning the face. The Hanafi says: I do not accept the attribute that it is purification in either branch or original, because purification is that which cleans the place and removes hadath, and we all agree that tayammum does not remove hadath, so it is not described as purification except metaphorically and broadly, and causal reasoning requires precision. The response to it has two aspects: First: Interpretation: that the one adducing evidence says: What is meant by our saying 'purification' is that it permits prayer, it has no meaning beyond that, and this is not objectionable. Second: Indication (dalala), by what is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The earth has been made for me...'
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical classification of a specific type of objection in jadal (disputation), namely 'objection by denying the attribute' (man' al-wasf). The author (presumably al-Baji or Ibn Aqil) presents the fifth sub-topic under objections to analogical reasoning (qiyas). He first explains that the denial can target the attribute (wasf) in the original case (asl), the branch (far'), or both. Then he focuses on the subtype where the objection is based on the objector's own principle (asl al-mu'tarid), which consists of three cases. For each case, he gives a concrete fiqhi example and then the proper methods of rebuttal.

Case 1 (man' al-wasf fi al-asl): The example involves the condition of continuity (muwalah) in ritual purification. The one adducing evidence (mustadill) from the Maliki/Shafi'i/Hanbali school claims that hadath (ritual impurity) invalidates prayer, so continuity is required. The objector (mu'tarid) denies that hadath directly invalidates prayer; instead, it invalidates purification, and then the invalidity of purification causes the prayer to be invalid. The response has two methods: interpretation (tafsir) and indication (dalala). Interpretation redefines the wording of the cause to an accepted meaning (e.g., 'invalidates' means prevents continuation). Indication uses the objector's own principles to show that hadath does independently invalidate prayer.

Case 2 (man' al-wasf fi al-far'): The example concerns whether killing a slave by mistake requires expiation (kaffarah). The Maliki argues that since the slave's life is compensated by qimah (like an animal), expiation is not required. The Hanafi objects by denying that qimah is required for a slave; in Hanafi law, the compensation is less than the blood-money of a free person, assessed at a fixed amount. The Maliki has three rebuttal methods: interpretation (redefining qimah as an unassessed substitute), clarification of the point of concession (specifying that he means the value can be up to half the blood-money), and indication (if limbs are guaranteed by qimah, then the whole life is also guaranteed by qimah, thus the attribute of qimah applies).

Case 3 (man' al-wasf fi al-asl wa al-far' ma'an): The example is about the extent of tayammum (dry ablution). The Maliki argues that tayammum should cover the arms up to the elbows because it is a purification with earth that must cover the place like washing the face in wudu. The Hanafi denies the attribute of 'purification' entirely for tayammum, since tayammum does not remove hadath, so it is not a real purification. The response uses interpretation (purification here means 'permits prayer', not removal of hadath) and indication (citing the hadith 'the earth has been made for me a mosque and a purification'). The passage thus illustrates the procedural tools of tafsir, bayan mawdi' al-taslim, and dalala for rebutting man' al-wasf.

Key Terms
منع الوصف
الأصل
الفرع
التفسير
الدلالة

Checkpoint
In the muwalah example, what is the attribute (wasf) that the opponent denies?
A) The continuity condition
B) That hadath invalidates prayer
C) That purification is an act of worship
D) That prayer requires continuity

Key Takeaway
Man' al-wasf fi al-asl requires the mustadill to reinterpret or provide evidence to re-establish the attribute in the original case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the pattern of the muwalah example, construct a similar jadal exchange regarding the attribute of 'riba' in a commodity exchange. Identify the asl, far', and the denial, then provide a tafsir and dalala rebuttal.

Man' al-Wasf fi al-Far': Denying the Attribute in the Branch Case
Author illustrates man' al-wasf fi al-far' with the slave-killing example and three rebuttal methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply tafsir, bayan mawdi' al-taslim, and dalala to rebut an objection that denies the attribute in the branch.

Core Idea
Denying the attribute in the branch often requires redefinition or partial concession.

When the opponent denies that the attribute applies to the branch case, the mustadill has three rebuttal methods: interpretation, clarification of the point of concession, and indication. This lesson explores the second type of man' al-wasf using the example of killing a slave by mistake.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mustadill's analogy
The mustadill compares the slave (far') to animals (asl) on the attribute of qimah (compensation) being due.
The attribute is that qimah is required when killed.
2
Recognize the mu'tarid's denial of the attribute in the far'
The Hanafi denies that qimah is due for a slave, claiming a fixed compensation less than the free person's blood-money.
This is man' al-wasf fi al-far'.
3
Distinguish the three rebuttal methods
Tafsir redefines the attribute to a conceded meaning. Bayan mawdi' al-taslim concedes part of the opponent's point to narrow the disagreement. Dalala provides evidence that the attribute holds from a broader principle.
All three aim to re-establish the attribute in the far'.
4
Apply tafsir
Redefine qimah as an unassessed substitute considering qualities, which is a different meaning from the Hanafi's fixed compensation.
The reinterpretation must still be linguistically valid.
5
Apply bayan mawdi' al-taslim
Concede that the value is up to half the blood-money, but argue that it is still qimah by definition.
This narrows the disagreement and forces the opponent to accept the attribute.

2 - منع الوصف في الفرع : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن قتل العبد خطأ لا تجب فيه الكفارة بأن هذا حيوان تجب بقتله القيمة، فلم تجب بقتله الكفارة، كالبهائم . فيقول الحنفي معترضا : لا أسلم أنه تجب بقتله القيمة لأن عندنا أن بدله يتقدر بما دون دية الحر لعشرة دراهم. والطريق للمالكي في الجواب على هذا الاعتراض من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : التفسير : مثل أن يقول : أريد بقولي القيمة ؛ أن هذا بدل غير مقدر، بل تعتبر فيه صفات المبدل منه وهذا معنى القيمة . الثاني : بيان موضع التسليم ؛ وذلك مثل أن يقول : أردت الذي يبلغ نصف الدية أو أقل من ذلك، فإنه مضمون بقيمة بالغة ما بلغت، وإنما يتقدر عندكم بدل من زادت قيمته على دية الحر. الثالث : الدلالة ؛ وذلك أن من ضمنت أطرافه بالقيمة، فنفسه مضمونة بالقيمة، فإذا ثبت ذلك صح الوصف واستمر القياس. أ - 3 - منع الوصف في الأصل والفرع معًا ومثاله، أن يستدل المالكي على أن التيمم واجب إلى المرفقين بأن كل طهارة شرعت بالصعيد في محل الوضوء أن تستوعب المحل، كتطهير الوجه . فيقول الحنفي : لا أسلم الوصف بأنها طهارة في فرع ولا أصل، لأن الطهارة ما طهر المحل ورفع الحدث، وقد أجمعنا على أن التيمم لا يرفع الحدث، فلم يوصف بأنه طهارة إلا على سبيل المجاز والاتساع والتعليل موضع تحقيق . والجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : التفسير : وهو أن يقول المستدل : إن المراد بقولنا طهارة أنه تستباح الصلاة، لا معنى له أكثر من ذلك، وهذا غير ممنوع. الثاني : الدلالة، وذلك بما روي عن النبي الله أنه قال : جعلت لي الأرض
The fifth sub-topic: Objection to it by denying the attribute, whether this attribute is in the original case, in the branch, or in both. This objection may be against the principle of the one adducing evidence or against the principle of the objector. A - Denying the attribute on the objector's principle: It has three forms: (1) Denying the attribute in the original case, (2) Denying the attribute in the branch, (3) Denying the attribute in both. A-1: Denying the attribute in the original case, example: The Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues that continuity (muwalah) is a condition in purification (taharah) because it is an act of worship invalidated by hadath (ritual impurity), so continuity is a condition for its validity like prayer. The opponent says: I do not accept that hadath invalidates prayer; rather it invalidates purification, then by the invalidity of purification prayer becomes invalid. The response to this objection is from two aspects: First: Interpretation (tafsir): that he interprets it according to an accepted meaning that agrees with the wording of the cause, such as saying: By my saying 'hadath invalidates it' I mean that hadath prevents continuation in it and counting it, and this is conceded. Second: Indication (dalala): that he indicates that hadath invalidates it, such as saying: The objection is not valid according to your principle because you say that if hadath overtakes him and he leaves to perform wudu then he experiences hadath on the way, his prayer becomes invalid, and there is no purification there, so it is established that hadath invalidates prayer independently and purification independently. A-2: Denying the attribute in the branch: Example: The Maliki argues that killing a slave by mistake does not require expiation (kaffarah) because this is a living creature for which compensation (qimah) is required when killed, so expiation is not required when killed, like animals. The Hanafi objects: I do not accept that compensation is required when killed because in our view its equivalent is assessed at less than the blood-money of a free person up to ten dirhams. The Maliki's method of response to this objection has three aspects: First: Interpretation: such as saying: By my saying 'compensation' I mean that this is an unassessed substitute, rather the qualities of the substituted thing are considered, and this is the meaning of compensation. Second: Clarification of the point of concession (bayan mawdi' al-taslim): such as saying: I mean that which reaches half the blood-money or less than that, for it is guaranteed by compensation whatever it reaches, but in your view only a substitute for one whose value exceeds the blood-money of a free person is assessed. Third: Indication (dalala): that if its limbs are guaranteed by compensation, then its soul is guaranteed by compensation, so when this is established the attribute is valid and the analogy continues. A-3: Denying the attribute in both original and branch together: Example: The Maliki argues that tayammum is obligatory up to the elbows because every purification legislated with pure earth in place of ablution (wudu) must cover the place, like cleaning the face. The Hanafi says: I do not accept the attribute that it is purification in either branch or original, because purification is that which cleans the place and removes hadath, and we all agree that tayammum does not remove hadath, so it is not described as purification except metaphorically and broadly, and causal reasoning requires precision. The response to it has two aspects: First: Interpretation: that the one adducing evidence says: What is meant by our saying 'purification' is that it permits prayer, it has no meaning beyond that, and this is not objectionable. Second: Indication (dalala), by what is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The earth has been made for me...'
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical classification of a specific type of objection in jadal (disputation), namely 'objection by denying the attribute' (man' al-wasf). The author (presumably al-Baji or Ibn Aqil) presents the fifth sub-topic under objections to analogical reasoning (qiyas). He first explains that the denial can target the attribute (wasf) in the original case (asl), the branch (far'), or both. Then he focuses on the subtype where the objection is based on the objector's own principle (asl al-mu'tarid), which consists of three cases. For each case, he gives a concrete fiqhi example and then the proper methods of rebuttal.

Case 1 (man' al-wasf fi al-asl): The example involves the condition of continuity (muwalah) in ritual purification. The one adducing evidence (mustadill) from the Maliki/Shafi'i/Hanbali school claims that hadath (ritual impurity) invalidates prayer, so continuity is required. The objector (mu'tarid) denies that hadath directly invalidates prayer; instead, it invalidates purification, and then the invalidity of purification causes the prayer to be invalid. The response has two methods: interpretation (tafsir) and indication (dalala). Interpretation redefines the wording of the cause to an accepted meaning (e.g., 'invalidates' means prevents continuation). Indication uses the objector's own principles to show that hadath does independently invalidate prayer.

Case 2 (man' al-wasf fi al-far'): The example concerns whether killing a slave by mistake requires expiation (kaffarah). The Maliki argues that since the slave's life is compensated by qimah (like an animal), expiation is not required. The Hanafi objects by denying that qimah is required for a slave; in Hanafi law, the compensation is less than the blood-money of a free person, assessed at a fixed amount. The Maliki has three rebuttal methods: interpretation (redefining qimah as an unassessed substitute), clarification of the point of concession (specifying that he means the value can be up to half the blood-money), and indication (if limbs are guaranteed by qimah, then the whole life is also guaranteed by qimah, thus the attribute of qimah applies).

Case 3 (man' al-wasf fi al-asl wa al-far' ma'an): The example is about the extent of tayammum (dry ablution). The Maliki argues that tayammum should cover the arms up to the elbows because it is a purification with earth that must cover the place like washing the face in wudu. The Hanafi denies the attribute of 'purification' entirely for tayammum, since tayammum does not remove hadath, so it is not a real purification. The response uses interpretation (purification here means 'permits prayer', not removal of hadath) and indication (citing the hadith 'the earth has been made for me a mosque and a purification'). The passage thus illustrates the procedural tools of tafsir, bayan mawdi' al-taslim, and dalala for rebutting man' al-wasf.

When the attribute is denied in the branch, the mustadill can use tafsir, bayan mawdi' al-taslim, and dalala to re-establish the analogy.

Man' al-Wasf fi al-Asl wa al-Far': Denying the Attribute in Both Original and Branch
Author presents man' al-wasf fi al-asl wa al-far' with the tayammum example and two rebuttal methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rebut an objection that denies the attribute altogether in both the original and branch using tafsir and dalala.

Core Idea
When the attribute is denied in both cases, the mustadill must redefine the attribute in a way that applies to both.

The most severe form of man' al-wasf is when the opponent denies the attribute entirely in both the original and the branch. The author uses the example of tayammum to show how tafsir and dalala can salvage the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mustadill's analogy
The Maliki argues that tayammum should cover the arms up to the elbows because it is a purification with earth, like washing the face in wudu.
The attribute is 'purification'.
2
Recognize the mu'tarid's denial in both asl and far'
The Hanafi denies that tayammum is a purification at all, since it does not remove hadath (ritual impurity).
This is man' al-wasf fi al-asl wa al-far'.
3
Understand the basis of the denial
The Hanafi argues that the true meaning of purification (taharah) is removing hadath, which tayammum does not do.
The denial challenges the very classification of tayammum as a purification.
4
Apply tafsir: redefine 'purification'
The Maliki redefines 'purification' as 'that which permits prayer', which does not require removal of hadath.
This reinterpretation avoids the objection and is still consistent with the term's usage.
5
Apply dalala: cite textual evidence
The Maliki refers to the prophetic hadith 'the earth has been made for me a mosque and a purification' (ṭahūr) to prove that tayammum is indeed a purification in Islamic law.
The hadith provides authoritative support for the attribute.

المطلب الخامس : الاعتراض عليه بمنع الوصف سواء كان هذا الوصف في الأصل أو في الفرع أو فيهما. وهذا الاعتراض قد يكون على أصل المستدل أو على أصل المعترض. أ - منع الوصف على أصل المعترض: وهو على ثلاثة أوجه : منع الوصف في الأصل، ومنع الوصف في الفرع، ومنع الوصف فيهما. أ - 1 - منع الوصف في الأصل ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي على أن الموالاة شرط في الطهارة، لأن هذه عبادة يبطلها الحدث، فكانت الموالاة شرطًا في صحتها كالصلاة . فيقول المخالف : لا أسلم أن الحدث يبطل الصلاة، وإنما يبطل الطهارة، ثم ببطلان الطهارة تبطل الصلاة . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض من وجهين : الأول : التفسير : وهو أن يفسر ذلك بمعنى مسلم ويكون موافقا للفظ العلة، وذلك مثل أن يقول : أريد بقولي إنه يبطلها الحدث أن الحدث يمنع من التمادي فيها، والاعتداد به، وهذا مُسَلَّم. الثاني : الدلالة وهو أن يدل على أن الحدث يبطلها، وذلك بأن يقول : لا تصح الممانعة على أصلك لأنك تقول إنه لو سبقه الحدث فانصرف ليتوضأ ثم أحدث في طريقه إن صلاته تبطل، وليس هناك طهارة، فثبت أن الحدث يبطل الصلاة على الانفراد والطهارة على الانفراد. أ - 2 - منع الوصف في الفرع : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن قتل العبد خطأ لا تجب فيه الكفارة بأن هذا حيوان تجب بقتله القيمة، فلم تجب بقتله الكفارة، كالبهائم . فيقول الحنفي معترضا : لا أسلم أنه تجب بقتله القيمة لأن عندنا أن بدله يتقدر بما دون دية الحر لعشرة دراهم. والطريق للمالكي في الجواب على هذا الاعتراض من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : التفسير : مثل أن يقول : أريد بقولي القيمة ؛ أن هذا بدل غير مقدر، بل تعتبر فيه صفات المبدل منه وهذا معنى القيمة . الثاني : بيان موضع التسليم ؛ وذلك مثل أن يقول : أردت الذي يبلغ نصف الدية أو أقل من ذلك، فإنه مضمون بقيمة بالغة ما بلغت، وإنما يتقدر عندكم بدل من زادت قيمته على دية الحر. الثالث : الدلالة ؛ وذلك أن من ضمنت أطرافه بالقيمة، فنفسه مضمونة بالقيمة، فإذا ثبت ذلك صح الوصف واستمر القياس. أ - 3 - منع الوصف في الأصل والفرع معًا ومثاله، أن يستدل المالكي على أن التيمم واجب إلى المرفقين بأن كل طهارة شرعت بالصعيد في محل الوضوء أن تستوعب المحل، كتطهير الوجه . فيقول الحنفي : لا أسلم الوصف بأنها طهارة في فرع ولا أصل، لأن الطهارة ما طهر المحل ورفع الحدث، وقد أجمعنا على أن التيمم لا يرفع الحدث، فلم يوصف بأنه طهارة إلا على سبيل المجاز والاتساع والتعليل موضع تحقيق . والجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : التفسير : وهو أن يقول المستدل : إن المراد بقولنا طهارة أنه تستباح الصلاة، لا معنى له أكثر من ذلك، وهذا غير ممنوع. الثاني : الدلالة، وذلك بما روي عن النبي الله أنه قال : جعلت لي الأرض
The fifth sub-topic: Objection to it by denying the attribute, whether this attribute is in the original case, in the branch, or in both. This objection may be against the principle of the one adducing evidence or against the principle of the objector. A - Denying the attribute on the objector's principle: It has three forms: (1) Denying the attribute in the original case, (2) Denying the attribute in the branch, (3) Denying the attribute in both. A-1: Denying the attribute in the original case, example: The Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues that continuity (muwalah) is a condition in purification (taharah) because it is an act of worship invalidated by hadath (ritual impurity), so continuity is a condition for its validity like prayer. The opponent says: I do not accept that hadath invalidates prayer; rather it invalidates purification, then by the invalidity of purification prayer becomes invalid. A-2: Denying the attribute in the branch: Example: The Maliki argues that killing a slave by mistake does not require expiation (kaffarah) because this is a living creature for which compensation (qimah) is required when killed, so expiation is not required when killed, like animals. The Hanafi objects: I do not accept that compensation is required when killed because in our view its equivalent is assessed at less than the blood-money of a free person up to ten dirhams. The Maliki's method of response to this objection has three aspects: First: Interpretation: such as saying: By my saying 'compensation' I mean that this is an unassessed substitute, rather the qualities of the substituted thing are considered, and this is the meaning of compensation. Second: Clarification of the point of concession (bayan mawdi' al-taslim): such as saying: I mean that which reaches half the blood-money or less than that, for it is guaranteed by compensation whatever it reaches, but in your view only a substitute for one whose value exceeds the blood-money of a free person is assessed. Third: Indication (dalala): that if its limbs are guaranteed by compensation, then its soul is guaranteed by compensation, so when this is established the attribute is valid and the analogy continues. A-3: Denying the attribute in both original and branch together: Example: The Maliki argues that tayammum is obligatory up to the elbows because every purification legislated with pure earth in place of ablution (wudu) must cover the place, like cleaning the face. The response to it has two aspects: First: Interpretation: that the one adducing evidence says: What is meant by our saying 'purification' is that it permits prayer, it has no meaning beyond that, and this is not objectionable. Second: Indication (dalala), by what is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The earth has been made for me...'

Responding to Prevention of the Attribute in the Branch
Author explains the objection preventing attribute in branch with Maliki example and three responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three responses (tafsir, bayan mawdi' al-taslim, dalala) to counter an objection that denies the attribute in the branch during a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Three tools rescue an analogy when the opponent denies the attribute in the branch.

In analogical reasoning (qiyas), the objector may challenge the existence of the attribute (wasf) in the branch case. This is called 'prevention of the attribute in the branch' (man' al-wasf fi al-far'). The author illustrates this with a dispute between a Maliki and a Hanafi over expiation for killing a slave. He then presents three distinct methods to respond: interpretation (tafsir), pinpointing the point of concession (bayan mawdi' al-taslim), and providing evidence (dalala). Mastering these responses is essential for defending analogical arguments in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original analogy
Find the analogical argument: the Maliki claims that killing a slave by mistake does not require expiation, just like killing a beast, because both are animals whose killing necessitates value (qima).
The attribute is 'necessitating value' (wujub al-qima).
2
Recognize the objection
The Hanafi objects by denying the attribute in the branch: 'I do not concede that killing a slave necessitates value, because according to us its compensation is less than the blood money of a free man.' This is man' al-wasf fi al-far'.
The attribute is being denied specifically in the branch (slave).
3
Analyze the three responses
The Maliki can respond using: 1) Tafsir – clarifying the meaning of 'value' as unestimated compensation based on qualities; 2) Bayan mawdi' al-taslim – specifying that 'value' means a value that reaches half blood money or less, which the opponent concedes; 3) Dalala – proving that if limbs are guaranteed with value, then life is also guaranteed with value.
Each response serves a different purpose: definitional, concessional, or evidential.
4
Apply to a new analogical debate
Given a new qiyas, when the opponent denies the presence of the attribute in the branch, select which of the three responses would be most effective and justify why.
The choice depends on whether the denial is due to ambiguity, partial concession, or lack of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - 2 - منع الوصف في الفرع : وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أن قتل العبد خطأ لا تجب فيه الكفارة بأن هذا حيوان تجب بقتله القيمة، فلم تجب بقتله الكفارة، كالبهائم . فيقول الحنفي معترضا : لا أسلم أنه تجب بقتله القيمة لأن عندنا أن بدله يتقدر بما دون دية الحر لعشرة دراهم. والطريق للمالكي في الجواب على هذا الاعتراض من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : التفسير : مثل أن يقول : أريد بقولي القيمة ؛ أن هذا بدل غير مقدر، بل تعتبر فيه صفات المبدل منه وهذا معنى القيمة . . الثاني : بيان موضع التسليم ؛ وذلك مثل أن يقول : أردت الذي يبلغ نصف الدية أو أقل من ذلك، فإنه مضمون بقيمة بالغة ما بلغت، وإنما يتقدر عندكم بدل من زادت قيمته على دية الحر. الثالث : الدلالة ؛ وذلك أن من ضمنت أطرافه بالقيمة، فنفسه مضمونة بالقيمة، فإذا ثبت ذلك صح الوصف واستمر القياس
A-2- Prevention of the attribute in the branch: That is like when a Maliki argues that killing a slave by mistake does not require expiation because it is an animal for which killing necessitates value, so expiation is not required for killing it, like beasts. So the Hanafi says as an objection: I do not concede that its killing necessitates value because according to us its compensation is estimated at less than the blood money of a free man by ten dirhams. And the way for the Maliki to answer this objection is from three aspects: First: Interpretation: such as saying: I mean by my saying 'value' that it is an unestimated compensation, rather the qualities of the compensated thing are considered, and this is the meaning of value. Second: Clarifying the point of concession: such as saying: I meant that which reaches half the blood money or less than that, for it is guaranteed with a value whatever it may reach, and according to you it is estimated as compensation for one whose value exceeds the blood money of a free man. Third: Indication: that whose limbs are guaranteed with value, then its life is guaranteed with value. So when that is established, the attribute is valid and the analogy continues.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage explains the first type of prevention of attribute (man' al-wasf): prevention in the branch (al-far'). The author gives an example from a dispute between a Maliki and a Hanafi regarding the ruling of killing a slave by mistake. The Maliki argues that since the slave is an animal (like cattle) for which killing requires payment of value (qima), expiation is not required. The Hanafi objects by denying that killing a slave requires payment of value, because according to the Hanafi school the compensation for a slave is less than the blood money of a free man. The author then presents three ways for the Maliki to respond: 1) Tafsir (interpretation): clarifying that by 'value' he means unestimated compensation that considers the qualities of the thing replaced. 2) Bayan mawdi' al-taslim (clarifying the point of concession): explaining that he meant a value that reaches half the blood money or less, and that such a value is indeed guaranteed. 3) Dalala (indication): using the principle that if the limbs of a person are guaranteed with value, then their life is also guaranteed with value. Once the attribute (value) is established, the analogy stands. The passage teaches the dialectical method of responding to an objection that denies the presence of the attribute in the branch.

Key Takeaway
The three response methods—tafsir, bayan mawdi' al-taslim, and dalala—provide a systematic way to counter an objection that denies the attribute in the branch, thereby preserving analogical reasoning in jadal.

Preventing the Attribute in Both Origin and Branch: The Tayammum Debate
Author explains the objection preventing attribute in both origin and branch with two examples and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection of preventing the attribute in both origin and branch and explain why it challenges analogical reasoning in jadal.

Core Idea
Denying the attribute in both cases forces the proponent to reinterpret or provide textual proof.

In jadal, an opponent may block an analogy by denying that the attribute (wasf) applies to both the origin and the branch. This is a powerful objection because it cuts off the analogy at its root. In this lesson, we examine the tayammum example to understand the structure of this objection and how a proponent can respond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogical argument
The Maliki argues: Tayammum is like washing the face (origin) because both are purifications (tahara) performed with earth in place of wudu. Therefore, tayammum must cover the hands to the elbows just as face washing covers the entire face.
This is a qiyas: origin (face washing), branch (tayammum), attribute (tahara).
2
Locate the objection
The Hanafi objects by denying the attribute 'tahara' in both the origin and the branch. He says: Tayammum is not tahara because tahara removes hadath, but tayammum does not. Thus the analogy fails because the attribute is absent.
The objector must reject the attribute in both cases to block the analogy completely.
3
Analyze the force of the objection
By denying the attribute in both, the objector prevents the analogy from even starting. The proponent cannot rely on the shared attribute if it is not admitted.
This is stronger than denying it in only one of the two.
4
Observe the proponent's potential responses
The author gives two responses: tafsir (reinterpret the attribute) and dalala (provide textual evidence that the attribute applies). In the tayammum case, the Maliki can reinterpret 'tahara' as 'making prayer permissible' or cite the hadith calling earth 'tahur'.
These responses aim to re-establish the attribute.

A-3- Prevention of the attribute in both origin and branch together. And its example: that the Maliki argues that tayammum is obligatory up to the elbows because every purification prescribed by clean earth in the place of wudu must encompass the place, like cleansing the face. So the Hanafi says: I do not concede the description that it is purification in either branch or origin, because purification is what cleans the place and removes ritual impurity, and we have agreed that tayammum does not remove ritual impurity, so it is not described as purification except metaphorically and broadly, and the cause requires precision. And the answer to it is from two aspects: First: Interpretation: that the proponent says: the intended meaning of our saying 'purification' is that prayer becomes permissible by it; there is no more meaning than that, and this is not prevented. Second: Indication: by what has been narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The earth was made for me a mosque and a purifier.' When the earth is called a purifier, then prevention from applying that to it is not valid. And whoever claims metaphor or deviation from the real meaning must provide evidence. And its example also: that the Hanafi says about the mutamatti' who does not fast during Hajj: that it is dropped because it is a substitute with a time, so it must drop with the passing of its time, like Friday prayer. So the objector says: I do not concede that Friday is a substitute, nor do I concede in the branch that it is time-bound. So the proponent needs to answer by clarifying his concession from his school or by providing evidence.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage explains the third type of prevention of attribute (according to the numbering in the text: A-3): prevention in both origin and branch together. The first example involves tayammum (dry ablution). The Maliki argues that tayammum must be performed up to the elbows because it is a purification (tahara) prescribed by clean earth, and just as washing the face must cover the entire face, tayammum must cover the hands to the elbows. The Hanafi objects by denying that tayammum is a purification in either the origin (tahara) or the branch (tayammum) because purification is defined as cleansing and removing ritual impurity, while tayammum does not remove impurity. Thus, calling it purification is metaphorical. The author then gives two responses: 1) Tafsir: reinterpret purification to mean merely that prayer becomes permissible, not that it removes impurity. 2) Dalala: citing the hadith where the earth is called 'tahur' (purifier) by the Prophet, proving that calling tayammum a purification is legitimate. The second example involves the Hanafi arguing that the compensation fast for tamattu' drops if not done in time, analogous to Friday prayer dropping after its time. The objector denies that Friday prayer is a substitute (badal) and that the branch (the fast) is time-bound. The proponent must then either clarify from his own school's position or provide evidence. This passage illustrates the dialectical technique of addressing objections that deny the attribute in both cases.

Responding to Attribute Prevention: Tafsir and Dalala
Author explains the objection preventing attribute in both origin and branch with two examples and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two response strategies—tafsir (reinterpretation) and dalala (textual evidence)—to counter a prevention-of-attribute objection in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
A strong objection can be answered by redefining terms or citing revealed evidence.

When an opponent prevents the attribute in both origin and branch, the proponent is not defenseless. The tradition provides two powerful responses: tafsir (reinterpreting the attribute to a meaning the opponent must accept) and dalala (providing a textual proof from Qur'an or Hadith that directly applies the attribute). In this lesson, we study these responses using the tayammum and tamattu' examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two response types
Tafsir: The proponent redefines the contested attribute in a way that the opponent cannot deny, often by clarifying that the attribute has a broader or different meaning in this context. Dalala: The proponent cites a revealed text (Qur'an or Hadith) that uses the attribute, proving its applicability.
Tafsir is a conceptual redefinition; dalala is an appeal to authority.
2
Apply tafsir to the tayammum example
The Maliki redefines 'tahara' as 'making prayer permissible' rather than 'removing hadath'. Since the Hanafi accepts that tayammum makes prayer permissible, he cannot deny the attribute under this definition.
This shifts the meaning to a point of common agreement.
3
Apply dalala to the tayammum example
The Maliki cites the hadith: 'The earth was made for me a mosque and a purifier (tahur).' Since the Prophet called the earth 'tahur', the attribute of purification is established even if tayammum does not remove hadath.
The text overrides the technical definition.
4
Examine the second example (tamattu' fast)
The Hanafi argues that the compensation fast for tamattu' drops if not performed in time, analogizing to Friday prayer. The objector prevents the attribute 'substitute with a time' in both origin and branch. The proponent must respond by either showing that Friday prayer is indeed a substitute (tawqit) or proving the branch is time-bound via dalala.
This example tests the proponent's ability to provide evidence from his own school or a text.

Preventing the Attribute on the Proponent's Origin: Objection and Responses
Author explains the objection preventing attribute on the proponent's origin with Shafi'i example and three responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection of preventing the attribute on the proponent's origin (man' al-wasf 'ala asl al-mustadill) and apply the three standard responses to defend an analogical argument in jadal.

Core Idea
When the opponent denies the key attribute in your own origin, shift ground, reinterpret, or prove it.

In dialectical debates (jadal), a common countermove is to deny that the attribute you rely on exists in the origin you yourself used. This is called 'prevention of the attribute on the origin of the interlocutor.' Without a proper response, your analogy collapses. This lesson examines a classical example from usul al-fiqh and the three standard rejoinders: clarifying a point of concession, reinterpreting the attribute to a conceded meaning, and providing direct evidence. Master these to keep your qiyas standing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy and the attribute
Locate the qiyas in the passage: the Shafi'i argues that order (tartib) is required in wudu by analogy with prayer (salat), because both are acts of worship (ibada) invalidated by sleep. The key attribute is 'ibada (worship).
The analogy: wudu is like prayer in being an 'ibada that is invalidated by sleep, so both require tartib.
2
Recognize the opponent's objection
The opponent says 'la asallam anna al-wudu 'ibada' — I do not concede that wudu is an act of worship. This prevents the attribute on the proponent's own origin (the origin is wudu).
The objection targets the existence of the attribute in the origin, not the analogy itself.
3
Analyze the first response: bayan mawdi' al-taslim
The proponent shifts the origin: he explains tayammum, not wudu. There is consensus that tayammum is worship, so the opponent must concede. The attribute is preserved in a different origin.
This works if the proponent can genuinely reroute the analogy to an uncontested origin.
4
Analyze the second response: al-tafsir
The proponent reinterprets 'ibada as 'obedience to Allah' (ta'a). This broader meaning is uncontroversial and includes wudu. The opponent concedes this interpretation.
Reinterpretation must be plausible and within the accepted linguistic scope.
5
Analyze the third response: al-dalala
The proponent provides direct textual evidence: the hadith 'wudu is half of faith.' This proves that wudu is an act of worship, overriding the opponent's denial.
Evidence from revelation takes precedence over mere denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - منع الوصف على أصل المستدل : من ذلك أن يقول الشافعي في إيجاب الترتيب في الطهارة : إنها عبادة يبطلها النوم، فوجب فيها الترتيب كالصلاة. فيقول المخالف : لا أسلم أن الوضوء عبادة. والجواب عنه من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : بيان موضع التسليم ؛ وهو أن يقول : أعلل التيمم، ولا خلاف أن ذلك عبادة . الثاني : التفسير بما يسلم وهو أن يقول : أريد به أنه طاعة لله عز وجل، فهذا مسلم . الثالث : الدلالة ؛ وذلك بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : «الوضوء شطر الإيمان (4) ، فدل على أنه عبادة
B- Prevention of the attribute on the origin of the interlocutor: Such as when the Shafi'i says regarding making order (tartib) obligatory in purification: it is an act of worship that is invalidated by sleep, so order is obligatory in it like prayer. So the opponent says: I do not concede that wudu is an act of worship. And the answer to it is from three aspects: First: Clarifying the point of concession: that he says: I am explaining tayammum, and there is no dispute that that is an act of worship. Second: Interpretation with what is conceded: that he says: I intend by it obedience to Allah Almighty, and this is conceded. Third: Indication: by what has been narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Wudu is half of faith,' so it indicates that it is an act of worship.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage explains the second type (B) of prevention of attribute: prevention on the origin of the interlocutor (mustadill). That is, the opponent denies that the attribute exists in the origin that the proponent himself used. The example is a Shafi'i arguing that order (tartib) is required in wudu because wudu is an act of worship (ibada) that is invalidated by sleep, analogous to prayer. The opponent objects by saying that wudu is not an act of worship. The three responses: 1) Bayan mawdi' al-taslim: the proponent clarifies that he is explaining tayammum (not wudu) and there is consensus that tayammum is worship. 2) Tafsir: reinterpret 'ibada' as obedience to Allah, which is conceded. 3) Dalala: cite the hadith 'wudu is half of faith' to prove it is worship. This passage shows how to defend the attribute when it is denied in the proponent's own origin.

Key Takeaway

Understanding Man' al-Wasf and Its Three Responses
Author defines man' al-wasf and lists three methods of responding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify man' al-wasf in a jadal exchange and recognize the three methods of responding to it.

Core Idea
Man' al-wasf is a targeted objection, not a blanket denial—it must be answered systematically.

In jadal (dialectical debate), an opponent may prevent the attribute (wasf) that the interlocutor uses in analogical reasoning. This lesson examines the first example from our passage: the Shafi'i's argument that wudu requires order because it is an act of worship, and the Hanafi's objection that wudu is not an act of worship. We will learn the three classical methods to counter such a prevention, making the debate structured and principled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the wasf
Find the original analogical argument: the Shafi'i claims wudu is like prayer because both are acts of worship (ibada) invalidated by sleep. The wasf here is 'being an act of worship'.
The claim is that order is obligatory due to this attribute.
2
Identify the objection (man' al-wasf)
The Hanafi says 'لا أسلم أن الوضوء عبادة' (I do not accept that wudu is an act of worship). This is a prevention of the description—he denies that wudu has the attribute claimed by the Shafi'i.
The objection is based on the opponent's own principles, not a general rejection.
3
Categorize the response methods
The author lists three methods: 1) Clarify acceptance elsewhere (e.g., tayammum is undisputedly an act of worship), 2) Reinterpret the term to something acceptable (e.g., obedience to Allah), 3) Provide textual evidence (e.g., hadith 'wudu is half of faith').
Each method directly addresses the prevention without changing the original claim's logical structure.
4
Apply to the example
Work through the three methods as they apply to the wudu case: first method points to tayammum, second method redefines ibada as obedience, third method cites the prophetic hadith. Check which one(s) best counter the objection.
The first method is strongest if the opponent agrees on tayammum; the second works if the opponent accepts the broader meaning; the third provides independent proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - منع الوصف على أصل المستدل :
من ذلك أن يقول الشافعي في إيجاب الترتيب في الطهارة : إنها عبادة يبطلها النوم، فوجب فيها الترتيب كالصلاة.
فيقول المخالف : لا أسلم أن الوضوء عبادة.
والجواب عنه من ثلاثة أوجه :
الأول : بيان موضع التسليم ; وهو أن يقول : أعلل التيمم، ولا خلاف أن ذلك عبادة .
الثاني : التفسير بما يسلم وهو أن يقول : أريد به أنه طاعة لله عز وجل، فهذا مسلم .
الثالث : الدلالة ; وذلك بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : «الوضوء شطر الإيمان» فدل على أنه عبادة .
ومن ذلك : قول أصحاب أبي حنيفة : إن اللعان فرقة تختص بالقول، فوجب أن لا يتأبد تحريمها، كالطلاق.
فيقول الشافعي عندي أن الطلاق لا يختص بالقول، فإنه يقع بالكناية مع النية، وليست قولا فإن قال المستدل : الكناية قائمة مقام القول ونائبة عنه، قيل له : لا يمنع ذلك صحة الممانعة لأن الكناية ليست بقول وإن نابت عنه
B - Prevention of the description according to the origin of the interlocutor: Among that is the Shafi'i saying in obligating order in purification: It is an act of worship that sleep invalidates, so order is obligatory in it like prayer. The opponent says: I do not accept that ablution is an act of worship. The answer to it is from three aspects: First: Clarifying the point of acceptance; that he says: I intend by it tayammum, and there is no dispute that that is an act of worship. Second: Interpretation in what is accepted, that he says: I mean by it obedience to Allah Almighty, this is accepted. Third: Evidence; that by what is narrated from the Prophet (PBUH) that he said: 'Ablution is half of faith', so it indicates that it is an act of worship. And among that is the statement of the followers of Abu Hanifa: The imprecation oath is a separation specific to speech, so its prohibition should not become eternal, like divorce. The Shafi'i says: In my view, divorce is not specific to speech, for it occurs by indirect expression with intention, and it is not speech. If the interlocutor says: The indirect expression stands in place of speech and represents it, it is said to him: This does not prevent the validity of the objection because the indirect expression is not speech even if it represents it.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of objection in jadal known as 'man' al-wasf' (prevention of the description/attribute) according to the madhhab of the interlocutor. The author gives two examples from fiqh to illustrate the concept and then enumerates three methods of responding to such an objection. The passage begins by stating the type: the objector denies that the attribute (wasf) used in the analogical reasoning is valid according to the original speaker's own school. The first example: the Shafi'i argues that order (tarteeb) is obligatory in wudu because wudu is an act of worship (ibada) invalidated by sleep, analogous to prayer. The Hanafi opponent responds: 'I do not accept that wudu is an act of worship.' This is a prevention of the description: the opponent does not accept that the attribute 'ibada' applies to wudu. The answer comes in three ways: First, clarifying the point of acceptance by showing that the attribute is accepted in another context (e.g., tayammum is undisputedly an act of worship). Second, interpreting the term in a way that is acceptable (e.g., meaning obedience to Allah, which all accept). Third, providing textual evidence (dalil) such as the hadith 'Ablution is half of faith'. The second example: the Hanafis argue that li'an (imprecation oath) is a separation specific to speech, so it should not make prohibition eternal, like divorce. The Shafi'i objects that divorce is not specific to speech because it can occur through kinaya (indirect expression) with intention, which is not speech. If the Hanafi argues that kinaya stands in place of speech, the response is that it is still not speech itself, so the objection holds. The three methods of response provide a systematic way to counter a prevention of the attribute: show acceptance elsewhere, reinterpret, or prove by textual evidence.

Key Terms
منع الوصف
أسلم
عبادة
كناية

B) Clarifying acceptance by referring to tayammum.

Key Takeaway
Man' al-wasf is answered by clarifying acceptance elsewhere, reinterpreting the term, or providing direct evidence.

Applying the Three Methods: The Li'an Example
Author defines man' al-wasf and lists three methods of responding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three methods of responding to man' al-wasf to new cases in Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
The three methods are reusable tools for any man' al-wasf objection, not tied to a single example.

The second example in our passage involves a different school conflict: Hanafis argue that the imprecation oath (li'an) is a separation specific to speech, so its prohibition should not be eternal—like divorce. The Shafi'i objects by preventing the attribute 'specific to speech' for divorce. We will analyze this exchange and see how the three response methods could be applied, even though the author uses a different rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the wasf
The Hanafis claim: li'an is a separation (furqa) that is specific to speech (takhtaṣṣ bi al-qawl), so its prohibition does not become eternal, analogous to divorce. The wasf is 'specific to speech'.
The analogy is between li'an and divorce.
2
Identify the objection (man' al-wasf)
The Shafi'i objects that divorce is not specific to speech because it occurs by kinaya (indirect expression) with intention, and kinaya is not speech. He thus prevents the attribute from applying to the analogical base (divorce).
This is a man' al-wasf because the attribute is denied of the original case (divorce).
3
Check if the three methods are used
The author does not explicitly use the three methods here but gives a direct refutation: kinaya is not speech even if it stands in place. However, we can imagine how the methods could be applied: method 1 might point to another case where kinaya is accepted as speech; method 2 might interpret 'speech' broadly; method 3 might cite a text where divorce occurs without explicit speech.
This exercise shows that the methods are flexible and may be adapted.
4
Evaluate the strength of the Shafi'i's response
The Shafi'i's response is a direct denial of the attribute's application. It is effective because it attacks the core of the analogy. Compare this to the three methods: which one does it most resemble? It resembles method 1 (clarifying acceptance) but in reverse: it shows that the attribute does not hold for the original case.
Understanding this helps you see the spectrum of responses to man' al-wasf.

ب - منع الوصف على أصل المستدل :
من ذلك أن يقول الشافعي في إيجاب الترتيب في الطهارة : إنها عبادة يبطلها النوم، فوجب فيها الترتيب كالصلاة.
فيقول المخالف : لا أسلم أن الوضوء عبادة.
والجواب عنه من ثلاثة أوجه :
الأول : بيان موضع التسليم ; وهو أن يقول : أعلل التيمم، ولا خلاف أن ذلك عبادة .
الثاني : التفسير بما يسلم وهو أن يقول : أريد به أنه طاعة لله عز وجل، فهذا مسلم .
الثالث : الدلالة ; وذلك بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : «الوضوء شطر الإيمان» فدل على أنه عبادة .
ومن ذلك : قول أصحاب أبي حنيفة : إن اللعان فرقة تختص بالقول، فوجب أن لا يتأبد تحريمها، كالطلاق.
فيقول الشافعي عندي أن الطلاق لا يختص بالقول، فإنه يقع بالكناية مع النية، وليست قولا فإن قال المستدل : الكناية قائمة مقام القول ونائبة عنه، قيل له : لا يمنع ذلك صحة الممانعة لأن الكناية ليست بقول وإن نابت عنه
B - Prevention of the description according to the origin of the interlocutor: Among that is the Shafi'i saying in obligating order in purification: It is an act of worship that sleep invalidates, so order is obligatory in it like prayer. The opponent says: I do not accept that ablution is an act of worship. The answer to it is from three aspects: First: Clarifying the point of acceptance; that he says: I intend by it tayammum, and there is no dispute that that is an act of worship. Second: Interpretation in what is accepted, that he says: I mean by it obedience to Allah Almighty, this is accepted. Third: Evidence; that by what is narrated from the Prophet (PBUH) that he said: 'Ablution is half of faith', so it indicates that it is an act of worship. And among that is the statement of the followers of Abu Hanifa: The imprecation oath is a separation specific to speech, so its prohibition should not become eternal, like divorce. The Shafi'i says: In my view, divorce is not specific to speech, for it occurs by indirect expression with intention, and it is not speech. If the interlocutor says: The indirect expression stands in place of speech and represents it, it is said to him: This does not prevent the validity of the objection because the indirect expression is not speech even if it represents it.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of objection in jadal known as 'man' al-wasf' (prevention of the description/attribute) according to the madhhab of the interlocutor. The author gives two examples from fiqh to illustrate the concept and then enumerates three methods of responding to such an objection. The passage begins by stating the type: the objector denies that the attribute (wasf) used in the analogical reasoning is valid according to the original speaker's own school. The first example: the Shafi'i argues that order (tarteeb) is obligatory in wudu because wudu is an act of worship (ibada) invalidated by sleep, analogous to prayer. The Hanafi opponent responds: 'I do not accept that wudu is an act of worship.' This is a prevention of the description: the opponent does not accept that the attribute 'ibada' applies to wudu. The answer comes in three ways: First, clarifying the point of acceptance by showing that the attribute is accepted in another context (e.g., tayammum is undisputedly an act of worship). Second, interpreting the term in a way that is acceptable (e.g., meaning obedience to Allah, which all accept). Third, providing textual evidence (dalil) such as the hadith 'Ablution is half of faith'. The second example: the Hanafis argue that li'an (imprecation oath) is a separation specific to speech, so it should not make prohibition eternal, like divorce. The Shafi'i objects that divorce is not specific to speech because it can occur through kinaya (indirect expression) with intention, which is not speech. If the Hanafi argues that kinaya stands in place of speech, the response is that it is still not speech itself, so the objection holds. The three methods of response provide a systematic way to counter a prevention of the attribute: show acceptance elsewhere, reinterpret, or prove by textual evidence.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Hanafi Claim on Li'an and Shafi'i Response: A Dialectical Exchange
A dialectical exchange between Hanafis and Shafi'i on whether divorce is specific to explicit speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a jadal objection (mumana'a) and analyze a legal analogy by locating the contested premise.

Core Idea
In jadal, even a weakened counter-argument can be valid if it challenges the premise.

This passage presents a classic jadal exchange between Hanafi and Shafi'i jurists. The Hanafis argue that li'an (mutual cursing) should not cause perpetual prohibition because it is like divorce, which is specific to speech. Al-Shafi'i responds by challenging the premise that divorce is specific to speech, using the concept of kinaya (indirect speech) with intention. This lesson will walk you through the dialectical moves and show how objections (mumana'a) work in Islamic legal debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim
The Hanafis argue that li'an is a separation specific to speech, so its prohibition should not be perpetual, just like divorce. Locate this claim in the Arabic.
The claim is an analogy: li'an is like divorce in being specific to speech.
2
Identify the premise being challenged
Al-Shafi'i attacks the premise that divorce is specific to speech. Note that this is a structural move: if the premise is false, the analogy collapses.
The contested premise is that divorce (talaq) is specific to explicit speech (qawl).
3
Understand the objection (mumana'a)
Al-Shafi'i says divorce can occur through kinaya (allusion) with intention, which is not explicit speech. This is a valid objection even if kinaya represents speech, because representation does not make it actual speech.
The objection is a mumana'a: it denies the universality of the opponent's premise.
4
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
The Hanafi attempts to salvage by saying kinaya stands in for speech, but the Shafi'i counters that this does not invalidate the objection. The debate remains unresolved in this passage, illustrating how objections work.
The outcome is a standoff: the objection stands, but the analogy is weakened.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : قول أصحاب أبي حنيفة : إن اللعان فرقة تختص بالقول، فوجب أن لا يتأبد تحريمها، كالطلاق. : فيقول الشافعي عندي أن الطلاق لا يختص بالقول، فإنه يقع بالكناية مع النية، وليست قولا فإن قال المستدل : الكناية قائمة مقام القول ونائبة عنه، قيل له : لا يمنع ذلك صحة الممانعة لأن الكناية ليست بقول وإن نابت عنه
Among that is the statement of the followers of Abu Hanifa: 'Indeed li'an (mutual cursing) is a separation specific to speech, so it is necessary that its prohibition does not become perpetual, like divorce.' So al-Shafi'i says: In my view, divorce is not specific to speech, for it occurs by indirect speech (kinaya) with intention, and it is not a statement. If the arguer says: 'Indirect speech stands in the place of speech and represents it,' it is said to him: 'That does not prevent the validity of the objection because indirect speech is not a statement even though it represents it.'
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange (jadal) between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i. The Hanafis argue that li'an is a separation that is specific to explicit speech (qawl), so it should not cause perpetual prohibition, analogizing it to divorce (talaq). Al-Shafi'i counters that divorce itself is not specific to explicit speech; it can occur through indirect speech (kinaya) with intention. Kinaya is not considered a statement in the strict sense. When the Hanafi argues that kinaya stands in for speech, the Shafi'i responds that this does not negate the validity of the objection because representation does not make kinaya actual speech. The exchange illustrates how legal rulings are debated by examining the nature of acts and their linguistic expression.

الطلاق
الكناية
النية
الممانعة

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a valid objection (mumana'a) targets the premise of an analogy, even if the opponent tries to defend it by claiming representation.
